Doubling of Natural Powers in the Stomach and Liver
Describes the doubling of the four powers in stomach and liver, with quotes from earlier works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four natural powers can be doubled in organs like the stomach and liver, serving distinct functions for the organ itself and for the whole body.

Core Idea
The same four powers can operate in duplicate for different purposes.

Al-Jurjani continues his notes on natural powers, now focusing on a fascinating phenomenon: in certain organs, the four powers—attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive—are doubled. The stomach and liver exemplify this: one set prepares nourishment for the entire body, while another set works exclusively for the organ's own sustenance. This passage, drawing from earlier philosophical works, explores this duplication and ends with al-Razi's extension to other digestive organs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stomach's dual roles
Read the opening lines: the stomach acts both to prepare food for other organs and for its own nourishment. Notice that this sets up the need for doubled powers.
The stomach is not just a one-purpose organ; it serves itself and the whole body.
2
Understand the two sets of four powers
The passage from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya describes two complete sets of the four powers in the stomach: one for the body's nourishment (attracting, retaining, changing into blood, pushing to liver) and one for the stomach itself (attracting its own food, retaining, changing into its substance, expelling wastes).
Each set has all four powers: جاذبة, ماسكة, هاضمة, دافعة.
3
Notice the distinction in changes
The change into blood is different from the change into the organ's substance. This justifies why separate digestive powers are needed.
The stomach changes food into chyle; the liver changes chyle into blood. Each requires its own digestive power.
4
Follow al-Razi's extension
Al-Razi argues that if this doubling occurs in stomach and liver, it must apply to all digestive organs: mouth, tongue, esophagus, intestines, and mesenteric veins.
This shows the principle is general, not limited to two organs.
5
Interpret the closing commentary
Al-Jurjani clarifies that the phrase 'one of them' refers to one of the two groups (sets of powers), each with its four parts, which were understood from context even if not explicitly stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه ( الثاني : قد تتضاعف هذه القوى في بعض الأعضاء فالمعدة فيها جاذبة إليها ما يصلح لها وجاذبة ) أيضاً (لغذاء البدن من خارج، وبالجملة فقد تفعل) المعدة (تارة (للإعداد وتهيئة الغذاء لسائر الأعضاء ) وتارة للاغتذاء وكذا كثير من الأعضاء كالكبد وسائر أدوات الغذاء. وفي المباحث المشرقية: قال بعض الحكماء : إن هذه القوى الأربع توجد في المعدة مضاعفة إحداهما التي تجذب غذاء البدن من خارج إلى تجويف المعدة والتي تمسكه هناك والتي تغيره إلى ما يصلح أن يكون دما والتي تدفعه إلى الكبد والثانية التي تجذب إلى المعدة غذاءها على الخصوص وتمسكه هناك وتغيره إلى جوهرها وتدفع الفضلات عنها، وكذا الحال في الكبد لأن التغيير إلى الدم غير التغيير إلى جوهر الكبد كما أن التغيير إلى العصارة غير التغيير إلى جوهر المعدة، وهذه الثانية موجودة بأجزائها الأربعة في جميع البدن على اختلاف جواهرها وأما في المعدة والكبد فيوجد معها أيضا الأولى بأجزائها الأربعة، ثم قال : قال الإمام الرازي : إن كان هذا حقاً وجب أن يحكم به في الفم واللسان والمري والأمعاء والعروق المسماة بماساريقا بالجملة في جميع أعضاء الغذاء.

قوله : (إحداهما ) أي إحدى الجملتين وهما وإن لم تكونا مذكورتين صريحاً لكنهما كانتا مفهومتين من وقوع قوله : مضاعفة حالا مقيدة لما قبلها ولكل واحدة من الجملتين أجزاء أربعة كما ذكره .
The second note: These powers may be doubled in some organs. The stomach has an attractive [power] attracting to it what is suitable for it, and also an attractive [power] for the nourishment of the body from outside. In summary, the stomach sometimes acts for the preparation and preparation of food for the other organs, and sometimes for its own nourishment. Likewise many organs such as the liver and the rest of the instruments of food. And in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: Some philosophers said: These four powers exist in the stomach doubled: one of them is that which attracts the nourishment of the body from outside into the cavity of the stomach, and that which retains it there, and that which changes it into what is suitable to be blood, and that which pushes it to the liver; and the second is that which attracts to the stomach its own food specifically, and retains it there, and changes it into its substance, and pushes the wastes away from it. And likewise the situation in the liver, because the change into blood is different from the change into the substance of the liver, just as the change into chyle is different from the change into the substance of the stomach. And this second [set] exists with its four parts in all the body according to the diversity of their substances. As for the stomach and liver, the first [set] also exists with its four parts along with it. Then he said: Imam al-Razi said: If this is true, then it must be applied to the mouth, tongue, esophagus, intestines, and the veins called mesentery, in short, in all the organs of nourishment. His statement: 'one of them' – meaning one of the two groups, and although they are not explicitly mentioned, they are understood from the occurrence of his statement 'doubled' as a circumstantial qualifier restricting what precedes, and each of the two groups has four parts as he mentioned.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a second note (tanbih) explaining that the four natural powers (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive) can be doubled in certain organs, specifically the stomach and liver. It begins by noting that the stomach has a power that attracts food suitable for itself and another that attracts nourishment for the whole body from outside. The stomach sometimes prepares food for other organs and sometimes feeds itself. Similarly, the liver and other digestive organs can have this duplication. The passage then quotes from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, where a philosopher explains that in the stomach, the four powers exist in a doubled form: one set that attracts external nourishment into the stomach cavity, retains it, converts it into blood, and pushes it to the liver; and a second set that attracts food specifically for the stomach itself, retains it, changes it into the stomach's own substance, and expels wastes. The same applies to the liver, because the transformation into blood is different from the transformation into the liver's substance. The second set (for self-nourishment) exists with its four parts throughout the entire body, varying by substance, but in the stomach and liver, the first set (for general nourishment) also coexists. Al-Razi is then quoted, asserting that if this is true, it should also apply to the mouth, tongue, esophagus, intestines, and mesenteric vessels. The final commentary explains that the phrase 'one of them' refers to one of the two groups (the two sets of powers), and each group has four parts.

Correction: This passage shows that in organs like the stomach and liver, the powers are doubled, with one set for the organ's own nourishment and one for the body's.

?

?

What one change will you make this week to better nourish your own heart and body so you can fulfill your responsibilities to those around you?

The Animal Soul and Its Two Powers: Perceptive and Motive
Al-Jurjani classifies the animal soul's powers into perceptive and motive, then subdivides perceptive into external and internal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the animal soul is distinguished from plants by its perceptive and motive powers, and how these are classified into external and internal perceptive powers.

Core Idea
Animals are set apart by perception and movement — the soul's two active powers.

In this passage, al-Jurjani lays out the fundamental classification of the animal soul's powers. He explains that animals share natural powers with plants but are unique in having perceptive and motive faculties. Perception itself is then divided into external and internal, with the five senses as the primary external powers. This classification underpins all later discussions in philosophical psychology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two major powers of the animal soul
Read the Arabic passage and note that the animal soul has two kinds of powers: perceptive (mudrika) and motive (muharrika). These are what distinguish animals from plants.
2
Understand the priority given to perceptive powers
Notice that the author places perceptive before motive because movement depends on will, which requires perception. Also external perception is placed before internal because it is more apparent.
3
List the five external senses
The passage states that the external perceptive powers are the five senses, beginning with sight. Think about why sight might be given special attention.
This leads into the detailed discussion of vision in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثالث : القسم الثاني : النفس الحيوانية
:
[ القسم الثاني : النفس الحيوانية ]
( في النفس الحيوانية وتسمى (قواها) التي لا توجد في النبات (نفسانية وهي إما مدركة وإما محركة ) لأن امتياز الحيوان عن مشاركاته في القوى الطبيعية بهاتين القوتين ) والمدركة إما ظاهرة وإما باطنة ) فهذه أنواع ثلاثة : ( النوع الأول : القوى المدركة الظاهرة ) قدم المدركة على المحركة لأن تحريكها إنما هو بالإرادة المتوقفة على الإدراك، وقدم الظاهرة على الباطنة لظهورها ( وهي المشاعر) أي الحواس (الخمس الأول البصر وللحكماء فيه أي في الإبصار ( قولان) بل أقول ثلاثة مشهورة إلا أن الثالث قريب من الثاني فذكره المصنف في قرنه وعدهما قولاً واحداً. (الأول: وهو مذهب أرسطو) وأتباعه من الطبيعيين ) أنه إنما يحصل الإبصار ( بانعكاس صورة المرئي بتوسط الهواء المشف) الذي لا لون له فلا يستر ما وراءه إلى الرطوبة الجليدية التي في العين ( وانطباعها في جزء منها) أي من تلك الجليدية ) وذلك الجزء ) الذي تنطبع فيه الصورة ( زاوية ) رأس ( مخروط) متوهم لا وجود له أصلاً، قاعدته سطح المرئي) ورأسه عند الباصرة ( ولذلك ) أي ولأن ( الإبصار بالانطباع على الوجه المذكور دون خروج الشعاع ) يرى القريب أعظم ) من البعيد مع تساويهما في المقدار بحسب نفس الأمر بل مع اتحاد المرئي في حالتي القرب والبعد وذلك ) لأن الوتر الواحد الذي هو امتداد سطح المرئي ( كلما قرب ) النقطة التي خرج منها إليه خطان مستقيمان محيطان بزاوية (كان أقصر ساقاً فأوتر) عند تلك النقطة ( زواية أعظم ، وكلما بعد ) عنها (كان أطول ساقاً فأوتر) عندها ( زواية أصغر كما تشهد به الفطرة السلمية ) والنفس إنما تدرك الصغر والكبر) في المرئي ) وباعتبار تلك الزواية فإنها إذا كانت صغيرة كان الجزء الواقع من الجليدية فيها صغيراً فترتسم صورة المرئي فيه فيرى صغيراً، وإذا كانت كبيرة كان الجزء الواقع فيها كبيراً فترتسم صورته فيه فيرى كبيراً. ومن المعلوم أن هذا إنما يستقيم إذا جعلت الزاوية موضعاً للإبصار كما ذهبنا إليه وأما إذا جعل موضع الإبصار من

قوله : ( في القوى الطبيعية وهي القوى التي كانت للنفس النباتية على ما مر في صدر القسم الأول، وقد عرفت أن سائر الحيوانات مشاركة للنباتات في القوى الطبيعية. وقوله : قريب من الثاني على ما سيجيء في هذه الورقة إن شاء الله تعالى. وقوله : فذكره المصنف في أقرنه أي شده في حبل واحد .
The first observatory - the third chapter - the second section: the animal soul. [The second section: the animal soul] Concerning the animal soul and its powers that are not found in plants are called psychic, and they are either perceptive or motive, because the animal's distinction from its partners in the natural powers is by these two powers. And the perceptive is either external or internal. So these are three types: (the first type: the external perceptive powers). He prioritized the perceptive over the motive because its movement is only by will which depends on perception, and he prioritized the external over the internal due to its apparentness. And they are the senses, i.e., the five senses. The first is sight. And for the philosophers concerning it, i.e., concerning vision, there are two opinions – rather I say three famous ones, except that the third is close to the second, so the author mentioned them in his neck and counted them as one opinion. (The first: which is the doctrine of Aristotle and his followers among the naturalists) that vision occurs only by the reflection of the image of the visible object through the transparent air, which has no color and does not conceal what is behind it, onto the icy moisture that is in the eye, and its impression on a part of it, i.e., of that icy [moisture]. And that part in which the image is impressed is an angle, the apex of a cone, imaginary, having no existence at all, whose base is the surface of the visible object and its apex is at the seer. And therefore, i.e., because vision is by impression in the aforementioned manner without the emission of a ray, the near is seen larger than the distant even if they are equal in magnitude in reality, nay even with the identity of the visible object in the states of nearness and distance. And that is because the single chord which is the extension of the surface of the visible object – whenever it approaches the point from which two straight lines enclosing an angle go out to it, it becomes shorter in leg, so it subtends at that point a greater angle, and whenever it is distant from it, it becomes longer in leg, so it subtends at it a smaller angle, as the sound innate disposition testifies. And the soul only perceives smallness and bigness in the visible object by consideration of that angle, for when it is small, the part occurring from the icy [moisture] in it is small, so the image of the visible object is imprinted in it and it is seen small; and when it is large, the part occurring in it is large, so its image is imprinted in it and it is seen large. And it is known that this is valid only if the angle is made the place of vision as we have held. But if the place of vision is made from – His statement: 'concerning the natural powers' – they are the powers that belonged to the plant soul as mentioned at the beginning of the first section, and you have known that other animals share with plants in the natural powers. His statement: 'close to the second' – as will come in this page, God willing. His statement: 'so the author mentioned them in his neck' – i.e., he tied them in one rope.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage introduces the second section on the animal soul (al-nafs al-hayawaniyya). It classifies the powers specific to animals (not found in plants) into two main categories: perceptive (mudrika) and motive (muharrika), since animals are distinguished from plants by these two. The perceptive powers are further divided into external (zāhira) and internal (bātina), thus yielding three types. The author prioritizes the perceptive over the motive because movement depends on will, which requires perception; and external perception is prioritized over internal due to its obviousness. The first type discussed is the external perceptive powers, i.e., the five senses, beginning with sight (baṣar). The passage notes that philosophers have two (or three) opinions on vision, but the third is close to the second, so the author (al-Iji) grouped them together. The first opinion is the Aristotelian theory: vision occurs by the reflection of the image of the visible object through transparent air (which has no color) onto the icy humor (al-rutūba al-jālidiyya) in the eye, and its impression on a part of that humor. That part is an angle at the apex of an imaginary cone whose base is the surface of the object and apex at the seer. Because vision is by impression and not by rays, a near object appears larger than a distant one even if they are equal in size. This is explained geometrically: the same chord (extent of the object) subtends a larger angle when closer, resulting in a larger impression on the icy humor, so the soul perceives it as larger. The passage concludes that this explanation works only if one holds that the angle is the place of vision (as the author does). The commentary then explains that 'natural powers' refers to the plant soul powers mentioned earlier, that 'close to the second' will be explained later, and that 'mentioned them in his neck' means he tied them together as one opinion.

The Aristotelian Theory of Vision: Impression and the Cone Model
Al-Jurjani presents the Aristotelian theory of vision by reflection and impression, with a geometric cone model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Aristotelian account of vision, including the role of transparent air, the icy humor, and the geometric explanation of size perception.

Core Idea
We see by impression of the object's image onto the eye, not by rays from the eye.

After classifying the senses, al-Jurjani delves into the first major theory of vision, attributed to Aristotle. This theory explains sight as the result of the reflection of the visible object's image via transparent air onto the icy humor of the eye. He uses a geometric model involving an imaginary cone to explain why objects appear larger when closer. This was a key point of debate among philosophers and theologians.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic mechanism of vision
Read the passage and note that vision occurs by 'reflection of the image of the visible object through transparent air' and its 'impression' on the icy humor in the eye. There is no ray emitted from the eye.
This is the core of the Aristotelian theory.
2
Visualize the imaginary cone
The passage describes an imaginary cone with its base at the object and apex at the seer (the 'bāṣirah'). The part of the icy humor that receives the image is an angle at the apex.
This geometric model explains visual angle.
3
Apply the model to size perception
The passage explains that the same object subtends a larger angle when closer, so it appears larger. This is because the chord (the object's extent) is closer to the apex, making the angle greater.
The soul judges size based on the angle.

Two Arguments for Impression (Intiba')
Al-Ījī presents two arguments for intiba' (impression).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key arguments used by proponents of intiba' (impression) theory of vision: the size variation argument and the afterimage argument.

Core Idea
Size variation and afterimage both support the impression theory of vision.

Al-Ījī presents two rational arguments to support the impression theory of vision against the emission theory. The first involves the apparent change in size of an object with distance, and the second uses the phenomenon of afterimage to show that the form of the object is literally imprinted in the eye. These arguments are foundational for understanding the debate between the two theories of vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow from the cone rule to the afterimage argument.
The passage begins with a discussion of the cone rule from emission theory.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument is based on the cone rule and size variation: if vision were by emission, the cone rule would predict no size change, but impression theory allows it. Locate this reasoning.
3
Identify the second argument
The second argument is the afterimage experiment: looking at the sun or a green meadow leaves an afterimage when eyes are closed, proving the form is impressed in the eye.
Notice the examples: sun and green meadow.
4
Reflect on the impact
Consider how these arguments challenge the emission theory. Why is the afterimage particularly strong evidence for impression?
The afterimage shows persistence, which emission theory cannot easily explain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة المخروط كما يقتضيه القول بخروج الشعاع، فيجب أن يرى الجسم كما هو سواء خرجت الخطوط الشعاعية من زاوية ضيقة أو غير ضيقة، هكذا قالوا وفيه بحث لأن الإبصار ليس حاصلاً بمجرد القاعدة بل لرأس المخروط فيه مدخل أيضاً فجاز أن يتفاوت حال المرئي صغراً وكبراً بتفاوت رأسه دقة وغلظاً. ألا ترى أن الإبصار إن كان بالانطباع كما زعموه كان الظاهر أن لا يتفاوت حال المرئي في الصغر والكبر بالقرب والبعد، لكن لما كان الانطباع على ما صوروه من توهم المخروط جاز أن يظهر التفاوت فيه بحسبهما . (و) يدل على صحة القول الأول أن ( من نظر إلى الشمس ) بتحديق وإمعان ) نظراً طويلاً ثم أعرض عنها ) وغمض عينيه ( فإنها تبقى صورتها في العين مدة ما حتى كأنه بعد التغميض ينظر إليها وكذا من نظر إلى الروضة المخضرة ) جداً ساعة طويلة نظراً بتدقيق، فإن عينيه يتكيفان بتلك الخضرة حتى إذا نظر إلى لون آخر لا يبصره خالصاً بل مخلوطاً بالخضرة أو غمض عينيه فإنه يجده كأنه ناظر إليها فلولا أن الإبصار بانطباع صورة المرئي لما كان الأمر كذلك ( ويمكن أن يقال على ( الدليل ( الأول : لعله ) أي لعل ما ذكرتموه من تفاوت المرئي الواحد في الكبر والصغر بالقرب والبعد ( لسبب آخر لا لانطباعه في جزء أكبر أو أصغر فإن عدم العلم به لا يوجب عدمه (و) أن يقال ( على الثاني : إن الصورة ) أي صورة الشمس أو الروضة ( إنما تبقى في الخيال دون الجليدية، ألا ترى أنه لا يتفاوت الحال بالتغميض والإبصار في هذه الحالة قطعاً
The cone rule as required by the theory of emission of rays, so the body should be seen as it is whether the radial lines exit from a narrow angle or a wide angle. Thus they said, but there is discussion because vision does not occur merely by the rule; rather, the apex of the cone also plays a role, so it is possible that the state of the visible object varies in smallness and largeness according to the fineness and coarseness of its apex. Do you not see that if vision were by impression as they claimed, it would appear that the state of the visible object would not vary in smallness and largeness with nearness and distance, but since impression occurs according to their portrayal of the imagined cone, it is possible that variation appears accordingly. And it indicates the soundness of the first theory that one who looks at the sun with squinting and intensity for a long time and then turns away from it and closes his eyes—its image remains in his eye for a while, as if after closing he looks at it. Similarly, one who looks at a very green meadow for a long time with careful gaze—his eyes become affected by that greenness, so that when he looks at another color, he does not see it purely but mixed with greenness, or when he closes his eyes, he finds it as if he is looking at it. Were it not that vision occurs by impression of the form of the visible object, it would not be so. And it can be said regarding the first proof: perhaps what you mentioned of the variation of a single visible object in largeness and smallness with nearness and distance is due to another cause, not to its impression in a larger or smaller part; for lack of knowledge of it does not necessitate its non-existence. And it can be said regarding the second proof: the image—i.e., the image of the sun or the meadow—remains only in the imagination, not on the cornea. Do you not see that the state does not vary with closing and opening the eyes in this case, absolutely?
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the impression (intiba') theory of vision, followed by two objections. The author begins by discussing the 'cone rule' associated with the emission theory, noting that the apex of the cone plays a role. He then transitions to the impression theory: observing that a nearby object appears larger supports impression. The first proof is the afterimage: looking at the sun or a green meadow leaves an afterimage when the eyes are closed, proving the form is impressed in the eye. Then two objections are given: first, the variation in apparent size may be due to another cause; second, the afterimage persists in the imagination (khayāl) not on the cornea (jālidiyya), as evidenced by the fact that closing the eyes does not affect the image.

شعاع (shu'ā')
مخروط (makhruṭ)

D) Imagination and visual impression are identical.

Key Takeaway
The afterimage argument is a key proof for the impression theory: persistent afterimages show that visual forms are literally imprinted on the eye.

Two Objections to the Intiba' Arguments
Al-Ījī presents two objections to the intiba' arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against the intiba' arguments and how they challenge the theory's conclusions.

Core Idea
Objections: size variation may have another cause, and afterimage persists in imagination, not eye.

After presenting the arguments for impression, al-Ījī anticipates two objections. The first challenges the size variation argument by suggesting an alternative cause, and the second contends that the afterimage persists in the imagination rather than on the eye's surface. These objections show the vulnerability of the impression theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the Arabic and translation of the two objections. Note that each begins with 'ويمكن أن يقال'.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection: the variation in size with distance might be due to another factor, not necessarily impression in a larger or smaller part of the eye.
This is a logical objection: lack of proof for the cause does not prove it is the only cause.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection: the afterimage remains in the imagination (khayāl), not on the cornea (jālidiyya), because closing the eyes does not affect it.
The evidence: the image does not change when eyes are closed, suggesting it is mental, not physical.
4
Consider the implications
These objections weaken the impression theory. Notice that al-Ījī does not refute them here; he leaves them as valid criticisms.
This shows scholarly fairness in presenting counterarguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة المخروط كما يقتضيه القول بخروج الشعاع، فيجب أن يرى الجسم كما هو سواء خرجت الخطوط الشعاعية من زاوية ضيقة أو غير ضيقة، هكذا قالوا وفيه بحث لأن الإبصار ليس حاصلاً بمجرد القاعدة بل لرأس المخروط فيه مدخل أيضاً فجاز أن يتفاوت حال المرئي صغراً وكبراً بتفاوت رأسه دقة وغلظاً. ألا ترى أن الإبصار إن كان بالانطباع كما زعموه كان الظاهر أن لا يتفاوت حال المرئي في الصغر والكبر بالقرب والبعد، لكن لما كان الانطباع على ما صوروه من توهم المخروط جاز أن يظهر التفاوت فيه بحسبهما . (و) يدل على صحة القول الأول أن ( من نظر إلى الشمس ) بتحديق وإمعان ) نظراً طويلاً ثم أعرض عنها ) وغمض عينيه ( فإنها تبقى صورتها في العين مدة ما حتى كأنه بعد التغميض ينظر إليها وكذا من نظر إلى الروضة المخضرة ) جداً ساعة طويلة نظراً بتدقيق، فإن عينيه يتكيفان بتلك الخضرة حتى إذا نظر إلى لون آخر لا يبصره خالصاً بل مخلوطاً بالخضرة أو غمض عينيه فإنه يجده كأنه ناظر إليها فلولا أن الإبصار بانطباع صورة المرئي لما كان الأمر كذلك ( ويمكن أن يقال على ( الدليل ( الأول : لعله ) أي لعل ما ذكرتموه من تفاوت المرئي الواحد في الكبر والصغر بالقرب والبعد ( لسبب آخر لا لانطباعه في جزء أكبر أو أصغر فإن عدم العلم به لا يوجب عدمه (و) أن يقال ( على الثاني : إن الصورة ) أي صورة الشمس أو الروضة ( إنما تبقى في الخيال دون الجليدية، ألا ترى أنه لا يتفاوت الحال بالتغميض والإبصار في هذه الحالة قطعاً
The cone rule as required by the theory of emission of rays, so the body should be seen as it is whether the radial lines exit from a narrow angle or a wide angle. Thus they said, but there is discussion because vision does not occur merely by the rule; rather, the apex of the cone also plays a role, so it is possible that the state of the visible object varies in smallness and largeness according to the fineness and coarseness of its apex. Do you not see that if vision were by impression as they claimed, it would appear that the state of the visible object would not vary in smallness and largeness with nearness and distance, but since impression occurs according to their portrayal of the imagined cone, it is possible that variation appears accordingly. And it indicates the soundness of the first theory that one who looks at the sun with squinting and intensity for a long time and then turns away from it and closes his eyes—its image remains in his eye for a while, as if after closing he looks at it. Similarly, one who looks at a very green meadow for a long time with careful gaze—his eyes become affected by that greenness, so that when he looks at another color, he does not see it purely but mixed with greenness, or when he closes his eyes, he finds it as if he is looking at it. Were it not that vision occurs by impression of the form of the visible object, it would not be so. And it can be said regarding the first proof: perhaps what you mentioned of the variation of a single visible object in largeness and smallness with nearness and distance is due to another cause, not to its impression in a larger or smaller part; for lack of knowledge of it does not necessitate its non-existence. And it can be said regarding the second proof: the image—i.e., the image of the sun or the meadow—remains only in the imagination, not on the cornea. Do you not see that the state does not vary with closing and opening the eyes in this case, absolutely?
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the impression (intiba') theory of vision, followed by two objections. The author begins by discussing the 'cone rule' associated with the emission theory, noting that the apex of the cone plays a role. He then transitions to the impression theory: observing that a nearby object appears larger supports impression. The first proof is the afterimage: looking at the sun or a green meadow leaves an afterimage when the eyes are closed, proving the form is impressed in the eye. Then two objections are given: first, the variation in apparent size may be due to another cause; second, the afterimage persists in the imagination (khayāl) not on the cornea (jālidiyya), as evidenced by the fact that closing the eyes does not affect the image.

Key Terms
جليدية (jālidiyya)
خيال (khayāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the afterimage argument is unquestionable proof for impression.
Correction: The second objection shows that the afterimage could be an imaginative aftereffect, not a physical imprint.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the second objection to the afterimage argument?
A) The afterimage does not occur in all people.
B) The afterimage remains unchanged when eyes are closed, suggesting it is in the imagination.
C) The afterimage proves emission theory.
D) The sun does not leave an afterimage.

Key Takeaway
The two objections cast doubt on the impression theory by offering alternative explanations: size variation may have another cause, and afterimage may be imaginative, not physical.

The Analogy Argument for Impression and Its Refutation
Al-Ījī uses analogy with other senses to argue for intiba', then refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ījī uses analogy to argue for the impression theory of vision and how it is refuted by challenging the validity of the analogy.

Core Idea
Analogy without a common factor is a weak argument, especially when comparing senses.

This passage presents a second argument for the impression theory: the eye must work like the other senses, where objects come to the sense organ. However, Al-Ījī immediately points out a flaw: this analogy lacks a valid common factor, making it insufficient to prove the theory. This lesson explores both the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage has two parts: the argument by analogy and the refutation.
The argument uses the term 'uswa (equality/analogy)'.
2
Understand the analogy argument
Identify the premise: the other senses perceive by objects coming to them. The conclusion: therefore, vision must also work by objects coming to the eye (intiba'). Ask yourself: Is this a sound analogy?
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation says this is 'tamthīl' (analogy) without a 'jāmi'' (common factor). The objector claims vision might work differently. Understand why this objection undermines the argument.
The key is that analogy requires a shared relevant property.
4
Reflect on the role of analogy
Consider why analogy alone is insufficient in serious theological and philosophical reasoning. How does this episode illustrate the careful use of analogical arguments?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ) مما يدل على صحته أيضاً أن يقال: (له) أي للبصر في إدراكه ( أسوة بسائر الحواس) الظاهرة (إذ ليس إدراكها لمدركاتها ( بأن يخرج منها شيء ويتصل ) ذلك الشيء (بالمحسوس، بل ) إدراكها إياها إنما هو لأن المحسوس يأتيها فوجب أن لا يكون الإحساس بالبصر لخروج شيء منه إلى المبصر بل لأن صورته تأتيه فدل ذلك على صحة الانطباع وفساد الشعاع (و) أن يقال (على الثالث أنه تمثيل) وقياس للبصر على الحواس الأخر ( بلا جامع) معتبر إذ من الجائز أن يكون إدراك هذه الحاسة بخروج شيء منها إلى مدركها دون باقي الحواس الظاهرة
And among the proofs of its soundness is that it is said: the eye has an equality with the other external senses, since their perception of their objects is not by something exiting from them and connecting to the sensed object, but rather because the sensed object comes to them. Therefore, it is necessary that vision is not by something exiting from it to the visible object, but because its form comes to it. That indicates the soundness of impression and the corruption of emission. And it can be said regarding the third proof that it is an analogy and comparison of vision to the other senses without a valid common factor, since it is possible that the perception of this sense is by something exiting from it to its object, unlike the other external senses.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument for the impression theory based on analogy with the other senses. The eye should be like hearing, smell, etc., where the object comes to the sense organ. Therefore, vision must work by the form of the object entering the eye, not by emission. Then a refutation is given: this is an analogy without a valid common factor (jāmi'), because it is possible that vision works differently from the other senses, i.e., by emission. This objection questions the basis of the analogy.

Key Terms
أسوة (uswa)
تمثيل (tamthīl)
جامع (jāmi')
انطباع (intiba')
شعاع (shu'ā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogy alone is a sufficient proof for a theory if the compared items are similar in some respect.
Correction: Analogy requires a valid common factor (jāmi') that is relevant and shared necessarily. Without it, the analogy is weak and does not constitute a proof.

Checkpoint
How is the analogy argument for intiba' criticized?
A) It relies on an unproven premise.
B) It is an analogy without a valid common factor.
C) The other senses are not reliable.
D) The eye is not physically like other senses.

Key Takeaway
The impression theory's analogy argument fails because it does not establish a necessary common factor between the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for a belief by comparing it to something else, do you carefully check for a valid common factor, or do you assume similarity without proof? This passage warns against weak analogy. Think of a time you used analogy to justify an action or belief. Did you really have the necessary common factor, or were you just comfortable with the comparison? Will you examine your analogies more critically starting today?
?
Al-Ījī presents both the argument and its refutation. Are you willing to honestly consider objections to your own deepest convictions, or do you dismiss them without thought? What is one belief you hold that you have never seriously questioned? Will you seek out its strongest refutation to test its foundation?
?
The analogy argument seemed plausible—many might accept it without noticing the missing common factor. Are there places in your faith or practice where you follow what seems plausible without deeper verification? What is one thing you have accepted 'because everyone does it' or 'it makes sense' that you should verify from sound evidence? Will you take one step today to investigate it properly?

Galen's Objection to Impression and Its Answer
Al-Ījī presents Galen's objection and the reply, distinguishing two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's argument against the theory of impression and the key distinction that saves the theory.

Core Idea
Galen's objection only refutes one misinterpretation of impression, not the theory itself.

The theory of impression holds that vision occurs when forms of objects impress upon the eye. Opponents raised several arguments, but Galen's was considered the most powerful. He argued that a small eye cannot receive the form of a large object, yet we see vast things. The answer to this objection hinges on a crucial distinction between the image (shabah) and the external object itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify Galen's argument
Find the premise and conclusion of Galen's argument. Why does he think vision of large objects is impossible under impression?
The premise: a body only receives forms equal in size.
3
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer says the impossible is the large shape itself being in the small, not an image of it. Notice how this distinction works.
The image can be smaller than the object.
4
Reflect on the two views
The passage contrasts two interpretations of impression: one where the image itself is the visible object, and one where the image is only a condition. Why does only the first fall to Galen's objection?
5
Consider the historical note
The author criticizes later scholars who misunderstood Aristotle. What mistake did they make?
They confused the image with the visible object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج النفاة ) للانطباع ( بوجوه والعمدة في الاحتجاج عليه ( ما ذكره جالينوس وهو أن الجسم لا ينطبع فيه من الأشكال إلا ما يساويه في المقدار فوجب ) على تقدير كون الإبصار نفس الانطباع أو مشروطاً به ( أن لا يبصر) من الأشياء ( إلا قدر نقطة الناظر منها ) وهو السواد الأصغر الذي فيه إنسان العين ( لكنا نبصر نصف كرة العالم والجواب : إنه لا يمتنع حصول شبح الكبير في الصغير إنما . المحال حصول ذلك الشكل ( الكبير ( بعينه ) في الصغير ( والحاصل ) مما ذكرنا في الجواب أن هذا الذي أورده جالينوس ( إنما يرد على من يرى) ويعتقد أن المبصر نفس الشبح ) المنطبع في الجليدية كما توهمه المتأخرون من كلام المعلم الأول وحكوه عنه ( وأما من يزعم أن حصول الشبح شرط للإبصار ) وأن المبصر هو ذلك الأمر الخارجي ( فلا يرد عليه ذلك ) الذي أورده فإن شبح الشيء قد لا يساويه في المقدار وإن كان موجباً لإبصاره على ما هو عليه ( وهذا) الأخير ( هو الحق) على القول بالانطباع وفي الملخص: إن المتأخرين لم يفهموا كلامه فحكوه على ما لا ينبغي، فتارة قالوا إن هذه الصورة نفس الإبصار وأخرى قالوا: إنها الإبصار والمبصر معاً، وأما الموجود الخارجي فغير مرئي أصلاً، ثم إنهم تعصبوا لهذه الخرافات وعرضوا معلمهم لطعن الطاعنين فهم كالرواة السوء للشاعر الجيد
The deniers of impression argued with several proofs, the most important of which is what Galen mentioned: that a body only receives forms equal to itself in magnitude. Therefore, assuming vision is itself impression or conditional upon it, we would only see from objects the size of the point of the observer, which is the smallest pupil that contains the black of the eye, whereas we see the whole hemisphere of the world. The answer: it is not impossible for an image of a large thing to exist in a small thing; rather, the impossible is the existence of that large shape itself in the small. The conclusion of what we mentioned in the answer is that what Galen brought forward only applies to those who believe that the visible object is itself the image impressed on the cornea, as the later scholars misunderstood from the First Teacher (Aristotle) and transmitted about him. But as for those who claim that the image's existence is a condition for vision and that the visible object is that external thing, then what he brought forward does not apply to them, because the image of a thing may not equal it in magnitude while still causing its vision as it is. And this latter view is the truth according to the theory of impression. In al-Mulakhkhas: The later scholars did not understand his words and transmitted them improperly. Sometimes they said that this image is vision itself, and other times they said it is both vision and the visible object together, while the external existent is not visible at all. Then they adhered fanatically to these absurdities and exposed their teacher to the criticism of critics; they are like evil transmitters of a good poet.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the main argument of the opponents of impression: Galen's objection that a small eye cannot contain the form of a large object. The author provides the answer: it is not the physical object itself that is impressed, but an image (shabah) of it, which can be smaller. The answer distinguishes between those who take the image itself to be the visible object (which would be subject to Galen's objection) and those who consider the image only a condition for seeing the external object (which avoids the problem). The author endorses the latter as the correct view on the theory of impression. Then he adds a critical note about later scholars who misinterpreted Aristotle, causing confusion and making their teacher a target of criticism.

Key Terms
جالينوس (Jālīnūs)
شبح (shabah)
جليدية (jālidiyya)
انطباع (intiba')
مبصر (mubṣar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Galen's argument disproves the theory of impression entirely.
Correction: Galen's argument only disproves a specific version of impression where the image itself is taken as the visible object. The correct version, where the image is only a condition for seeing the external object, remains valid.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does Galen's argument actually refute?
A) The possibility of any impression.
B) The view that the impressed image is itself the visible object.
C) The existence of images in the eye.
D) The emission theory.

Key Takeaway
Galen's argument against impression is answered by distinguishing the image from the external object, saving the theory from refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage warns against blindly adhering to a misinterpretation and misrepresenting a teacher. When you hear a criticism of a scholar or a doctrine, do you check the original source before accepting or transmitting it? Are you careful not to become an 'evil transmitter'?
?
The later scholars fanatically clung to their misunderstanding despite clear reasoning. In your own religious practice, are there any beliefs you hold out of habit or group loyalty, even when the evidence points otherwise? What would it cost you to re-examine them?
?
The correct view distinguishes between the image and the external object—a kind of intellectual precision. Where in your life do you conflate your mental picture of something (e.g., a person, an issue) with the reality? Are you willing to let the external truth correct your internal image?

The Three Variants of the Emission Theory
Al-Ījī enumerates three versions of the emission theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct versions of the emission theory of vision as proposed by the mathematicians.

Core Idea
Vision occurs via a radiant cone from the eye, but its structure is debated.

Al-Ījī introduces the emission theory as the second major explanation of vision, popular among mathematicians. However, they disagreed on the exact nature of the emitted radiant body. This lesson examines the three variants they proposed: solid, composite of lines, and scanning line.

Guided Path
1
Read the three variants
Read the passage describing the three versions of the emission theory. Notice the structure: first the general claim, then each variant.
The variants are numbered first, second, third.
2
Identify each variant's key feature
For each variant, note what the emitted cone or line is made of and how it leads to perception.
The first variant is solid, the second is composed of thin lines, the third is a single scanning line.
3
Compare the mechanisms
Think about how each variant accounts for seeing small objects or the entire field. The second variant explains why very small objects are missed; the third uses rapid scanning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( القول الثاني : إنه يخرج من العين جسم شعاعي على هيئة مخروط متحقق (رأسه يلي العين وقاعدته تلي المبصر والإدراك التام إنما يحصل من الموضع الذي هو موضع المخروط) وهو مذهب جمهور الرياضين، ثم إنهم اختلفوا فيه على وجوه ثلاثة : الأول : إن ذلك المخروط مصمت. الثاني : إنه ملتئم من خطوط مستقيمة شعاعية هي أجسام دقاق قد اجتمع أطرافها عند مركز البصر وامتدت متفرقة إلى المبصر فما وقع عليه أطراف تلك الخطوط أدركه البصر وما وقع بين أطرافها لم يدركه، ولذلك يخفى على البصر الأجزاء التي في غاية الصغر الثالث : إنه يخرج من العين جسم شعاعي رفيع كأنه خط واحد مستقيم ينتهي إلى المبصر ثم يتحرك على سطحه حركة سريعة جدا في طول المرئي وعرضه فيحصل الإدراك به. واحتجوا على مذهبهم بأن الإنسان إذا رأى وجهه في المرآة فليس ذلك لانطباع صورته فيها وإلا كانت منطبعة في موضع معين منها ولم تختلف باختلاف أمكنة الرائي من الجوانب بل لأن الشعاع خرج من العين إلى المرآة ثم انعكس منها لصقالتها إلى الوجه. ألا يرى أنه إذا قرب الوجه منها تخيل أن صورته مرتسمة في سطحها وإذا بعد عنها توهم أنها غائرة فيها مع علمنا بأن المرآة ليس لها غور بذلك المقدار.
The second theory: there exits from the eye a radiant body in the form of a real cone, its apex at the eye and its base at the visible object, and complete perception only occurs from the location of the cone. This is the doctrine of the majority of mathematicians. Then they differed concerning it in three ways: First: that cone is solid. Second: it is composed of straight radiant lines that are thin bodies; their ends meet at the center of sight and extend separately to the visible object; what falls on the ends of those lines is seen, and what falls between them is not seen, and therefore extremely small parts are hidden from vision. Third: there exits from the eye a thin radiant body like a single straight line that reaches the visible object and then moves rapidly over its surface in length and width, and perception occurs thereby. They argued for their doctrine by the fact that when a man sees his face in a mirror, that is not due to the impression of his image in it, otherwise it would be impressed in a particular place and would not differ with different positions of the viewer, but because the ray goes out from the eye to the mirror and then is reflected by its smoothness to the face. Do you not see that when he brings his face near it, he imagines his image as depicted on its surface, and when he moves away, he imagines it as deep within it, even though we know that the mirror has no such depth.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the second major theory of vision: a radiant body (jism shu'ā'ī) exits the eye in the form of a cone. This view belongs to the majority of mathematicians (riyāḍiyyīn). They differ on the nature of the cone: first, solid; second, composed of thin lines; third, a single line that scans the object. The proponents of this theory argue using the mirror example: if vision were by impression, the image in a mirror would be fixed in one spot, but it moves with the viewer. Instead, the ray leaves the eye, reflects off the mirror, and reaches the face. The changing apparent depth of the image supports the emission theory.

Key Terms
شعاع (shu'ā')
مخروط (makhruṭ)
رياضيون (riyāḍiyyūn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second variant of the emission theory, why are very small objects invisible?
A) Because they are too far away
B) Because they fall between the radiating lines
C) Because the cone is solid and absorbs them
D) Because the scanning line moves too fast

Key Takeaway
The emission theory of vision has three main variants: a solid cone, a composite of lines, and a scanning line.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You use your eyes every moment without thinking about how they work. Which of these three models best matches how you actually experience seeing? And what does that reveal about your tendency to assume your perception is complete?
?
The mathematicians debated these models without microscopes or lasers. When you consider the limits of their knowledge, do you trust your own senses too much? In what area of your life are you relying on surface-level observation when you should be seeking deeper truth?
?
The third model describes a rapid scan that misses nothing, yet you often scan through your day without truly seeing. What are you overlooking—in people, in your own heart, or in the signs of Allah—because you move too quickly to perceive carefully?

The Mirror Argument for the Emission Theory
Al-Ījī provides a mirror argument to support the emission theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mirror reflection argument is used to support the emission theory over the impression theory.

Core Idea
The changing image in a mirror proves a ray leaves the eye.

After presenting the three variants of the emission theory, al-Ījī provides a key argument based on mirror reflection. The argument challenges the alternative impression theory by showing that the apparent depth and movement of a reflection cannot be explained unless a ray travels from the eye to the mirror and back.

Guided Path
1
Read the mirror argument passage
Read the argument carefully, noting the logical structure: if impression, then image fixed; but image changes; therefore not impression; therefore emission.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the premise and observation
Understand that according to the impression theory, the image should be fixed in the mirror, but in reality it appears at different depths depending on viewer distance.
The observation is that the image seems to move forward or backward.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The emission theory explains this by saying the ray goes from eye to mirror and then reflects to the face, so the apparent depth changes with the viewer's distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( القول الثاني : إنه يخرج من العين جسم شعاعي على هيئة مخروط متحقق (رأسه يلي العين وقاعدته تلي المبصر والإدراك التام إنما يحصل من الموضع الذي هو موضع المخروط) وهو مذهب جمهور الرياضين، ثم إنهم اختلفوا فيه على وجوه ثلاثة : الأول : إن ذلك المخروط مصمت. الثاني : إنه ملتئم من خطوط مستقيمة شعاعية هي أجسام دقاق قد اجتمع أطرافها عند مركز البصر وامتدت متفرقة إلى المبصر فما وقع عليه أطراف تلك الخطوط أدركه البصر وما وقع بين أطرافها لم يدركه، ولذلك يخفى على البصر الأجزاء التي في غاية الصغر الثالث : إنه يخرج من العين جسم شعاعي رفيع كأنه خط واحد مستقيم ينتهي إلى المبصر ثم يتحرك على سطحه حركة سريعة جدا في طول المرئي وعرضه فيحصل الإدراك به. واحتجوا على مذهبهم بأن الإنسان إذا رأى وجهه في المرآة فليس ذلك لانطباع صورته فيها وإلا كانت منطبعة في موضع معين منها ولم تختلف باختلاف أمكنة الرائي من الجوانب بل لأن الشعاع خرج من العين إلى المرآة ثم انعكس منها لصقالتها إلى الوجه. ألا يرى أنه إذا قرب الوجه منها تخيل أن صورته مرتسمة في سطحها وإذا بعد عنها توهم أنها غائرة فيها مع علمنا بأن المرآة ليس لها غور بذلك المقدار.
The second theory: there exits from the eye a radiant body in the form of a real cone, its apex at the eye and its base at the visible object, and complete perception only occurs from the location of the cone. This is the doctrine of the majority of mathematicians. Then they differed concerning it in three ways: First: that cone is solid. Second: it is composed of straight radiant lines that are thin bodies; their ends meet at the center of sight and extend separately to the visible object; what falls on the ends of those lines is seen, and what falls between them is not seen, and therefore extremely small parts are hidden from vision. Third: there exits from the eye a thin radiant body like a single straight line that reaches the visible object and then moves rapidly over its surface in length and width, and perception occurs thereby. They argued for their doctrine by the fact that when a man sees his face in a mirror, that is not due to the impression of his image in it, otherwise it would be impressed in a particular place and would not differ with different positions of the viewer, but because the ray goes out from the eye to the mirror and then is reflected by its smoothness to the face. Do you not see that when he brings his face near it, he imagines his image as depicted on its surface, and when he moves away, he imagines it as deep within it, even though we know that the mirror has no such depth.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the second major theory of vision: a radiant body (jism shu'ā'ī) exits the eye in the form of a cone. This view belongs to the majority of mathematicians (riyāḍiyyīn). They differ on the nature of the cone: first, solid; second, composed of thin lines; third, a single line that scans the object. The proponents of this theory argue using the mirror example: if vision were by impression, the image in a mirror would be fixed in one spot, but it moves with the viewer. Instead, the ray leaves the eye, reflects off the mirror, and reaches the face. The changing apparent depth of the image supports the emission theory.

Key Terms
انعكاس (in'ikās)
صقالة (ṣiqāla)
مرآة (mir'āh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mirror argument proves that the emission theory is correct.
Correction: The argument only serves as evidence for the emission theory; it does not definitively prove it, as other objections (not mentioned here) might exist.

Checkpoint
Why does the mirror argument challenge the impression theory?
A) Because mirrors are smooth
B) Because the image would be fixed in one place if it were an impression
C) Because the ray cannot reflect
D) Because the mirror has depth

Key Takeaway
The mirror argument uses the changing apparent depth of a reflection to argue that a ray must exit the eye.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ījī uses the shifting mirror image to argue that you are not a passive receiver of impressions. In what areas of your life have you been waiting passively for truth or change, rather than actively seeking it out as a ray from within you?
?
The emission theory says the ray of sight goes from you to meet the object. How does this challenge you to take personal responsibility for what you 'see'—not just physically, but in your understanding of situations and people?
?
The image in the mirror is not a fixed impression but depends on your position. How often do you let others' reflections of you—their opinions, praise, or criticism—define who you are, when your true self is deeper than these shifting surfaces?

The Third Theory of Vision: Air as Instrument
Iji defines the third theory of vision and notes its similarity to the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third theory of vision in kalam and how it remains dependent on the concept of a ray.

Core Idea
The third theory replaces the ray with modified air but still relies on a ray-like concept.

Iji presents the third theory of vision, which attempts to avoid the emission of a ray from the eye. Instead, it proposes that the air between the eye and the object takes on the quality of a ray. However, as Iji notes, this theory is essentially a variant of the second theory because it still builds on the notion of a ray. Read the passage to see how this theory is described and then categorized.

Guided Path
1
Read the third theory definition
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to the phrase 'في حكم المذهب الثاني كما مر'. The translation is provided. Notice that the theory says no ray exits, but the air becomes modified.
The key term 'شعاع' (ray) appears in the negation.
2
Identify the role of air
The theory states that the air between the eye and the object becomes 'an instrument in vision' after acquiring the quality of a ray. Reflect on what this means.
3
Understand the classification
Iji then says this theory is 'in the ruling of the second school' because it also depends on the ray concept. Ask yourself why that is.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالث : هو أنه ليس يخرج من العين شعاع لكن الهواء الذي بينها وبين المرئي يتكيف بكيفية الشعاع الذي فيها ويصير ذلك آلة في الإبصار، ولما كان هذا أيضاً مبنياً على الشعاع كان في حكم المذهب الثاني كما مر . ( ويبطله ) أي المذهب الثاني ( أنه إذا كان ) هناك ( ريح ) عاصفة ( أو اضطراب في الهواء وجب أن تتشوش تلك الشعاعات ) الخارجة من العين ) وتتصل بالأشياء الغير المقابلة للوجه، فوجب أن يرى الإنسان ما لا يقابله لاتصال شعاعه به كما أنه لما كان الصوت عبارة عن الكيفية التي يحملها الهواء المتموج لا جرم أنه يضطرب عند هبوب الرياح ويميل من جهة إلى جهة )
Third: it is that a ray does not exit from the eye, but the air between it and the visible object becomes modified with the quality of the ray that is in it, and that becomes an instrument in vision. And since this is also built on the ray, it is in the ruling of the second school as passed. And it refutes it, meaning the second school: that if there is a violent wind or disturbance in the air, then those rays (exiting from the eye) would necessarily be disturbed and would connect to objects not opposite the face, so a person would necessarily see what is not opposite him because his ray connects to it, just as when sound is an expression of the quality that the undulating air carries, inevitably it is disturbed when the winds blow and inclines from one direction to another.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the third theory of vision and then refutes the second theory via the argument from wind disturbance. It begins by stating the third theory: the eye does not emit a ray; rather, the air between the eye and the object takes on the quality of a ray and becomes the instrument of vision. It then notes that this theory is built on the notion of a ray, so it is effectively similar to the second theory (which posits a luminous ray from the eye). Then comes the refutation of the second theory: if there is a strong wind or air disturbance, the rays exiting the eye would be disrupted and would connect to objects not directly opposite the face. Consequently, a person would see things not facing him, which is false. The argument draws an analogy with sound: sound is a quality carried by undulating air, so it is naturally disturbed by wind and shifts direction. Since we do not in fact see objects that are not in front of us even in windy conditions, the second theory is invalidated.

Key Terms
القول بالشعاع
الهواء
الإبصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third theory of vision, what becomes the instrument of sight?
A) A ray emitted from the eye.
B) The air modified with the ray's quality.
C) The object itself.
D) The eye's lens.

Key Takeaway
The third theory of vision still depends on the concept of a ray because the air acquires the ray's quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on something invisible (air) every moment for vision, yet rarely reflect on its created nature. How does this dependence on a created medium remind you of your constant need for Allah's sustenance? In what specific action today can you turn that dependence into gratitude?
?
Iji's critique shows that a seemingly new theory can still rest on old flawed assumptions. Consider a belief or habit in your life that you thought you had reformed, but still depends on the same old neglect of your soul. What is one concrete step you can take today to truly break that foundation?

Refuting the Emission Theory: The Wind Argument
Iji refutes the second theory of vision using the wind disturbance argument and an analogy with sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument against the emission theory using the effect of wind on rays and the analogy with sound.

Core Idea
Wind would disturb emitted rays, causing them to connect with non-opposite objects, which does not happen.

Iji refutes the second theory (emission) by considering the effect of wind. If rays exit the eye, a strong wind would disturb them and cause them to reach objects not directly opposite the face. But we do not see such objects. He strengthens the argument by analogy with sound, which is also carried by air and is disturbed by wind. Read the refutation carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic from 'ويبطله' to the end. The translation explains the conditional argument.
The word 'ويبطله' means 'and it refutes it'—referring to the second theory.
2
Analyze the conditional statement
The argument: IF there is wind, THEN rays would be disturbed and connect to objects not opposite the face. THEN a person would see what is not opposite him. This is a hypothetical consequence.
The conclusion 'فوجب أن يرى الإنسان ما لا يقابله' is the prediction.
3
Understand the sound analogy
Iji compares with sound: sound is a quality carried by undulating air, so when wind blows, sound is disturbed and shifts direction. Similarly, rays would be disturbed. But we do not experience seeing behind, so the theory fails.
The analogy shows the theory's inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالث : هو أنه ليس يخرج من العين شعاع لكن الهواء الذي بينها وبين المرئي يتكيف بكيفية الشعاع الذي فيها ويصير ذلك آلة في الإبصار، ولما كان هذا أيضاً مبنياً على الشعاع كان في حكم المذهب الثاني كما مر . ( ويبطله ) أي المذهب الثاني ( أنه إذا كان ) هناك ( ريح ) عاصفة ( أو اضطراب في الهواء وجب أن تتشوش تلك الشعاعات ) الخارجة من العين ) وتتصل بالأشياء الغير المقابلة للوجه، فوجب أن يرى الإنسان ما لا يقابله لاتصال شعاعه به كما أنه لما كان الصوت عبارة عن الكيفية التي يحملها الهواء المتموج لا جرم أنه يضطرب عند هبوب الرياح ويميل من جهة إلى جهة )
Third: it is that a ray does not exit from the eye, but the air between it and the visible object becomes modified with the quality of the ray that is in it, and that becomes an instrument in vision. And since this is also built on the ray, it is in the ruling of the second school as passed. Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the third theory of vision and then refutes the second theory via the argument from wind disturbance. It begins by stating the third theory: the eye does not emit a ray; rather, the air between the eye and the object takes on the quality of a ray and becomes the instrument of vision. It then notes that this theory is built on the notion of a ray, so it is effectively similar to the second theory (which posits a luminous ray from the eye). Then comes the refutation of the second theory: if there is a strong wind or air disturbance, the rays exiting the eye would be disrupted and would connect to objects not directly opposite the face. Consequently, a person would see things not facing him, which is false. The argument draws an analogy with sound: sound is a quality carried by undulating air, so it is naturally disturbed by wind and shifts direction. Since we do not in fact see objects that are not in front of us even in windy conditions, the second theory is invalidated.

Correction: The passage shows that if rays are emitted through the air, they would be subject to disturbances like sound.

The Impossibility of the Eye's Ray Reaching the Celestial Sphere
Iji refutes emission and third theories by impossibility of light reaching heavens.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that refutes the emission theory and the third theory by demonstrating the impossibility of the eye's light affecting the celestial sphere.

Core Idea
A sparrow's light cannot reach the stars; therefore, the eye does not emit a ray for vision.

We return to the refutation of the theories of vision. The author presents a second, stronger argument that targets both the emission theory and the third theory (air modification). The argument appeals to necessary knowledge: the light from a tiny eye cannot possibly penetrate vast distances to affect the heavens. This self-evident fact undermines any theory that posits an outward effect from the eye.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the structural markers: 'وأيضاً', 'بل نقول', 'وإذا كان الأمر كذلك'.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states: 'you necessarily know that the light from a sparrow's eye cannot affect what is between it and the fixed stars.' This is the premise.
The example of a sparrow emphasizes the smallness of the eye.
3
Follow the extended reasoning
The author then argues that even if the entire creature were light, it would not extend ten leagues. This shows the absurdity of the ray reaching celestial bodies.
This strengthens the argument by showing that no possible emission from the eye could cover such distance.
4
Understand al-Razi's confirmation
Imam al-Razi states that we know by necessary knowledge that the small eye cannot affect half the celestial sphere. This reinforces the conclusion.
Al-Razi also expresses amazement that the three theories were accepted, highlighting their obvious falsity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the claim of the ray and its mediation in vision is absolutely nullified. All three schools are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار إلى إبطاله وإبطال المذهب الثالث معاً بقوله : وأيضاً فتعلم ضرورة أن النور الذي يخرج من عين العصفور يستحيل أن يؤثر فيما بينه وبين الكواكب الثابتة ) أي يستحيل أن يقوى ذلك النور على خرق الهواء والأفلاك بحيث يصل إلى الثوابت ويتصل بنصف كرة العالم، ويستحيل أيضاً أن يقوى نور عينه على إحالة ما بينهما إلى كيفيته . بل نقول : ذلك العصفور أو الإنسان أو الفيل إن كان كله نوراً لما امتد ولا أحال ) إلى كيفيته ( من الهواء عشرة فراسخ وإن لم يكن هذا جلياً في العقل فلا جلي عنده) وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يتصور امتداده إلى الثوابت ولا إحالة الشعاع الذي في العين ما بينهما إلى جوهره فبطل القول بالشعاع وتوسطه في الإبصار مطلقاً . قال الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية : حاصل الكلام في هذا المقام أن نقول : إنا نعلم علماً ضرورياً بأن العين على صغرها لا يمكن أن تحيل نصف كرة العالم إلى كيفيتها ولا أن يخرج منها ما يتصل بنصف كرته ولا أن يدخل فيها صورة نصفه . فالمذاهب الثلاثة ظاهرة الفساد بتأمل قليل في هذا الذي ذكرناه وإني لأتعجب من اشتهارها فيما بين الناس وإقبالهم على قبولها .
And he pointed to its refutation and the refutation of the third school together by his saying: And also you necessarily know that the light that comes out of a sparrow's eye is impossible to affect what is between it and the fixed stars — that is, it is impossible for that light to have the power to penetrate the air and the celestial spheres such that it reaches the fixed stars and connects with half the celestial sphere, and it is also impossible for the light of his eye to have the power to transform what is between them into its quality. Rather we say: that sparrow or human or elephant, if it were entirely light, would not extend nor transform into its quality even ten leagues of air, and if this is not evident to the intellect, then nothing is evident to it. And if this is the case, its extension to the fixed stars is inconceivable, nor the transformation of the ray that is in the eye of what is between them into its substance. So the claim of the ray and its mediation in vision is nullified absolutely. Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: The gist of the discussion in this place is to say: We know with necessary knowledge that the eye, despite its smallness, cannot transform half the celestial sphere into its quality, nor can there exit from it something that connects to half its sphere, nor can the image of half of it enter into it. So the three schools are clearly invalid with a little reflection on what we have mentioned, and I am amazed at their fame among people and their acceptance of them.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a second, stronger refutation that targets both the second theory (emission) and the third theory (air modification). It begins by quoting a phrase from the original text: 'you necessarily know that the light from a sparrow's eye cannot affect what is between it and the fixed stars.' The commentator then explains: it is impossible for that tiny light to penetrate the air and spheres to reach the fixed stars and connect with half the world. Moreover, even if the entire body of the sparrow were light, it could not extend ten leagues. Since this is self-evident, the idea of the ray reaching the fixed stars is absurd. Thus, any theory relying on a ray from the eye is false. Then Imam al-Razi is cited for confirmation: we know by necessary knowledge that the small eye cannot transform half the world, nor emit something connecting to it, nor have its image entering it. Therefore, all three schools are clearly false, and the author expresses amazement at their widespread acceptance.

Key Terms
الكواكب الثابتة
الشعاع
الإحالة
العقل
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the eye's ray could be extremely subtle and thus reach the heavens despite the eye's smallness.
Correction: The argument shows that even if the entire body were light, it would still be insufficient. The smallness of the source makes it impossible to affect vast distances, as this is self-evident.

Checkpoint

A) The eye can transform the air into its quality.
B) The eye cannot affect the celestial sphere due to its smallness.
C) The eye emits a ray that reaches the fixed stars.
D) Vision is impossible without a ray.

Key Takeaway
The necessary knowledge that a small eye cannot affect vast distances refutes all theories that postulate an external effect from the eye.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage says if the impossibility of a small light reaching the stars is not clear to your intellect, then nothing is. Are you willing to trust your God-given intellect when it points to clear truths, even if they challenge your habits or desires?
?
Imam al-Razi was amazed at people's acceptance of false theories. What 'amazing' falsehoods have you accepted without scrutiny? Will you commit today to examining one such belief with the same necessary knowledge used here?

Vision as a Relational State
Iji proposes an alternative account of vision as a special relational state without emission or impression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Iji's alternative account of vision as a special relational state and why rejecting one theory does not automatically validate the other.

Core Idea
Vision is a relational state, not emission or impression.

Iji offers a third possibility for how vision works, beyond the two dominant theories. Instead of physical emission or impression, he defines vision as a special relational state that occurs when conditions are met. This passage also clarifies a key logical point about contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Iji's proposal come through before interpreting.
Notice the concise style; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the components of the alternative theory
Find the definition of vision as a 'special sensation' and then as a 'relational state'. Note the conditions: sound sense, all conditions present, obstacles removed.
3
Understand the logical point
The passage states that refuting emission or impression does not prove the other. Why? Because they are not logical contradictories. There can be a third option.
This is a key insight in kalam methodology.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this alternative avoids the problems of emission and impression theories. It presents vision as an immediate relational state, not a physical process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومن المحتمل أن يقال : الإبصار شعور مخصوص وذلك الشعور حالة إضافية فمتى كانت الحاسة سليمة وسائر الشرائط حاصلة والموانع مرتفعة حصلت للمبصر هذه الإضافة من غير أن يخرج من عينه أو ينطبع فيها صورة فليس يلزم من إبطال الشعاع أو الانطباع صحة الآخر إذ ليسا على طرفي النقيض )
He said: And it is possible to say: vision is a special sensation, and that sensation is a relational state. So when the sense faculty is sound, all conditions are present, and obstacles are removed, this relation occurs for the seer without anything emitting from his eye or an image being impressed in it. So refuting the ray or impression does not entail the correctness of the other, since they are not contradictories.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage proposes an alternative explanation of vision that avoids both the emission theory and the impression theory. It defines vision as a special sensation (shu'ur makhsous) that is not a physical emission or impression but a relational state (hala idafiyya). When the sense faculty is healthy, all necessary conditions are met, and no obstacles are present, this relation simply occurs. The passage also clarifies a logical point: refuting one theory (e.g., the emission theory) does not automatically validate the other (impression theory), because the two are not logical contradictories; there could be a third possibility, as suggested here.

Key Terms
شعور مخصوص
حالة إضافية
الشعاع
الانطباع
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rejecting the emission theory automatically proves the impression theory.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they are not contradictories; therefore, rejecting one does not entail accepting the other. A third option, like the relational state, remains possible.

Checkpoint
According to the alternative theory, what is vision?
A) An emission of a ray from the eye.
B) An impression of an image in the eye.
C) A special sensation that is a relational state.
D) A combination of ray and impression.

Key Takeaway
Vision can be understood as a special relational state that arises under appropriate conditions, independent of any physical emission or impression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Iji presents seeing as a relational state established by God when conditions are right, not a mechanical process. Reflect: Are there areas in your life where you rely on mechanical explanations (cause and effect) and fail to recognize Allah's direct creation of events? How might this shift affect your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
The passage argues that disproving one theory doesn't prove another. Consider a belief you hold strongly about how the world works. Are you assuming that because a competing view is false, your view must be true? How can you guard against false dichotomies in your understanding of religion?
?
Vision is described as a 'special sensation' that occurs spontaneously when obstacles are removed. Think of a spiritual 'blindness' you may have — an area where your heart is not seeing clearly. What obstacles (pride, sin, distraction) need to be removed for you to perceive Allah's signs and truths clearly today?
