Classification of Active Faculties: Inciting and Motor
Classification of active faculties into inciting and motor, with four principles of voluntary action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of active faculties into inciting and motor, the subdivision of inciting into appetitive and irascible, and the four principles of voluntary action: conception, desire, will, and motor execution.

Core Idea
Voluntary action involves four sequential principles: conception, desire, will, and motor execution.

This passage from al-Mawaqif examines the active faculties that produce movement. The author distinguishes between the inciting power that prompts action and the motor power that executes it, and then unpacks the psychological process behind every voluntary act. By tracing the sequence from conception to physical movement, we gain insight into the integrated nature of human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the definitions and divisions.
2
Identify the two main types of active faculties
Notice the division into inciting (ba'itha) and motor (muharrika). The inciting is further divided into appetitive and irascible.
3
Trace the four principles of voluntary action
The passage lists conception, desire, will, and motor execution. Understand how they are arranged sequentially.
4
Reflect on the benefit of the term 'active'
The commentary explains why the author prefers 'active' over 'motor'.
5
Review the lexical notes
Understand the meanings of 'nazu'a' and 'shanj' as given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(النوع الثالث : القوى الفاعلة) هي التي عبر عنها فيما سبق بالمحركة على معنى أن لها مدخلاً في الحركة إما بالتحريك أو الإعانة على قياس ما مر في المدركة وفائدة العدول ظاهرة ( وتنقسم إلى ( قوة ( باعثة ) على الحركة (و) قوة ( محركة) مباشرة للتحريك . ( أما الباعثة ) وتسمى شوقية ونزوعة ( فإما لجلب النفع وتسمى شهوية وإما لدفع الضرر وتسمى غضبية. وأما المحركة فهي التي تمدد الأعصاب ) بتشنيج العضلات ( فتقرب الأعضاء إلى مباديها كما في قبض اليد ) مثلاً ( وترخيها ) أي ترخي الأعصاب بإرخاء العضلات ( فتبعد الأعضاء عن مبادئها كما في البسط ) أي بسط اليد، وهذه القوة ) المنبتة في العضلات ( هي المبدأ القريب ( للحركة والمبدأ البعيد ) هو ( التصور وبينهما الشوق والإرادة) فهذه مبادئ أربعة مترتبة للأفعال الاختيارية الصادرة عن الحيوان ( فإن النفس تتصور الحركة) أولاً، ( فتشتاق إليها ) ثانياً، بناءً على اعتقاد نفع فيها ( فتريدها ) ثالثاً، (إرادة قصد ) إليها ( وإيجاد لها فتحصل الحركة بتمديد الأعصاب وإرخائها رابعاً، وقال بعضهم: الشوق إنما يوجد فيمن ليس قدرته تامة فتردد وتشتاق وأما الذي يثق بقدرته فلا شوق له . قوله : ( وفائدة العدول ظاهرة ولعل فائدة العدول هي التنبيه على أن تلك القوى كما سميت محركة سميت أيضاً فاعلة والأولى من ذلك أن يقال الفاعلية كما تتناول أيضاً نفيس الأخذ والبطش والقبض والبسط مثلا يترتب على المحركية والمحركية لا تتناول نفس الأخذ والبطش ونحوهما فتكون الفاعلية أشمل من المحركة. وقوله : نزوعة يقال : نزع إلى أهله أي اشتاق . وقوله : بتشنيج العضلات الشنج بفتح الشين المعجمة وسكون النون هو تقبض في الجلد وشنجه تشنيجاً كذا في الصحاح.
(The third type: the active faculties) are those which were previously called motor faculties, meaning that they have an involvement in movement either by moving or by assisting, analogous to what was mentioned regarding the perceptive faculty, and the benefit of the alteration is clear. (And they are divided into) a power (inciting) to movement (and) a power (motor) directly causing movement. (As for the inciting) and it is called desiderative (shawqiyya) and appetitive (nazu'a): it is either for attracting benefit and is called appetitive (shahwiyya) or for repelling harm and is called irascible (ghadabiyya). (As for the motor) it is that which expands the nerves by contracting the muscles and brings the limbs closer to their origins as in closing the hand, for example, and relaxes them, i.e., relaxes the nerves by relaxing the muscles, so it distances the limbs from their origins as in opening, i.e., opening the hand. And this power (embedded in the muscles) is the proximate principle (of movement, while the remote principle) is (conception, and between them is desire and will). So these are four principles arranged for voluntary actions emanating from the animal. (For the soul conceives the movement) firstly, (then desires it) secondly, based on a belief of benefit in it, (then wills it) thirdly, (a will of intention) towards it (and bringing it into existence, so the movement occurs by expansion and relaxation of the nerves) fourthly. And some said: desire occurs only in one whose power is not complete, so he hesitates and desires, but one who is confident in his power has no desire. His words: (And the benefit of the alteration is clear. Perhaps the benefit of the alteration is to indicate that those faculties, just as they are called motor, are also called active, and better than that is to say: activity (al-fa'iliyya) as it also includes the very act of grasping, seizing, clenching, and opening, for example, which follow from motricity, while motricity does not include the very act of grasping, seizing, and the like; so activity is more comprehensive than motricity. And his words: 'nazu'a' is said: nazi'a ila ahlihi, meaning he yearned. And his words: 'by contracting the muscles': al-shanj, with fatha on the shin and sukun on the nun, is a contraction in the skin; and shanajahu tashnijan, thus in al-Sihah.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of faculties: the active faculties (al-quwwa al-fa'ila). The author notes that these were previously called motor faculties (al-muharrika), but he uses 'active' because they have a role in movement either directly or indirectly, and the benefit of the change is later explained. He then divides them into two kinds: (1) the inciting faculty (al-ba'itha), also called the desiderative (shawqiyya) or appetitive (nazu'a), which is further subdivided into the appetitive (shahwiyya) for seeking benefit and the irascible (ghadabiyya) for repelling harm; and (2) the motor faculty (al-muharrika) which directly causes movement by expanding and contracting nerves and muscles, bringing limbs toward or away from their origins, as in closing and opening the hand. The motor faculty is the proximate principle of movement, while the remote principle is conception (tasawwur), with desire (shawq) and will (irada) between them. These four principles—conception, desire, will, and motor execution—are arranged sequentially in voluntary actions: the soul first conceives the movement, then desires it based on a belief of benefit, then wills it with an intention, and finally the movement occurs through nerve expansion and relaxation. A note is added that some say desire only occurs when one's power is incomplete, leading to hesitation and desire, whereas one confident in his power has no desire. The passage concludes with a commentator's gloss: the benefit of calling them 'active' rather than 'motor' is that 'active' (fa'ila) includes the actual acts of grasping, seizing, etc., which follow from motricity, while 'motor' does not; thus 'active' is more comprehensive. Additionally, lexical explanations are given for 'nazu'a' (yearning) and 'shanj' (contraction) with a reference to the dictionary al-Sihah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the motor faculty alone causes movement.
Correction: The passage shows that movement is preceded by conception, desire, and will, which are necessary principles.

Checkpoint

A) Conception (tasawwur)

Key Takeaway
Voluntary action arises from four sequential principles: conception, desire, will, and motor execution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever pause to examine whether your desire is truly for benefit or merely a fleeting impulse? How often do you act without the first principle of conception, simply out of habit or whim? This lesson calls you to be conscious of the sequence of your voluntary actions. Will you commit to pausing before acting to ensure your actions are grounded in true conception and noble desire?
?
The source mentions that some scholars say desire is absent in one who is confident in his power. Do you live with such confidence in Allah's power that you act without hesitation or desire for worldly gain? Or are your actions still driven by restless desire because your reliance is on your own incomplete power? Examine your heart: are your actions marked by trust in Allah or by anxious desire?

The Theoretical and Practical Powers of the Rational Soul
Defining the rational soul and its two powers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the theoretical and practical powers of the rational human soul, their functions, and how they interact in human cognition and action.

Core Idea
The rational soul has two powers: theoretical for universals, practical for particulars.

In this passage, Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a foundational classification of the rational soul's powers. It distinguishes between the theoretical power, which grasps universal truths, and the practical power, which guides particular actions. The text also explains how they are related, how the practical power derives from the theoretical through syllogistic reasoning, and how this process gives rise to human emotions. This framework is essential for understanding Islamic philosophical psychology.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage with translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Don't worry about details yet.
2
Identify the two powers
Locate where the text defines the theoretical power (al-quwwa al-nazariyya) and the practical power (al-quwwa al-‘amaliyya). Notice their respective functions.
The theoretical power deals with universal judgments; the practical power deals with particular actions.
3
Understand their relationship
The text states that the practical power derives from the theoretical. Find the explanation of how this occurs through reflection and syllogism.
The example of the syllogism with a universal major premise and a particular minor premise is key.
4
Note the emotional outcomes
The passage describes how the practical power's judgments lead to emotional states like laughter, shame, and fear. Observe the causal chain.
These emotions are specific to humans because of the rational soul.
5
Reflect on human uniqueness
Consider why these two powers are said to be unique to humans among animals. This ties into the broader theme of human distinctiveness.

The third section: the rational human soul. It is among the three sections in the third chapter which is devoted to explaining the composites that have (in the human soul, i.e., in explaining its powers), and therefore he said: 'and its powers,' meaning those specific to it. It is called the rational power. By virtue of its perception of universals and judgment between them with affirmative or negative relation, it is called the theoretical power and the theoretical intellect. And by virtue of its derivation of intellectual crafts and its practice of opinion and deliberation in particular matters concerning what should be done or left, it is called the practical power and the practical intellect. These are two powers that are distinct either in essence or in consideration, which humans are distinguished by among all animals. The first is for universal judgments, whether true or false, and the second is for judgments concerning particular actions, whether they are goods or evils, beautiful or ugly. This power is derived from the theoretical power, because extracting particular opinions occurs only through a form of reflection and syllogism; there must be a universal premise. For example, one says: this action is such and such, and everything that is such is beautiful (should be done) or ugly (should be left). Then the minor premise of the syllogism is particular and its major is universal, and from them an opinion is obtained about a particular future matter among possible things. For necessary and impossible things are not deliberated about in how to bring them into existence or annihilate them, nor are past and present deliberated about for bringing into existence or annihilating; rather, that is specific to future matters. When this power judges this particular opinion, its judgment is followed by the movement of the social power to move the body, and there occur in it (i.e., the rational human soul) from the practical desiderative power passive states which are followed by bodily conditions, which are laughter (which follows wonder occurring in the soul from perception of strange things whose causes are hidden), and shame, modesty, and their sisters, such as fear, grief, hatred, and other emotions specific to humans. Thus it becomes evident that the soul is affected by its powers just as it affects them. His saying: (The third section among the three sections, etc.) Do not be unaware that the author mentioned in the first observatory of this station on substance two chapters, and mentioned in the second of them five sections, and mentioned in the fourth of them three chapters, and mentioned in the third of them also three sections. So the third section that he mentions here is the third of these three sections, and the fifth section that he mentions after this is the fifth of those five sections. And his saying: 'distinguished by' — the letter bā here enters upon the restricted [term] not the restricting [term]. And his saying: 'are not deliberated' — 't-tarawwī' on the measure of 't-tarqī' meaning reflection.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the third section of the third chapter concerning the rational human soul and its powers. It begins by stating that this section is part of three sections in the chapter that explains composites in the human soul, i.e., its powers. The rational power is named such because it perceives universals and judges between them. It then distinguishes two powers: the theoretical power (al-quwwa al-nazariyya) which deals with universal judgments and is also called the theoretical intellect (al-‘aql al-nazari); and the practical power (al-quwwa al-‘amaliyya) which derives intellectual crafts and engages in deliberation about particular actions. These two powers are distinct either in essence or in consideration and are unique to humans. The theoretical power handles universal judgments whether true or false, while the practical power handles judgments about particular actions whether good or evil. The practical power is derived from the theoretical because extracting particular opinions requires reflection and syllogism involving a universal premise. The example given is a syllogism: this action is such, and everything such is beautiful (should be done) or ugly (should be left). The minor premise is particular, the major is universal, producing an opinion about a future possible matter. Necessary and impossible things are not deliberated, nor past or present. When the practical power judges, the social power moves the body, resulting in emotional states like laughter, shame, modesty, etc. The passage ends with a commentator's note clarifying the book's structure and linguistic points about the word 't-tarawwī'.

Correction: The passage states they are two powers distinct either in essence or in consideration, and the practical power derives from the theoretical, but they are not the same.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's theoretical and practical powers are distinct yet complementary, enabling human cognition and moral action through syllogistic reasoning and giving rise to uniquely human emotions.

The Philosophers' Materialistic View of Bodies
Natural phenomena explained by philosophers' materialistic framework.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation of natural phenomena and bodily differences as purely material processes, denying the role of a freely acting Creator.

Core Idea
Philosophers explain all bodily change through matter and motion, not divine action.

The text begins by describing natural phenomena such as earthquakes and fires as physical processes, then summarizes that these are the views of philosophers who deny the existence of a creator who freely chooses. This lesson examines their materialistic framework and its contrast with theological explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of natural phenomena
Read the Arabic passage describing vapor, earthquakes, and fires. Notice how each phenomenon is explained by natural causes like heat, moisture, and motion.
The details show the philosophers' commitment to natural causation.
2
Identify the summary statement
Find where the text says 'All that we mentioned... are the views of the philosophers.' This ties the natural descriptions to their denial of al-Qādir al-Mukhtār.
3
Analyze the chain of explanation
Trace how philosophers attribute differences in bodies to preparedness in matter, then to forms, then to celestial motions. This is their complete causal chain.
This chain excludes any direct divine intervention.

The First Observatory - Fifth Section: On the mixtures, vapor and smoke approaching oiliness, and they become fire due to the intensity of motion necessitating ignition and transformation into fire. And sometimes the matter strengthens to split the earth, causing tremendous sounds. Then if this split occurs in a town, it makes its high parts low. And sometimes in the place of the split there are depressions, so what is above the earth falls into those depressions little by little, and the earth shakes due to the fall of those mountains upon it with the frequency and intensity of rain. And also we say: there occurs in the earth a sulfurous power, and in the air moisture; the sulfur vapor mixes with the parts of moist air, yielding a temperament, so it becomes oily, i.e., of the nature of oil. And sometimes it ignites by the lights of the planets and others, so at night in that place luminous flames are seen that are not burnt with a fire that matters, due to their subtlety. (Summary) In a comprehensive and sufficient expression: all that we mentioned in the second chapter or in the first observatory are the views of the philosophers, since they denied the Powerful Chooser (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār), as previously indicated in attributing the speech repeatedly. So they attributed the difference of bodies in forms to their preparedness in their matters, which requires the difference of the forms subsisting in them. And they attributed the difference of their effects to their diverse forms and different temperaments. And they attributed all that in elemental bodies and ultimately ascribed it to the motions of the spheres and their configurations. As for the mutakallimūn, they said: bodies are homogeneous in essence, i.e., congruent in reality, because they are composed of indivisible atoms (jawāhir al-afrād), and they are similar with no difference in them. Rather, difference occurs to bodies not in their essences but through what occurs in them of accidents (aʿrāḍ) by the act of the Powerful Chooser. So bodies, in their view, are congruent in reality, differing by matters external to their essences. This is what they have agreed upon, except al-Naẓẓām, for he makes bodies themselves the accidents from which bodies are composed, and the accidents from which the body is composed are different in reality necessarily, so bodies are also likewise, i.e., different in reality. And it has preceded in the second goal from the first chapter of this observatory that there is no escape for one who holds the homogeneity of indivisible atoms from making accidents enter into the reality of the body, and it is based on the fact that bodies are different in reality necessarily, so it would be contradictory to what they have agreed upon of their similarity in reality and their difference by external matters subsisting in them. His statement: 'Of making accidents enter into the reality of the body' — the prepositional phrase is attached to His statement: 'there is no escape' — and you have known that they have an escape from making accidents enter, so then the reality of the body is by making accidents conditions for its distinction from parts entering into its reality. And His statement: 'and it is based etc.' means: making accidents enter into the reality of the body is based etc. For if it is said: here there is that the bodies' being different in reality is based on making accidents enter into the reality of the body, so if making accidents enter into the reality of the body were based on the condition that bodies be different in reality, as you claim, a circle would follow. We say: what is meant by 'making accidents enter into the reality of the body' is the judgment that accidents enter into its reality, not that they actually enter into its reality in itself, so that a circle would follow. For since bodies are necessarily different in reality while indivisible atoms are homogeneous in their view, they are obliged to judge that accidents enter into their realities. And His statement: 'so it would be contradictory to what they have agreed upon etc.' And it is possible to interpret their statement: since they saw that indivisible atoms are the greatest pillar of the body and that accidents are subordinate to them, they made the indivisible atoms the realities of bodies, by way of tolerance in that.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage first describes natural phenomena (vapor, earthquakes) as part of the philosophers' materialistic explanation, then summarizes that all such explanations are the views of philosophers who deny a freely acting Creator (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār). The philosophers attribute differences in bodies to inherent preparedness in matter and ultimately to celestial motions. In contrast, the mutakallimūn hold that bodies are homogeneous in essence, composed of identical indivisible atoms (jawāhir al-afrād), and any difference arises from accidents (aʿrāḍ) produced by God's act. This is the consensus except for al-Naẓẓām, who identifies bodies themselves with the accidents and thus concludes bodies are essentially different. The author then critiques the mutakallimūn's position: the thesis of atomic homogeneity forces them to include accidents in the reality of the body, which contradicts their claim that bodies are similar in essence and differ only by external accidents. The commentary on the phrase 'no escape' clarifies that the mutakallimūn could avoid this by treating accidents as conditions for differentiation rather than constituents. An objection is raised: if bodies must be different in reality for accidents to be essential, and accidents are essential because bodies are different, a circularity results. The answer distinguishes between the judgment (ḥukm) that accidents are essential and actual ontological inclusion. The mutakallimūn, observing that bodies are necessarily different while atoms are homogeneous, are forced to judge that accidents are part of the reality, but this does not mean they are actually essential. Finally, an alternative interpretation is offered: the mutakallimūn loosely called atoms the realities of bodies because atoms are the principal component, with accidents as subordinate.

Key Terms
الأجسام
القادر المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' explanations are compatible with theistic creation.
Correction: The text explicitly states that these are views of philosophers who deny al-Qādir al-Mukhtār, so they exclude any voluntary divine action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do philosophers ultimately attribute all bodily differences to?
A) The direct act of a Creator
B) The motions of the spheres and their configurations
C) The indivisible atoms
D) The accidents subsisting in bodies

Key Takeaway
Philosophers explain all bodily phenomena through an unbroken chain of material causes, denying any role for a freely acting Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a natural event—whether destructive like an earthquake or beautiful like a sunset—does your heart instinctively trace it back to Allah as al-Qādir al-Mukhtār, or do you find yourself satisfied with physical explanations? This lesson exposes the philosophers’ denial of direct divine action. Where in your own life have you been content with material causes, forgetting that every moment is sustained by His free will? Commit today to consciously pause when you observe nature and whisper 'Al-Qādir al-Mukhtār' before any secondary cause enters your mind.
?
The philosophers built an unbroken chain from matter to celestial motions, deliberately excluding any need for divine choice. What 'chains' have you constructed in your own thinking—habits, routines, or systems—that leave Allah out of the picture? For example, do you attribute your success solely to your efforts, or your failures to circumstances? This lesson forces you to examine whether your worldview, like that of the philosophers, has become functionally dismissive of Allah's direct involvement. Write down one specific chain you rely on, and then break it by actively affirming that nothing in it moves except by His will.

The Mutakallimun's Doctrine: Bodies Homogeneous, Differences from Accidents
Mutakallimun's consensus on bodily homogeneity and al-Nazzam's exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's consensus that bodies are essentially homogeneous, composed of identical atoms, and that differences arise from accidents created by God, as well as al-Nazzam's dissenting view.

Core Idea
For mutakallimun, bodies are identical in essence; all variety comes from God's created accidents.

Having presented the philosophers' materialistic view, the text now turns to the mutakallimun's alternative. They argue that bodies are fundamentally alike, being built from indivisible atoms, and any apparent diversity is due to accidents that God brings into existence at each moment. This lesson presents their core doctrine and the significant exception of al-Nazzam.

Guided Path
1
Read the central claim
Read the Arabic statement: 'bodies are homogeneous in essence because they are composed of indivisible atoms.' Understand that this means all bodies share the same essential nature.
This is the foundational claim.
2
Identify the source of difference
Notice that difference is not in the atoms themselves but in the accidents that God creates in them. This explains diversity without compromising essential unity.
3
Examine al-Nazzam's exception
Al-Nazzam rejects homogeneity by identifying bodies with the accidents themselves, making bodies essentially different. Compare his view to the consensus.

المرصد الأول - القسم الخامس : من المركبات البخار والدخان مقرباً إلى الدهنية وقد صارا ناراً لشدة الحركة المقتضية للاشتعال والانقلاب إلى النارية وربما قويت المادة على شق الأرض فتحدث أصوات هائلة، ثم إن وقع هذا الشق في بلدة جعل عاليها سافلها . وربما كان في موضع الانشقاق وهدات فيسقط ما فوق الأرض في تلك الوهدات قليلا ما تتزلزل الأرض بسقوط تلك الجبال عليها بتواتر المطر وشدته ( وأيضاً نقول : ( فيحدث في الأرض قوة كبريتية وفي الهواء رطوبة يختلط بخار الكبريت بأجزاء الهواء الرطب فيفيد مزاجاً فيصير دهنا) أي في طبيعة الدهن ( وربما يشتعل بأنوار الكواكب وبغيرها فيرى بالليل في ذلك الموضع شعل مضيئة غير محترقة احتراقا يعتد به وذلك للطفها . (ملخص) بعبارة جامعة وافية ( ما ذكرناه في الفصل الثاني أو في المرصد الأول ( كله آراء الفلاسفة حيث نفوا القادر المختار) كما سبقت إليه الإشارة في إسناده الكلام مرة بعد أخرى ( فأحالوا اختلاف الأجسام بالصور إلى استعدادها) في موادها يقتضي اختلاف الصور الحالة فيها (و) أحالوا اختلاف) آثارها إلى صورها المتباينة وأمزجتها ) المتخالفة ( و ) أحالوا ( كل ذلك ) في الأجسام العنصرية وأسندوه بالآخرة ( إلى حركات الأفلاك وأوضاعها. وأما المتكلمون فقالوا : الأجسام متجانسة بالذات ( أي متوافقة الحقيقة ( لتركبها من الجواهر الأفراد وإنها متماثلة لا اختلاف فيها وإنما يعرض الاختلاف للأجسام لا في ذواتها بل بما يحصل فيها من الأعراض بفعل القادر المختار) فالأجسام على رأيهم متوافقة في الحقيقة متخالفة بالأمور الخارجية عن ذواتها ( هذا ما قد أجمعوا عليه إلا النظام فإنه يجعل الأجسام نفس الأعراض) الملتئمة منها الأجسام ( والأعراض التي تركبت منها الجسم مختلفة بالحقيقة) قطعاً ( فتكون الأجسام أيضاً (كذلك) أي مختلفة بالحقيقة. وقد سبق في المقصد الثاني من الفصل الأول من هذا المرصد أنه لا محيص لمن يذهب إلى تجانس الجواهر الأفراد من جعل الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم وهو مبني على أن الأجسام متخالفة الحقائق بالضرورة فيكون منافياً لما قد أجمعوا عليه من تماثلها في الحقيقة وتخالفها بالأمور الخارجة الحالة فيها . قوله : (من جعل الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم الجار والمجرور متعلق بقوله : لا محيص وقد عرفت أن لهم محيصاً من جعل الأعراض فحينئذ حقيقة الجسم بأن يجعل الأعراض شروطاً لامتيازه لأجزاء داخلة في حقيقته. وقوله : وهو مبني إلخ أي جعل الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم مبني إلخ . فإن قيل : هاهنا دون فإن كون الأجسام متخالفة الحقائق مبني على جعل الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم فلو كان جعل الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم مبنياً على أن يكون الأجسام متخالفة الحقائق كما زعمتم يلزم الدور. قلنا : المراد بجعل الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم هو الحكم بكون الأعراض داخلة في حقيقته لا كونها داخلة في حقيقته في نفس الأمر حتى يلزم الدور، فإنه لما كانت الأجسام متخالفة الحقائق أن الجواهر الفردة متجانسة عندهم لزمهم أن يحكموا بأن الأعراض داخلة في حقائها. وقوله : فيكون منافياً لما أجمعوا عليه إلخ. ويمكن تأويل كلامهم بأنهم لما رأوا أن الجواهر الفردة هي الركن الأعظم للجسم وأن الأعراض تابعة لها جعلوا الجواهر الفردة حقائق بالضرورة مع الأجسام مسامحة منهم في ذلك .
The First Observatory - Fifth Section: On the mixtures, vapor and smoke approaching oiliness, and they become fire due to the intensity of motion necessitating ignition and transformation into fire. And sometimes the matter strengthens to split the earth, causing tremendous sounds. Then if this split occurs in a town, it makes its high parts low. And sometimes in the place of the split there are depressions, so what is above the earth falls into those depressions little by little, and the earth shakes due to the fall of those mountains upon it with the frequency and intensity of rain. And also we say: there occurs in the earth a sulfurous power, and in the air moisture; the sulfur vapor mixes with the parts of moist air, yielding a temperament, so it becomes oily, i.e., of the nature of oil. And sometimes it ignites by the lights of the planets and others, so at night in that place luminous flames are seen that are not burnt with a fire that matters, due to their subtlety. (Summary) In a comprehensive and sufficient expression: all that we mentioned in the second chapter or in the first observatory are the views of the philosophers, since they denied the Powerful Chooser (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār), as previously indicated in attributing the speech repeatedly. So they attributed the difference of bodies in forms to their preparedness in their matters, which requires the difference of the forms subsisting in them. And they attributed the difference of their effects to their diverse forms and different temperaments. And they attributed all that in elemental bodies and ultimately ascribed it to the motions of the spheres and their configurations. As for the mutakallimūn, they said: bodies are homogeneous in essence, i.e., congruent in reality, because they are composed of indivisible atoms (jawāhir al-afrād), and they are similar with no difference in them. Rather, difference occurs to bodies not in their essences but through what occurs in them of accidents (aʿrāḍ) by the act of the Powerful Chooser. So bodies, in their view, are congruent in reality, differing by matters external to their essences. This is what they have agreed upon, except al-Naẓẓām, for he makes bodies themselves the accidents from which bodies are composed, and the accidents from which the body is composed are different in reality necessarily, so bodies are also likewise, i.e., different in reality. And it has preceded in the second goal from the first chapter of this observatory that there is no escape for one who holds the homogeneity of indivisible atoms from making accidents enter into the reality of the body, and it is based on the fact that bodies are different in reality necessarily, so it would be contradictory to what they have agreed upon of their similarity in reality and their difference by external matters subsisting in them. His statement: 'Of making accidents enter into the reality of the body' — the prepositional phrase is attached to His statement: 'there is no escape' — and you have known that they have an escape from making accidents enter, so then the reality of the body is by making accidents conditions for its distinction from parts entering into its reality. And His statement: 'and it is based etc.' means: making accidents enter into the reality of the body is based etc. For if it is said: here there is that the bodies' being different in reality is based on making accidents enter into the reality of the body, so if making accidents enter into the reality of the body were based on the condition that bodies be different in reality, as you claim, a circle would follow. We say: what is meant by 'making accidents enter into the reality of the body' is the judgment that accidents enter into its reality, not that they actually enter into its reality in itself, so that a circle would follow. For since bodies are necessarily different in reality while indivisible atoms are homogeneous in their view, they are obliged to judge that accidents enter into their realities. And His statement: 'so it would be contradictory to what they have agreed upon etc.' And it is possible to interpret their statement: since they saw that indivisible atoms are the greatest pillar of the body and that accidents are subordinate to them, they made the indivisible atoms the realities of bodies, by way of tolerance in that.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage first describes natural phenomena (vapor, earthquakes) as part of the philosophers' materialistic explanation, then summarizes that all such explanations are the views of philosophers who deny a freely acting Creator (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār). The philosophers attribute differences in bodies to inherent preparedness in matter and ultimately to celestial motions. In contrast, the mutakallimūn hold that bodies are homogeneous in essence, composed of identical indivisible atoms (jawāhir al-afrād), and any difference arises from accidents (aʿrāḍ) produced by God's act. This is the consensus except for al-Naẓẓām, who identifies bodies themselves with the accidents and thus concludes bodies are essentially different. The author then critiques the mutakallimūn's position: the thesis of atomic homogeneity forces them to include accidents in the reality of the body, which contradicts their claim that bodies are similar in essence and differ only by external accidents. The commentary on the phrase 'no escape' clarifies that the mutakallimūn could avoid this by treating accidents as conditions for differentiation rather than constituents. An objection is raised: if bodies must be different in reality for accidents to be essential, and accidents are essential because bodies are different, a circularity results. The answer distinguishes between the judgment (ḥukm) that accidents are essential and actual ontological inclusion. The mutakallimūn, observing that bodies are necessarily different while atoms are homogeneous, are forced to judge that accidents are part of the reality, but this does not mean they are actually essential. Finally, an alternative interpretation is offered: the mutakallimūn loosely called atoms the realities of bodies because atoms are the principal component, with accidents as subordinate.

Key Terms
الأجسام
الجواهر الأفراد
الأعراض
القادر المختار
التجانس
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimun's view implies bodies are identical in all respects.
Correction: They hold that bodies are identical in essence (the atoms), but accidents produce observable differences.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what is the cause of differences between bodies?
A) Differences in the atoms themselves
B) Accidents created by the Powerful Chooser
C) The motions of the spheres
D) The inherent nature of matter

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun maintain that all bodies are essentially identical in their atomic substance; diversity arises solely from God's creation of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at another person, do you see their accidents (skin color, wealth, status) as their essence? How can the mutakallimun's teaching that bodies are essentially identical reshape your view of others and your gratitude to God for the differences He creates?
?
If all accidents are directly created by God, where does that leave your sense of control over your own life? What would it mean to truly see every change in your circumstances as an act of the Most Powerful?
?
The mutakallimun believed differences are from God. Do you live as if your achievements are your own? Take one area where you take credit and consciously attribute it to God.

Atomic Homogeneity and the Necessity of Accidents in the Reality of the Body
Critique of atomic homogeneity and the alternative of accidents as conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique that atomic homogeneity forces including accidents in the reality of the body, and the commentator's solution that accidents can be conditions for differentiation rather than constitutive parts.

Core Idea
The critic argues atoms are homogeneous so differences must be real; the commentator shows they can be conditions, not constituents.

The text now raises a logical difficulty for the mutakallimun's position: if atoms are truly homogeneous, then any real difference between bodies must come from something within the body's reality—namely, accidents. This seems to contradict their claim that bodies are essentially alike. The commentator offers a way out: accidents need not be part of the essence but can serve as conditions for individuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Read the Arabic: 'there is no escape for one who holds the homogeneity of indivisible atoms from making accidents enter into the reality of the body.' Understand the logical force of this claim.
2
Identify the commentator's response
Find the phrase 'they have an escape' and note how the commentator redefines the reality of the body: by making accidents conditions for distinction, not parts of the essence.
This is the key solution.
3
Compare the two views
Reflect on how this solution preserves the mutakallimun's original position while avoiding the critique.

Commentary
This passage first describes natural phenomena (vapor, earthquakes) as part of the philosophers' materialistic explanation, then summarizes that all such explanations are the views of philosophers who deny a freely acting Creator (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār). The philosophers attribute differences in bodies to inherent preparedness in matter and ultimately to celestial motions. In contrast, the mutakallimūn hold that bodies are homogeneous in essence, composed of identical indivisible atoms (jawāhir al-afrād), and any difference arises from accidents (aʿrāḍ) produced by God's act. This is the consensus except for al-Naẓẓām, who identifies bodies themselves with the accidents and thus concludes bodies are essentially different. The author then critiques the mutakallimūn's position: the thesis of atomic homogeneity forces them to include accidents in the reality of the body, which contradicts their claim that bodies are similar in essence and differ only by external accidents. The commentary on the phrase 'no escape' clarifies that the mutakallimūn could avoid this by treating accidents as conditions for differentiation rather than constituents. An objection is raised: if bodies must be different in reality for accidents to be essential, and accidents are essential because bodies are different, a circularity results. The answer distinguishes between the judgment (ḥukm) that accidents are essential and actual ontological inclusion. The mutakallimūn, observing that bodies are necessarily different while atoms are homogeneous, are forced to judge that accidents are part of the reality, but this does not mean they are actually essential. Finally, an alternative interpretation is offered: the mutakallimūn loosely called atoms the realities of bodies because atoms are the principal component, with accidents as subordinate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimun's position is logically untenable because it requires accidents in the essence.
Correction: The commentator shows that accidents can be conditions for distinction, not internal constituents.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how can the mutakallimun avoid making accidents part of the body's reality?
A) By denying that atoms are homogeneous
B) By making accidents conditions for distinction
C) By claiming bodies have no reality
D) By accepting al-Nazzam's view

Key Takeaway
The commentator resolves the tension by interpreting accidents as conditions for differentiation, not as components of the body's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see differences between yourself and others, do you tend to attribute them to fundamental differences in nature, or to external circumstances? How might this lesson's distinction between essence and conditions change your approach to judging others?
?
The critic assumed that accidents must be part of the body's reality. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that external conditions (wealth, status, abilities) define your true worth? What would it mean to see them as conditions rather than constituents of your identity?
?
If atoms are truly homogeneous, then all diversity comes from external accidents. How does this fact make you reflect on the unity of creation and the role of attributes? Are you willing to see others as essentially the same as you, differing only in accidental qualities?

The Circularity Objection and the Distinction Between Judgment and Reality
Circularity objection answered by distinguishing judgment from ontology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection raised against the mutakallimun's view and the answer that distinguishes between the judgment that accidents enter the reality and their actual ontological inclusion.

Core Idea
The alleged circularity is resolved by separating the mental judgment from actual constitution.

A further objection is raised: if the mutakallimun are forced to include accidents in the reality of the body because bodies are different, and yet they claim bodies are different because accidents are included, a vicious circle arises. The answer carefully distinguishes between what the mutakallimun are compelled to judge (ḥukm) and what actually obtains in reality. This lesson unpacks that subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic: 'if it is said: here there is a circle because...' Understand the structure of the objection: it claims mutual dependence.
2
Identify the answer's key move
Find where the answer says 'the meaning of making accidents enter is the judgment (ḥukm) that they enter, not that they actually enter in themselves.' This is the central distinction.
3
Understand why the circle dissolves
Reflect: The mutakallimun observe that bodies are different in reality, and since atoms are homogeneous, they must judge that accidents are part of the reality. This judgment does not make accidents actually essential. No circle.

The First Observatory - Fifth Section: On the mixtures, vapor and smoke approaching oiliness, and they become fire due to the intensity of motion necessitating ignition and transformation into fire. And sometimes the matter strengthens to split the earth, causing tremendous sounds. Then if this split occurs in a town, it makes its high parts low. And sometimes in the place of the split there are depressions, so what is above the earth falls into those depressions little by little, and the earth shakes due to the fall of those mountains upon it with the frequency and intensity of rain. And also we say: there occurs in the earth a sulfurous power, and in the air moisture; the sulfur vapor mixes with the parts of moist air, yielding a temperament, so it becomes oily, i.e., of the nature of oil. And sometimes it ignites by the lights of the planets and others, so at night in that place luminous flames are seen that are not burnt with a fire that matters, due to their subtlety. (Summary) In a comprehensive and sufficient expression: all that we mentioned in the second chapter or in the first observatory are the views of the philosophers, since they denied the Powerful Chooser (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār), as previously indicated in attributing the speech repeatedly. So they attributed the difference of bodies in forms to their preparedness in their matters, which requires the difference of the forms subsisting in them. And they attributed the difference of their effects to their diverse forms and different temperaments. And they attributed all that in elemental bodies and ultimately ascribed it to the motions of the spheres and their configurations. As for the mutakallimūn, they said: bodies are homogeneous in essence, i.e., congruent in reality, because they are composed of indivisible atoms (jawāhir al-afrād), and they are similar with no difference in them. Rather, difference occurs to bodies not in their essences but through what occurs in them of accidents (aʿrāḍ) by the act of the Powerful Chooser. So bodies, in their view, are congruent in reality, differing by matters external to their essences. This is what they have agreed upon, except al-Naẓẓām, for he makes bodies themselves the accidents from which bodies are composed, and the accidents from which the body is composed are different in reality necessarily, so bodies are also likewise, i.e., different in reality. And it has preceded in the second goal from the first chapter of this observatory that there is no escape for one who holds the homogeneity of indivisible atoms from making accidents enter into the reality of the body, and it is based on the fact that bodies are different in reality necessarily, so it would be contradictory to what they have agreed upon of their similarity in reality and their difference by external matters subsisting in them. His statement: 'Of making accidents enter into the reality of the body' — the prepositional phrase is attached to His statement: 'there is no escape' — and you have known that they have an escape from making accidents enter, so then the reality of the body is by making accidents conditions for its distinction from parts entering into its reality. And His statement: 'and it is based etc.' means: making accidents enter into the reality of the body is based etc. For if it is said: here there is that the bodies' being different in reality is based on making accidents enter into the reality of the body, so if making accidents enter into the reality of the body were based on the condition that bodies be different in reality, as you claim, a circle would follow. We say: what is meant by 'making accidents enter into the reality of the body' is the judgment that accidents enter into its reality, not that they actually enter into its reality in itself, so that a circle would follow. For since bodies are necessarily different in reality while indivisible atoms are homogeneous in their view, they are obliged to judge that accidents enter into their realities. And His statement: 'so it would be contradictory to what they have agreed upon etc.' And it is possible to interpret their statement: since they saw that indivisible atoms are the greatest pillar of the body and that accidents are subordinate to them, they made the indivisible atoms the realities of bodies, by way of tolerance in that.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage first describes natural phenomena (vapor, earthquakes) as part of the philosophers' materialistic explanation, then summarizes that all such explanations are the views of philosophers who deny a freely acting Creator (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār). The philosophers attribute differences in bodies to inherent preparedness in matter and ultimately to celestial motions. In contrast, the mutakallimūn hold that bodies are homogeneous in essence, composed of identical indivisible atoms (jawāhir al-afrād), and any difference arises from accidents (aʿrāḍ) produced by God's act. This is the consensus except for al-Naẓẓām, who identifies bodies themselves with the accidents and thus concludes bodies are essentially different. The author then critiques the mutakallimūn's position: the thesis of atomic homogeneity forces them to include accidents in the reality of the body, which contradicts their claim that bodies are similar in essence and differ only by external accidents. The commentary on the phrase 'no escape' clarifies that the mutakallimūn could avoid this by treating accidents as conditions for differentiation rather than constituents. An objection is raised: if bodies must be different in reality for accidents to be essential, and accidents are essential because bodies are different, a circularity results. The answer distinguishes between the judgment (ḥukm) that accidents are essential and actual ontological inclusion. The mutakallimūn, observing that bodies are necessarily different while atoms are homogeneous, are forced to judge that accidents are part of the reality, but this does not mean they are actually essential. Finally, an alternative interpretation is offered: the mutakallimūn loosely called atoms the realities of bodies because atoms are the principal component, with accidents as subordinate.

Key Terms
الدور
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimun's position is logically circular and unsalvageable.
Correction: The commentator shows the circle is only apparent once we distinguish between epistemic judgment and ontological constitution.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the circularity objection?
A) By denying that bodies differ in reality
B) By distinguishing the judgment that accidents enter from actual entry
C) By claiming atoms are heterogeneous
D) By accepting that a circle exists

Key Takeaway
The circularity is avoided by recognizing that the mutakallimun's claim about accidents entering the reality is a judgment based on observed diversity, not an assertion of actual ontological composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just saw that a deep confusion arose from not distinguishing between a judgment (what we say about reality) and reality itself. Where in your own life do you mistake your inferences for the way things actually are? What would change if you paused to separate them?
?
The mutakallimun were forced to their conclusion by their premises. What core beliefs do you hold that might be leading you to conclusions you haven't examined? Are you willing to trace your judgments back to their foundations today?
?
This passage resolves an apparent contradiction by clarifying what was meant by a word. Think of a recent disagreement you had—was it really about the facts, or about the meanings of terms? How might the distinction between judgment and reality have changed that conversation?

Reconciling the Mutakallimun's Position: Atoms as the Substance of Bodies
Reconciliatory interpretation of the mutakallimun's view on atoms and reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a reconciliatory interpretation that the mutakallimun loosely called indivisible atoms the realities of bodies because atoms are the principal component, with accidents as subordinate.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun's talk of atoms as the reality of bodies is a loose way of speaking, prioritizing the principal part.

Finally, the text offers a charitable interpretation of the mutakallimun's position. Recognizing that indivisible atoms are the fundamental building blocks and accidents are merely accidental, the mutakallimun may have used the term 'reality of the body' loosely to refer to the atoms, by way of tolerance. This lesson examines this reconciliatory move.

Guided Path
1
Read the interpretation
Read the Arabic: 'it is possible to interpret their statement... they made the indivisible atoms the realities of bodies, by way of tolerance.' Understand that this is a reconciliatory reading.
2
Identify the reasoning
Note the reasoning: because atoms are the greatest pillar and accidents are subordinate, they speak loosely.
3
Evaluate the interpretation
Consider how this interpretation would defend the mutakallimun against the critique that they included accidents in the reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If a fellow Muslim took a figurative statement of yours literally, would you be patient in clarifying, or would you be offended? What does the willingness to interpret loosely (musamaha) teach you about humility in theological disagreements?

The Fourth Proof: Tatbiq Against Infinite Regress
Al-Iji presents the fourth proof using the method of tatbiq to show the impossibility of infinite regress of motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of tatbiq (application/mapping) demonstrates the impossibility of an infinite regress of motions.

Core Idea
Mapping an infinite series to a shifted version reveals the contradiction of infinity.

This lesson unpacks the fourth proof against infinite regress, known as the proof by tatbiq. It constructs two overlapping series from the assumed infinite chain of motions and shows that assuming infinity leads to a logical contradiction. We'll follow the reasoning step by step to see why infinite regress is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Read the passage and note the assumption: an infinite sequence of motions. Two sets are constructed: one starting from a given motion, the other starting from a motion finitely prior (e.g., ten cycles earlier).
The sets are arbitrary but represent the same infinite series shifted by a finite amount.
2
Follow the two mapping outcomes
The argument maps the two sets element by element. Consider two possibilities: either every element of the excessive set has a counterpart in the deficient set, or some element does not. Walk through each branch and see how it leads to a contradiction or to finiteness.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Both branches force the conclusion that the sets are finite, contradicting the original assumption of infinity. Hence, infinite regress of motions is impossible.
This argument refutes the possibility of an actual infinite regress in causal chains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقريرها هاهنا أن نقول : لو تسلسلت الحركات متعاقبة بلا نهاية كان لنا أن نفرض من حركة (ما كدورة معينة مثلا ) إلى ما لا بداية له جملة ) واحدة (و) نفرض أيضا من ( حركة قبلها بمقدار متناه كعشر دورات مثلاً ( جملة أخرى ثم نطبق الجملتين الجزء الأول ) من إحداهما (بالأول) من الأخرى ) والثاني بالثاني ) وهكذا ( لا إلى نهاية، فإن كان بإزاء كل من أجزاء الجملة الزائدة جزء من أجزاء الجملة الناقصة كان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره فيكون الزائد مساويا للناقص . ( هذا خلف وإلا وجد في أجزاء الزائدة ما كان لا يوجد بإزائه من الناقصة جزء فتنقطع الناقصة ضرورة فتكون متناهية والزائدة إنما تزيد عليها بمتناه والزائد على المتناهي بالمتناهي متناه ) بلا شبهة فتكون الزائدة أيضاً متناهية فيلزم تناهيهما وهو خلاف المفروض). أعني عدم تناهيهما في تلك الجهة فلو كانت الحركات غير متناهية كانت متناهية وما استلزم وجوده عدمه كان محالاً قطعاً. وقد عرفت الكلام عليه) أي على الاستدلال بالتطبيق ) في إبطال التسلسل سؤالاً وجواباً فلا نعيده) دفعاً للإملال.
And its exposition here is that we say: if motions were to succeed each other without end, we could assume from a motion (such as a particular circular motion, for example) to what has no beginning, one set, and we also assume from a motion before it by a finite measure, such as ten cycles, for example, another set. Then we map the two sets: the first part of one of them to the first of the other, and the second to the second, and so on without end. If against every part of the excessive set there is a part of the deficient set, then the thing with its other is like it without its other, so the excessive would be equal to the deficient—this is a contradiction. Otherwise, there would be found in the parts of the excessive what has no part corresponding to it from the deficient, so the deficient would necessarily end, thus being finite, and the excessive only exceeds it by a finite amount, and what exceeds the finite by the finite is finite without doubt, so the excessive would also be finite, thus necessitating their finitude, which is contrary to the assumption—I mean their non-finiteness in that direction. So if motions were infinite, they would be finite, and what necessitates from its existence its non-existence is absolutely impossible. And you have known the discussion about it—i.e., about the inference by application—in the refutation of regress, question and answer, so we do not repeat it to avoid boredom.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a proof by the method of tatbiq (application/mapping) to show that an infinite regress of motions is impossible. The author assumes an infinite series of motions. He constructs two sets: one starting from a given motion (e.g., a particular cycle) and extending backward without beginning, and another set starting from an earlier motion that is finitely prior (e.g., ten cycles earlier). He then maps the two sets element by element. If every element of the excessive set (the one starting later) has a corresponding element in the deficient set, then the sets are equal in number, but the excessive set has more elements (since it includes a finite addition), leading to a contradiction. If there is an element in the excessive set without a match, then the deficient set must end, making it finite. Since the excessive set exceeds it by a finite amount, the excessive set is also finite. Thus both sets are finite, contradicting the assumption of infinity. Therefore, infinite regress of motions is impossible. The author notes that this argument has been discussed elsewhere and he does not repeat it.

Key Terms
تطبيق
جملة زائدة
جملة ناقصة
خلف
متناه
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mapping two infinite series in this way is straightforward and does not produce a contradiction.
Correction: The mapping reveals a contradiction because the excessive set has an extra finite segment, so equating the two sets leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what happens if every part of the excessive set has a corresponding part in the deficient set?
A) Both sets are equal in number, which is possible.
B) The excessive set is equal to the deficient set, which is a contradiction because the excessive set has more elements.
C) The deficient set becomes infinite.
D) The excessive set becomes finite.

Key Takeaway
The tatbiq proof shows that an infinite regress of motions is impossible because any attempt to map an infinite series to a shifted version produces a contradiction, forcing both series to be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an infinite regress of motions is impossible, then your very existence and every action you take must trace back to a beginning. Have you ever genuinely considered that your life is not a random sequence but has a starting point that demands a Creator? What would it mean for your daily decisions if you truly lived as though there is a First Cause behind everything?
?
The tatbiq proof shows that a finite addition to an infinite series leads to contradiction. In your own life, you often assume you have endless time—whether for repentance, good deeds, or personal goals. Is not this assumption itself a form of 'infinite regress' in your mind? What finite step can you take today to break that false sense of endless opportunity?
?
The proof relies on mapping two series and finding that both must be finite. Consider the series of your own blessings: do you ever count them as if they are infinite, failing to acknowledge their finite source? How does the finitude of all things—including your own life—change your attitude of gratitude and urgency to serve your Creator?

The Fifth Proof: Method of Correlation (Tada'uf)
Al-Iji presents the fifth proof using the method of tada'if to show the impossibility of infinite regress of motions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of correlation (tada'uf) demonstrates the impossibility of an infinite regress of motions by requiring equality between the number of preceding and preceded elements.

Core Idea
Correlation demands equality; an infinite series with a last element violates that.

In this proof, al-Iji introduces a powerful logical tool: the correlation between relational opposites. By examining the relationship between 'preceding' and 'preceded' in a series of motions, he shows that an infinite series leads to a numerical imbalance that contradicts the very nature of such correlates. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of tada'uf
Recall that tada'uf (correlation) refers to two relational concepts that necessarily exist together and require mutual balance, like father and son.
This is the key to the proof.
2
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, noticing the terms 'سابق' (preceding) and 'مسبوق' (preceded). The translation will help clarify the logical flow.
3
Follow the logical steps of the proof
Identify the assumption (infinite series), the selection of a last element, and the counting of preceding and preceded instances. Note the conclusion that the numbers are unequal.
The last element is only preceded, not preceding.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the inequality is impossible given the nature of real correlates (mutadā'ifān). Consider how a finite series would restore balance.
This refutes the possibility of an infinite regress of motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الخامس : ) من تلك الوجوه ( طريقة التضايف ) وقد عرفتها أيضا هناك ) وتقريرها هنا أن الحركات تتألف من أجزاء بعضها سابقة وبعضها مسبوقة ولنجعلها أياماً مثلاً فلو كانت تلك الأيام غير متناهية أمكن لنا أن نجعل من يوم ما وهو اليوم الذي نحن فيه جزءاً أخيراً فنقول : هذا الجزء في هذه السلسلة التي لا تتناهى (مسبوق ) أي موصوف بالمسبوقية ) وليس بسابق وكل جزء من أجزائها الأخر سابق ومسبوق بحسب الفرض) إذ المفروض لا تناهي السلسلة فكل واحد من أجزائها الأخر موصوف بالمسبوقية والسابقية معاً إذ لو وجد فيها سابق غير موصوف بالمسبوقية لانقطعت السلسلة به وعلى هذا التقدير فكل سابق مسبوق من غير عكس كلي كالأخير المذكور فيكون عدد المسبوق ) أي المسبوقية ) أزيد من عدد السابق ) أي السابقية بواحد وأنه محال لأنهما متضايفان) حقيقيان ( يجب تكافؤهما في الوجود وتساويهما في العدد وأن يكون بإزاء كل واحد من أحدهما ( واحد ) من الآخر. وأما تساوي عدد المشهوريين فغير لازم كاب واحد له أبناء إلا أن يعتبر التغاير الاعتباري بحسب الوصف، ولو كانت السلسلة متناهية كان هناك سابق ليس بمسبوق فيتكافأ الإضافيان.
The fifth of those ways: the method of correlation (tada'uf), and you have also known it there. Its exposition here: motions consist of parts, some preceding and some preceded. Let us make them days, for example. If those days were infinite, we could take a day, namely the day we are in, as a last part. We say: this part in this endless series is preceded—i.e., described with precededness—and not preceding, while every other part of its parts is preceding and preceded according to the assumption, for the assumption is the non-finiteness of the series. So each one of its other parts is described with precededness and precedingness together, for if there were in it a preceding not described with precededness, the series would be cut off by it. On this estimation, every preceding is preceded, without a universal converse like the aforementioned last one. Thus the number of the preceded—i.e., precededness—is greater than the number of the preceding—i.e., precedingness—by one, and that is impossible because they are two real correlates; they must be equivalent in existence and equal in number, and for each one of the one there must be one from the other. As for the equality of the number of the two knowns, it is not necessary, like a father with sons, unless one considers the conceptual diversity according to description. And if the series were finite, there would be a preceding that is not preceded, so the two correlates would be equivalent.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a proof by the method of tada'if (correlation) against infinite regress. The author considers an infinite series of motions, using days as an example. He selects the present day as the last part in the series. This last part is only preceded (mashuq) and not preceding. All other parts are both preceding and preceded. In an infinite series, every preceding part is also preceded; otherwise the series would terminate. This means the number of 'preceded' instances exceeds the number of 'preceding' instances by at least one (the last element). But 'precededness' and 'precedingness' are real correlates (mutada'ifan) that must be equal in number and existence. Therefore, the series cannot be infinite. If the series were finite, there would be a first element that is preceding but not preceded, restoring the balance. The author also notes that the equality of the number of the two knowns (perhaps referring to the specific instances) is not required in all cases, like a father and sons, but here the correlates are real and require exact correspondence.

Key Terms
تضايف
سابق
مسبوق
إضافيان
تكافؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an infinite series can have a last element without affecting the count of preceding and preceded.
Correction: The last element is purely preceded, creating an imbalance that contradicts the necessary equality of correlates.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, why is an infinite series of motions impossible?
A) The series would have no beginning.
B) The number of preceded elements would exceed the number of preceding elements, violating their correlation.
C) Motions cannot be arranged in a series.
D) The last element would be both preceding and preceded.

Key Takeaway
The method of correlation refutes infinite regress by showing that the relational concepts 'preceding' and 'preceded' must be equal in number, but an infinite series with a last element creates an imbalance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there logical contradictions in your own worldview that you have been ignoring because they are comfortable? What is one belief you hold that, if examined, would force you to change how you live?
?
If you truly accept that an infinite regress is impossible and that your existence depends on a First Cause, what does that mean for how you treat your time and purpose today? Will you act on that conviction or continue as if you are self-sufficient?
?
Imagine standing before the Eternal One tomorrow. Will the logical proof that convinced your mind also transform your heart? What is one concrete step you will take today to let this truth shape your priorities?

Definition and Proof of Impossibility of Interpenetration
Defines interpenetration and proves its impossibility by essence and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of interpenetration of substances and why it is impossible by self-evident reason, not by spatialization.

Core Idea
Interpenetration is impossible by the very essence of substances, not by spatialization.

This passage opens the Fourth Objective on substances, tackling the concept of interpenetration. The author first defines it precisely, then clarifies that its impossibility is not due to spatialization (as the Mu'tazila claimed) but is a self-evident truth inherent in the essences themselves. He then provides a powerful proof by absurdity, showing the catastrophic consequences if interpenetration were allowed.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Notice how interpenetration is defined: one substance entering the locus of another so they become one in place, position, and volume. Understand the three aspects (place, position, magnitude).
2
Identify the correct basis of impossibility
The author rejects the Mu'tazila's explanation (spatialization) and asserts it is by virtue of the essences themselves, known by self-evident necessity. This is a key polemical point.
3
Follow the proof by absurdity
The proof shows that if interpenetration were possible, absurdities follow: a single body could be many bodies, a cubit could be a thousand cubits, the entire world could fit in a mustard seed, etc. This demonstrates the self-evident rejection by reason.
The proof relies on 'clear reason' (sarih al-'aql) which is immediate and does not require syllogism.
4
Note the consensus
The passage adds that all rational people agree on the impossibility, strengthening the argument beyond mere reason.
This consensus is a standard kalam argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : الجواهر وامتناع التداخل عليها ] ( الجواهر يمتنع عليها التداخل أي دخول بعضها في حيز بعض آخر بحيث يتحدان في المكان والوضع ومقدار الحجم وهذا الامتناع ليس معللاً بالتحيز كما ذهب إليه المعتزلة من أن الحيز له باعتبار وجود أحد الجوهرين فيه كون مضاد لكونه باعتبار وجود الآخر فيه بل هو ( لذاتها بالضرورة ) البديهية (إذ لو جاز ذلك) أي تداخل الجواهر (لجاز أن يكون هذا الجسم المعين أجساماً) كثيرة متداخلة (و) جاز أن يكون (الذراع الواحد من الكرباس مثلاً ألف ذراع، بل) جاز ( تداخل العالم كله في حيز خردلة واحدة، وجاز أيضاً أن ينفصل عنها عوالم متعددة مع على هيئتها ( وصريح العقل ) ببداهته ( يأباه ) ، وقد اتفق العقلاء على امتناع التداخل . وأما النظام فقيل : إِنه جوزه، والظاهر أنه لزمه ذلك فيما صار إليه من أن الجسم المتناهي المقدار مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية العدد إذ لا بد حينئذ من وقوع التداخل فيما بينها ( وأما أنه التزمه وقال به صريحاً فلم يعلم كيف؟ وهو جحد للضرورة يرتضيه عاقل لنفسه ، ( وإن صح ) أنه قال به ) كان مكابراً ) لمقتضى عقله .
The Fourth Objective: On Substances and the Impossibility of Interpenetration upon Them. Substances are impossible to interpenetrate, meaning the entry of some into the locus of another such that they become one in place, position, and magnitude. This impossibility is not caused by spatialization (al-tahayyuz) as the Mu'tazila held—that the locus, by virtue of the existence of one substance in it, has a certain state that is opposite to the state it would have by virtue of the existence of another substance—rather it is by virtue of their essences necessarily and self-evidently. For if that were possible, i.e., interpenetration of substances, then it would be possible for this particular body to be many interpenetrating bodies, and it would be possible for a single cubit of cloth, for example, to be a thousand cubits, and indeed for the entire world to interpenetrate into the locus of a single mustard seed, and also for multiple worlds to separate from it while remaining in its original form. And clear reason, by its self-evidence, rejects this. And the rational people have agreed upon the impossibility of interpenetration. As for al-Nazzam, it is said that he permitted it, and apparently it was a necessary consequence of his view that a finite-sized body is composed of an infinite number of parts, for then interpenetration must occur among them. But as for whether he explicitly committed to it and stated it, it is not known. It is a denial of self-evident truth that no rational person would accept for himself. If it is true that he said it, then he was arguing obstinately against the requirement of his own reason.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth objective: the impossibility of interpenetration (tadakhul) of substances. Interpenetration is defined as one substance entering the locus (hayyiz) of another so that they become united in place, position, and magnitude. The author states that this impossibility is not due to spatialization (tahayyuz) as the Mu'tazila claim—who argue that the locus cannot contain two substances because the state of the locus with one substance is opposite to its state with another—but rather the impossibility is inherent in the essences of substances themselves, known by self-evident necessity. The proof is by absurdity: if interpenetration were possible, then this specific body could be many bodies interpenetrating, a cubit of cloth could be a thousand cubits, the entire world could fit into a mustard seed, and from that seed multiple worlds could separate while retaining their form. Clear reason (sarih al-'aql) rejects this immediately. The author adds that all rational people agree on the impossibility. Then the passage discusses al-Nazzam: it is reported that he permitted interpenetration, apparently as a consequence of his view that a finite body is composed of an infinite number of parts (atoms), which would necessitate interpenetration among those parts. However, it is not clear if he explicitly endorsed it; such a view is a denial of self-evident truth that no rational person would admit. If he did say it, he would be obstinately contradicting his own reason.

Key Terms

جواهر
حيز
تحيز
ضرورة
صريح العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the impossibility of interpenetration is caused by spatialization (al-tahayyuz), as the Mu'tazila claimed.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the impossibility is by virtue of the essences of substances themselves, known by self-evident necessity, not by spatialization.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for the impossibility of interpenetration of substances?
A) Spatialization (al-tahayyuz)
B) The self-evident necessity of their essences
C) The fact that two things cannot occupy the same place
D) Consensus of the rational people

Key Takeaway
Interpenetration of substances is impossible by the very essence of substances, proven by self-evident reason and absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that interpenetration is impossible 'by virtue of their essences', you are forced to acknowledge that some truths are so basic that even the cleverest arguments cannot change them. Is there an area of your life where you have convinced yourself that something is possible simply because you haven't thought through the consequences? What would it look like to submit your reasoning to the self-evident truths that your heart already knows?
?
The author uses absurd consequences—a mustard seed containing the whole world—to prove impossibility. Think of a belief or habit you hold that, if followed to its logical end, would lead to an absurdity about who you are or what you worship. Are you willing to let that belief go, starting today?
?
If you truly accept that interpenetration is impossible because clear reason itself rejects it, then you must also accept that your intellect can grasp metaphysical realities beyond the physical. Have you been trusting your reason in matters of faith, or have you been relying only on feelings and traditions? What would it mean to let 'clear reason' guide you in your relationship with Allah, even when it is uncomfortable?
