Al-Nazzam's Alleged View on Interpenetration
Examines al-Nazzam's alleged view on interpenetration and condemns it as irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the controversy surrounding al-Nazzam's view on interpenetration and why it is considered a denial of self-evident truth.

Core Idea
Al-Nazzam's view, if true, is a denial of self-evident reason that no rational person would accept.

After stating the consensus on the impossibility of interpenetration, the author turns to a potential dissenter: al-Nazzam. It is reported that al-Nazzam permitted interpenetration, perhaps as a consequence of his atomic theory. The author critically examines this claim, questioning whether al-Nazzam explicitly endorsed it, and concluding that such a view is a denial of self-evident truth and would constitute obstinacy against reason.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Nazzam's context
Al-Nazzam was a Mu'tazilite theologian who held that a finite body is composed of an infinite number of parts (atoms). This view, as the author notes, forces interpenetration among those parts.
The author mentions that it 'apparently' (الظاهر) follows from his position, not that he explicitly said so.
2
Analyze the author's cautious language
The author uses 'قيل' (it is said) and 'الظاهر' (apparently), indicating uncertainty about al-Nazzam's actual commitment. He refrains from definitive attribution.
The author then asks 'فلم يعلم كيف' (it is not known how), showing he is not sure of the details.
3
Grasp the strong condemnation
The author states that permitting interpenetration is 'جحد للضرورة' (denial of self-evident truth) that no rational person would accept for himself. If al-Nazzam truly said it, he would be 'مكابراً' (obstinately arguing) against his own reason.
This is a very strong rhetorical move, equating the view with irrationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : الجواهر وامتناع التداخل عليها ] ( الجواهر يمتنع عليها التداخل أي دخول بعضها في حيز بعض آخر بحيث يتحدان في المكان والوضع ومقدار الحجم وهذا الامتناع ليس معللاً بالتحيز كما ذهب إليه المعتزلة من أن الحيز له باعتبار وجود أحد الجوهرين فيه كون مضاد لكونه باعتبار وجود الآخر فيه بل هو ( لذاتها بالضرورة ) البديهية (إذ لو جاز ذلك) أي تداخل الجواهر (لجاز أن يكون هذا الجسم المعين أجساماً) كثيرة متداخلة (و) جاز أن يكون (الذراع الواحد من الكرباس مثلاً ألف ذراع، بل) جاز ( تداخل العالم كله في حيز خردلة واحدة، وجاز أيضاً أن ينفصل عنها عوالم متعددة مع على هيئتها ( وصريح العقل ) ببداهته ( يأباه ) ، وقد اتفق العقلاء على امتناع التداخل . وأما النظام فقيل : إِنه جوزه، والظاهر أنه لزمه ذلك فيما صار إليه من أن الجسم المتناهي المقدار مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية العدد إذ لا بد حينئذ من وقوع التداخل فيما بينها ( وأما أنه التزمه وقال به صريحاً فلم يعلم كيف؟ وهو جحد للضرورة يرتضيه عاقل لنفسه ، ( وإن صح ) أنه قال به ) كان مكابراً ) لمقتضى عقله .
The Fourth Objective: On Substances and the Impossibility of Interpenetration upon Them. Substances are impossible to interpenetrate, meaning the entry of some into the locus of another such that they become one in place, position, and magnitude. This impossibility is not caused by spatialization (al-tahayyuz) as the Mu'tazila held—that the locus, by virtue of the existence of one substance in it, has a certain state that is opposite to the state it would have by virtue of the existence of another substance—rather it is by virtue of their essences necessarily and self-evidently. For if that were possible, i.e., interpenetration of substances, then it would be possible for this particular body to be many interpenetrating bodies, and it would be possible for a single cubit of cloth, for example, to be a thousand cubits, and indeed for the entire world to interpenetrate into the locus of a single mustard seed, and also for multiple worlds to separate from it while remaining in its original form. And clear reason, by its self-evidence, rejects this. And the rational people have agreed upon the impossibility of interpenetration. As for al-Nazzam, it is said that he permitted it, and apparently it was a necessary consequence of his view that a finite-sized body is composed of an infinite number of parts, for then interpenetration must occur among them. But as for whether he explicitly committed to it and stated it, it is not known. It is a denial of self-evident truth that no rational person would accept for himself. If it is true that he said it, then he was arguing obstinately against the requirement of his own reason.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth objective: the impossibility of interpenetration (tadakhul) of substances. Interpenetration is defined as one substance entering the locus (hayyiz) of another so that they become united in place, position, and magnitude. The author states that this impossibility is not due to spatialization (tahayyuz) as the Mu'tazila claim—who argue that the locus cannot contain two substances because the state of the locus with one substance is opposite to its state with another—but rather the impossibility is inherent in the essences of substances themselves, known by self-evident necessity. The proof is by absurdity: if interpenetration were possible, then this specific body could be many bodies interpenetrating, a cubit of cloth could be a thousand cubits, the entire world could fit into a mustard seed, and from that seed multiple worlds could separate while retaining their form. Clear reason (sarih al-'aql) rejects this immediately. The author adds that all rational people agree on the impossibility. Then the passage discusses al-Nazzam: it is reported that he permitted interpenetration, apparently as a consequence of his view that a finite body is composed of an infinite number of parts (atoms), which would necessitate interpenetration among those parts. However, it is not clear if he explicitly endorsed it; such a view is a denial of self-evident truth that no rational person would admit. If he did say it, he would be obstinately contradicting his own reason.

Key Terms
النظام
ضرورة
مكابر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might believe that al-Nazzam explicitly and openly endorsed interpenetration of substances.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is not known whether al-Nazzam explicitly stated it; it was only a necessary consequence of his atomic theory, and his actual commitment is uncertain.

Checkpoint

Al-Nazzam's alleged view on interpenetration is condemned as a denial of self-evident reason, though his explicit commitment is uncertain.

?

The Mutual Necessity of Unity of Substance and Its Locus
Proves the mutual necessity and critiques a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the self-evident mutual necessity between the unity of a substance and the unity of its locus, and why the proof offered by some imams is not a valid demonstration.

Core Idea
Unity of substance and unity of locus are mutually necessary and self-evident.

This passage from the Fifth Objective of Sharh al-Mawaqif establishes a key principle in kalam ontology: a single substance cannot occupy two places at one moment, just as two substances cannot occupy one place. The author presents this as self-evident and critiques a proof that attempts to demonstrate it, showing that the proof is not clearer than the conclusion itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note the statement of mutual necessity and the critique.
The passage begins with 'المقصد الخامس'.
2
Identify the self-evident claim
Notice that the author says the impossibility of one substance in two loci is 'also necessary (daruri), like the first.' This means it is immediately known without proof.
The first rule (two substances not in one locus) was established in the previous objective.
3
Examine the proof from some imams
Read the proof: if a single substance could be in two loci, we could not be certain which body is which, and there would be no difference between one body and two bodies. Then note the author's critique.
The proof is introduced with 'وقال بعض الأئمة'.
4
Understand the critique
The author says the proof is merely a reminder (tanbih) that pictures the idea, not a valid inference. Reflect on why this critique matters in kalam methodology.
The conclusion is self-evident, so any proof that is not clearer than it is circular or weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : تلازم وحدة الجوهر ووحدة حيزه ] مر ( وحدة الجوهر ووحدة حيزه متلازمتان، فكما لا يجوز كون جوهرين في حال كما واحد في حيز واحد) آنفاً فلا يجوز ) أيضاً (كون الجوهر الواحد في آن واحد في حيزين وهذا (ضروري) أيضاً كالأول . ( وقال بعض الأئمة في إثباته لو جاز ذلك لم يكن لنا ( الجزم بأن الجسم الحاصل في هذا الحيز غير ) الجسم ( الحاصل في الحيز الآخر، وأيضاً فلا يبقى فرق بين الجسم الواحد والجسمين ولعل ذلك) الذي أورده في إثباته ) تنبيه على الضرورة بعبارات ( مختلفة ( تصور المطلوب في الذهن تصويراً واضحاً ( فإن شيئاً من ذلك الذي جعله دليلاً (ليس بأوضح من المطلوب) فكيف يصح الاستدلال به
The Fifth Objective: The Mutual Necessity of the Unity of the Substance and the Unity of its Locus. The unity of the substance and the unity of its locus are mutually necessary. So just as it is not permissible for two substances at one moment to be in a single locus, as stated above, so also it is not permissible for a single substance at one moment to be in two loci. This is also necessary, like the first. Some imams said in proving it: if that were possible, we could not be certain that the body present in this locus is different from the body present in the other locus, and also there would remain no difference between a single body and two bodies. Perhaps what he adduced in proving it is a reminder of the necessity through different expressions that picture the required idea in the mind clearly, but none of what he used as a proof is clearer than the required idea itself, so how can it be valid to use it as an inference?
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth objective: the mutual necessity between the unity of a substance and the unity of its locus. It states that just as two substances cannot occupy one locus at the same moment (as established in the previous objective), likewise one substance cannot occupy two different loci at the same moment. This is presented as self-evident and necessary. The author then reports a proof offered by some imams: if a single substance could be in two loci, we would not be able to be certain that the body in this locus is distinct from the body in that locus, and there would be no difference between one body and two bodies. However, the author criticizes this proof: it is not clearer than the conclusion it seeks to prove, so it is not a valid inference. Rather, the proof is merely a reminder (tanbih) that uses different expressions to picture the idea clearly in the mind, but it does not actually demonstrate the conclusion, since the conclusion is already self-evident.

Key Terms

ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof offered by some imams is a valid logical demonstration that the impossibility of one substance in two loci requires proof.
Correction: The author clarifies that the claim is self-evident, and the proof is merely a clarifying remark (tanbih), not a stronger argument.

Checkpoint

A) It is a sound logical proof
B) It is stronger than the required idea
C) It is not clearer than the required idea itself
D) It is an invalid argument based on flawed premises

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a single substance occupying two loci is self-evident, and attempts to prove it with weaker arguments are not valid inferences.

The Term 'Opposite': A Verbal Dispute in Kalam
Author discusses terminological dispute on calling substances opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the disagreement over whether two substances can be called opposites is purely terminological, not doctrinal, and depends on how one defines the conditions for opposition.

Core Idea
The dispute about calling substances opposites is about words, not reality.

This lesson examines a note (tanbih) by the author that addresses a seemingly divisive issue among theologians: can two substances, like two bodies, be called opposites? As the author clarifies, the disagreement hinges entirely on the definition of the term 'opposite' itself. By understanding this, we learn how careful terminological precision prevents unnecessary doctrinal disputes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its English translation. Notice that the author begins with a note (tanbih) and presents two examples: a theological dispute and a philosophical dispute.
The passage is dense but the structure is clear: 'تنبيه' introduces the topic.
2
Identify the two disputes
The passage presents two separate but analogous disputes: (1) among theologians about whether two substances are opposites, and (2) among philosophers about whether specific forms (e.g., fieriness and wateriness) are opposites. Identify the positions of al-Qadi and Abu Ishaq, and the philosophers.
Al-Qadi forbade calling substances opposites; Abu Ishaq allowed it.
3
Understand the author's framing
Notice that the author says both disputes are 'verbal' (lafzi) and 'return to mere convention' (اصطلاح). He emphasizes that one may define the term 'opposite' as one wishes—there is no restriction.
The author uses 'بحث لفظي' to indicate the dispute is about words, not reality.
4
Analyze the conditions for opposition
The key conditions mentioned are: (a) succession upon a subject (mawdu') and (b) succession upon a locus (mahall). Understand how these conditions affect whether substances or specific forms can be called opposites.
Specific forms have no subject, so if subject is required, they are not opposites; if locus suffices, they are.
5
Appreciate the lesson's relevance
Reflect on how this passage teaches that many theological disagreements are merely terminological. It shows the importance of defining terms clearly before engaging in debate.
This is a recurring theme in Islamic theology—many disputes dissolve once terminology is clarified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : ( هل يسمى الجسمان باعتبار امتناع اجتماعهما في حيز واحد ( ضدين كما يسمى العرضان باعتبار امتناع اجتماعهما في محل واحد (ضدين). كما عرفت ( فيه خلاف بین المتكلمين) فمنع القاضي من إطلاق اسم الضد على الجواهر فكأنه راعى في التضاد تعاقب الضدين على المحل المقوم وذلك غير متصور في الجواهر بخلاف الأعراض، وجوزه الاستاذ أبو إسحاق (وهو) بحث ( لفظي عائد إلى مجرد (الاصطلاح في إطلاق الألفاظ ( ولكل أن يصطلح في لفظ الضدين على ما يشاء ) من المعاني إذ لا حجر في ذلك . ( واعلم أن للحكماء خلافاً قريباً منه في الصور النوعية كالنارية والمائية هل هما ضدان أم لا فقال بعضهم: نعم. وقال آخرون : لا ( وهو أيضاً ) بحث (لفظي مرجعه إلى اشتراط توارد الضدين على موضوع أو محل فإن شرط توارد هما على موضوع لم يكونا ضدين) إذ لا موضوع لهما ( وإن اكتفى بالمحل ) الذي هو أعم من الموضوع ( فهما ضدان ) لتواردهما على المادة العنصرية . ( والاصطلاح المشهور على الأول ) .
A note: Whether two bodies, in virtue of the impossibility of their mutual occurrence in a single locus, are called opposites (diddayn), just as two accidents, in virtue of the impossibility of their mutual occurrence in a single substrate (mahall), are called opposites. As you know, there is a disagreement among the theologians. Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) forbade applying the term 'opposite' to substances, as if he considered in opposition (tadadd) the succession of the two opposites upon the constitutive substrate (al-mahall al-muqawwim), and that is inconceivable in substances unlike accidents. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq (al-Isfarayini) permitted it. It is a verbal discussion, returning to mere convention in the application of terms. Anyone may conventionally define the term 'two opposites' as he wishes, for there is no restriction in that. Know that the philosophers have a similar disagreement regarding specific forms (al-suwar al-naw'iyya) like fieriness and wateriness: are they opposites or not? Some said yes, others said no. This too is a verbal discussion, whose reference is the condition of the succession of the two opposites upon a subject (mawdu') or a locus (mahall). If one conditions their succession upon a subject, they are not opposites—since they have no subject. But if one is satisfied with the locus, which is more general than the subject, then they are opposites, due to their succession upon material substance. The prevailing convention is the first view.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a 'tanbih' (note) addressing a terminological issue: whether two bodies are called opposites (diddayn) because they cannot coexist in one locus, similar to how two accidents are called opposites because they cannot coexist in one substrate. The author notes a disagreement among theologians: al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) forbade calling substances opposites, because he defined opposition as requiring succession upon a constitutive substrate (mahall muqawwim), which applies to accidents but not to substances. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq (al-Isfarayini) permitted it. The author clarifies that this is merely a verbal (lafzi) dispute about terminology; there is no restriction on how one wishes to define the term 'opposite'. Then the author extends the comparison to the philosophers' dispute about specific forms (e.g., the form of fire vs. water): are they opposites? Again, it depends on whether one conditions succession upon a subject (mawdu')—if so, they are not opposites because specific forms have no subject; but if one conditions it upon a locus (mahall), which is broader, then they are opposites because they succeed each other on material substance. The prevailing convention, the author notes, is the first view (i.e., not considering them opposites). The entire discussion is terminological.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author explicitly states it is a 'verbal discussion' (بحث لفظي) and a matter of convention (اصطلاح); there is no restriction in how one defines the term.

Checkpoint

B) Because he defined opposition as requiring succession upon a constitutive substrate, which substances do not have
C) Because he followed the philosophers in this matter
D) Because the term 'opposite' applies only to accidents

Key Takeaway
The classification of opposites in kalam is a matter of terminological convention, not of objective reality.

The Ash'ari Consensus on Accidents and Their Opposites
States the Ash'ari consensus on the impossibility of a body being free from an accident and its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that a body must always possess either an accident or its opposite, and the dissenting view of some Dahriyya.

Core Idea
A body cannot be empty of accidents; it always has one of every opposite pair.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Ash'ari kalam: the impossibility of a body being devoid of accidents. The Ash'aris argue that for every accident and its opposite, one must be present in the body. We will also see a contrasting view from the Dahriyya, who claimed substances were originally empty in pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the key terms: khalw (emptiness), 'arad (accident), didd (opposite).
2
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Find the sentence stating the Ash'ari consensus: 'كل عرض مع ضده يجب أن يوجد أحدهما في الجسم'. Understand the rule: for each pair of opposites, one must exist.
3
Examine the Dahriyya counterview
The passage mentions that some Dahriyya allowed emptiness in pre-eternity. Consider what this implies about their conception of substances before accidents.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is this principle important? It establishes that bodies are never independent of accidents, which connects to arguments about the contingency of the world and the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : في خلو الجسم عن العرض وضده ] ( الجسم هل يخلو عن العرض وضده اتفق المتكلمون ) من الأشاعرة ) على منعه ) . وقالوا : كل عرض مع ضده يجب أن يوجد أحدهما في الجسم ( وجوزه بعض الدهرية في الأزل)، وقالوا إن الجواهر كانت خالية في الأزل عن جميع أجناس
The Sixth Objective: On the Emptiness of a Body from an Accident and Its Opposite. The theologians—from the Ash'aris—have agreed upon its prohibition. They said: For every accident and its opposite, one of them must exist in the body. Some of the Dahriyya (eternalists) permitted it in pre-eternity, and they said that substances were empty in pre-eternity of all genera [of accidents].
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage begins the sixth objective, which asks whether a body can be free (khalw) from an accident (al-'arad) and its opposite (diddahu). The answer: the theologians, specifically the Ash'aris, unanimously agree on its impossibility. Their ruling is that for every accident and its opposite, one of them must necessarily exist in the body. For example, a body must either be moving or at rest, colored or not colored, etc. The passage then mentions a contrary view held by some of the Dahriyya (eternalists), who permitted this emptiness in pre-eternity (al-azal). They claimed that substances (jawahir) were originally empty of all genera of accidents. The passage is cut off here, but it sets up the Ash'ari position against the Dahriyya. The Ash'ari view is that there is no moment when a body is devoid of all accidents; rather, it always has some accident or its opposite, by divine creation.

أشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris believe a body must always have every accident simultaneously.
Correction: The Ash'aris believe that for each accident and its opposite, one of the two must be present; not that all accident types are present at once.

Checkpoint

C) It is possible only if God wills
D) It is a matter of verbal dispute

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari consensus is that a body must always have either an accident or its opposite; it can never be empty of both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Mutual Necessity of a Substance's Unity and Its Location
Al-Jurjani states the mutual necessity of a substance's unity and its location's unity and discusses a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the unity of a substance and the unity of its location are mutually necessary, and how the author distinguishes between intuitive necessity and attempted proof.

Core Idea
A substance cannot be in two places at once, and two substances cannot share one place.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a fundamental principle in kalam physics: the mutual dependence between a substance's numerical unity and the unity of its spatial location. The author presents this as intuitively necessary, then examines a proof offered by some scholars, ultimately showing that the proof merely clarifies the intuition rather than providing a logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the core statement: the unity of a substance and the unity of its location are mutually necessary.
2
Identify the two components
Note that the mutual necessity has two sides: (1) two substances cannot occupy one location, and (2) one substance cannot occupy two locations at the same time.
The first was mentioned earlier ('آنفاً').
3
Examine the proof and critique
Read the proof offered by some Imams and the author's response. The author says the proof is not a logical deduction but a reminder of the self-evident truth.
The author argues that the proof's premise is no clearer than the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس: تلازم وحدة الجوهر ووحدة حيزه. وحدة الجوهر ووحدة حيزه متلازمتان، فكما لا يجوز كون جوهرين في حال كما واحد في حيز واحد آنفاً فلا يجوز أيضاً كون الجوهر الواحد في آن واحد في حيزين وهذا ضروري أيضاً كالأول. وقال بعض الأئمة في إثباته لو جاز ذلك لم يكن لنا الجزم بأن الجسم الحاصل في هذا الحيز غير الجسم الحاصل في الحيز الآخر، وأيضاً فلا يبقى فرق بين الجسم الواحد والجسمين ولعل ذلك الذي أورده في إثباته تنبيه على الضرورة بعبارات مختلفة تصور المطلوب في الذهن تصويراً واضحاً فإن شيئاً من ذلك الذي جعله دليلاً ليس بأوضح من المطلوب فكيف يصح الاستدلال به
The Fifth Objective: The mutual necessity of a substance's unity and the unity of its location. The unity of a substance and the unity of its location are mutually necessary. Just as it is not permissible for two substances to be in a single location at one moment, as previously mentioned, so also it is not permissible for one substance to be in two locations at one moment. And this is also necessary like the first. And some Imams said in proving it: If that were permissible, we would not be certain that the body occurring in this location is other than the body occurring in another location, and also there would be no difference between one body and two bodies. And perhaps what he adduced in proving it is a reminder of the necessity by different expressions, to depict the desired in the mind clearly, for nothing of that which he made a proof is clearer than the desired; so how can deduction by it be valid?
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the mutual necessity between a substance's unity and the unity of its location. It begins by stating the principle: the two are inseparable. It then gives the intuitive example: just as two substances cannot occupy one location at one time, one substance cannot be in two locations at one time. This is presented as self-evident. The passage then reports a proof from some Imams: if a substance could be in two locations, we could not distinguish bodies, and there would be no difference between one body and two. However, the author notes that this proof is actually a reminder rather than a logical deduction, because the conclusion is no clearer than the premise. Thus, the argument serves to clarify what is already intuitive.

Key Terms
جوهر
حيز
متلازمتان
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof offered by the Imams is a logical demonstration that proves the principle of mutual necessity.
Correction: The author clarifies that the proof is only a reminder (تنبيه) of what is already self-evident, not a valid deduction, since its premises are no clearer than the conclusion it tries to prove.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between a substance's unity and the unity of its location?
A) They are independent
B) They are mutually necessary
C) They are mutually exclusive
D) They are unrelated

Key Takeaway
The unity of a substance and its location are mutually necessary by intuitive necessity, and attempts to prove it are only reminders of that necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on religious arguments that, upon honest reflection, are no clearer than the conclusion they claim to prove? How can you, like al-Jurjani, train yourself to distinguish self-evident truths from weak proofs?
?
Consider your belief in the oneness of Allah (tawhid). Are there aspects of it that you accept only because of arguments you've heard, or do you see it as immediately necessary to reason? What would it mean for your faith if you could grasp it as a 'ḍarūrī' truth?
?
When you think about the fact that you cannot be in two places at once, do you sense the hand of the Creator in establishing this order? How does this simple necessity call you to gratitude and submission to His wisdom?

Are Two Bodies Called Opposites? A Lexical Dispute
Al-Jurjani discusses lexical disputes on calling bodies opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question of whether two bodies can be called 'opposites' is a matter of lexical convention, not doctrinal disagreement.

Core Idea
Disputes about calling bodies opposites are about word definitions, not theology.

Al-Jurjani presents a seemingly technical question: can two bodies be called opposites, just as two accidents are called opposites? He immediately clarifies that this is not a doctrinal dispute but a lexical one. The disagreement among theologians and philosophers boils down to how one defines the term 'opposite.' This lesson will walk through the two main positions and their reasoning, showing that each scholar is free to stipulate his own definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two main examples: bodies vs. accidents, and specific forms like fire and water.
Pay attention to how al-Jurjani frames the issue as a 'note' (تنبيه).
2
Identify the two disputes
The passage contains two parallel disputes: one among theologians (al-Qadi vs. Abu Ishaq) about bodies, and one among philosophers about specific forms. Identify the reasoning for each side.
3
Understand the key condition: succession on a substrate
Al-Qadi required that opposites succeed each other on a 'constitutive substrate' (محل مقوم). This applies to accidents but not to bodies. Abu Ishaq allowed the term more broadly. Similarly, philosophers disagreed on whether a 'subject' or merely a 'substrate' is needed.
The difference between 'subject' (موضوع) and 'substrate' (محل) is crucial in the philosophers' discussion.
4
Recognise the lexical nature of the dispute
Al-Jurjani explicitly says it is a 'lexical discussion' (بحث لفظي) and a matter of convention (اصطلاح). He concludes that anyone may define 'opposites' as they wish.
This underscores the broader principle: not every disagreement among scholars is doctrinal; some are merely terminological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: هل يسمى الجسمان باعتبار امتناع اجتماعهما في حيز واحد ضدين كما يسمى العرضان باعتبار امتناع اجتماعهما في محل واحد ضدين. كما عرفت فيه خلاف بين المتكلمين: فمنع القاضي من إطلاق اسم الضد على الجواهر فكأنه راعى في التضاد تعاقب الضدين على المحل المقوم وذلك غير متصور في الجواهر بخلاف الأعراض، وجوزه الاستاذ أبو إسحاق وهو بحث لفظي عائد إلى مجرد الاصطلاح في إطلاق الألفاظ ولكل أن يصطلح في لفظ الضدين على ما يشاء من المعاني إذ لا حجر في ذلك. واعلم أن للحكماء خلافاً قريباً منه في الصور النوعية كالنارية والمائية هل هما ضدان أم لا فقال بعضهم: نعم. وقال آخرون: لا وهو أيضاً بحث لفظي مرجعه إلى اشتراط توارد الضدين على موضوع أو محل فإن شرط توارد هما على موضوع لم يكونا ضدين إذ لا موضوع لهما وإن اكتفى بالمحل الذي هو أعم من الموضوع فهما ضدان لتواردهما على المادة العنصرية. والاصطلاح المشهور على الأول.
Note: Are two bodies called opposites on account of the impossibility of their being together in one location, just as two accidents are called opposites on account of the impossibility of their being together in one substrate? As you know, there is disagreement among the theologians on this: al-Qadi forbade applying the term 'opposite' to substances, as if he considered in opposition the succession of the two opposites upon the constitutive substrate, and that is not conceivable in substances unlike accidents. And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq permitted it. And it is a lexical discussion returning to mere convention in the application of words, and everyone may stipulate in the word 'opposites' whatever meaning he wishes, for there is no restriction in that. And know that the philosophers have a similar disagreement regarding specific forms like fire-ness and water-ness: are they opposites or not? Some said yes, others said no. And it is also a lexical discussion referring to the condition of the succession of the two opposites upon a subject or a substrate. If the succession of the two upon a subject is required, they are not opposites since they have no subject; but if one is satisfied with the substrate, which is more general than the subject, they are opposites because of their succession upon the material element. And the well-known convention is upon the first.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a lexical note on whether two bodies can be called 'opposites' (diddan). It begins by raising the question: just as two accidents are called opposites because they cannot coexist in the same substrate, can two substances be called opposites because they cannot be in the same location? Al-Jurjani reports that al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar forbade this usage, since true opposition involves succession on a constitutive substrate (mahall muqawwim), which applies to accidents but not to substances. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini permitted it. The author characterizes this dispute as purely lexical, based on arbitrary convention. He then extends the discussion to philosophers' disagreement over whether specific forms (like fire and water) are opposites, again a lexical matter depending on whether one requires a subject or a substrate.

Key Terms
ضدين
جوهر
عرض
محل
موضوع
اصطلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement about calling bodies opposites is a doctrinal dispute with implications for theology.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies it is a lexical discussion (بحث لفظي) based on convention (اصطلاح), not a matter of correct belief.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Qadi forbid calling two bodies 'opposites'?
A) Because bodies are always together
B) Because opposition requires succession on a constitutive substrate, which bodies lack
C) Because bodies are eternal
D) Because he followed a different school

Key Takeaway
The question of whether bodies or specific forms are opposites is a matter of lexical convention, not theological or philosophical doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a theological dispute about opposites is merely lexical. Think of a disagreement you have with a fellow Muslim about a matter of belief. Could it be that you are using different definitions of a key term? Will you now take the first step to clarify definitions before defending your position?
?
The philosophers debated whether fire-ness and water-ness are opposites, concluding it depends on whether you require a subject or a substrate. This abstract point mirrors a deeper truth: your definitions shape your worldview. What are the unexamined definitions in your own understanding of good and evil, right and wrong? Will you examine them today?
?
Al-Jurjani says 'there is no restriction' in defining terms. How often do you hold rigidly to a theological stance, forgetting that it might be based on a mere convention? This lesson humbles you: even great scholars disagreed on word meanings. Are you willing to admit that some of your certainties might be terminological rather than doctrinal?

The Ash'ari Argument from Homogeneity
Al-Jurjani presents the Ash'ari argument that bodies cannot be free from accidents because they are distinguished only by accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that bodies cannot exist without accidents because their individuality depends entirely on accidents.

Core Idea
Bodies are blank slates; only accidents give them identity.

This lesson introduces a foundational argument in Ash'ari theology. The Ash'aris argue that bodies are fundamentally alike, composed of identical atoms. What distinguishes one body from another are the accidents that inhere in it. Without those accidents, a body would be a mere abstraction with no real existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the premises
Look for the key premises: homogeneity of bodies (they are all composed of identical substances) and that distinction comes only from accidents.
3
Understand the conclusion
Without accidents, a body becomes an 'absolute body' (jism mutlaq) which has no independent existence. Grasp why the Ash'aris hold this as a necessary truth.
4
Reflect on the implication
This argument proves that accidents are necessary for any particular body to exist. Ask yourself: what would happen if a body were completely accident-free?
The answer: it would be an unreal abstraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتكلمون أي الأشاعرة فمنعهم منه بناء على أن الأجسام متجانسة عندهم لتركبها من الجواهر الأفراد المتماثلة، وإنما تتميز الأجسام بعضها عن بعض بالأعراض الحالة فيها فلو خلا الجسم عنها بأسرها لم يكن ذلك الجسم شيئاً من الأجسام المخصوصة المتميزة عن غيرها بل كان جسماً مطلقاً غير مخصوص معين والمطلق لا وجود له بالاستقلال ضرورة.
And as for the theologians, i.e., the Ash'aris, they prohibited it based on the fact that bodies are homogeneous according to them, because they are composed of individual similar substances, and bodies are only distinguished from each other by the accidents inhering in them. So if the body were devoid of all of them, that body would not be any particular body distinct from others; rather, it would be an absolute body, not particular and determined, and the absolute does not exist independently, necessarily.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the core Ash'ari argument against the possibility of a body being free of all accidents. It begins by stating the premise: bodies are homogeneous (mutajanisa) because they are all composed of identical individual substances (jawahir afrad mutamathila). Therefore, the only thing that distinguishes one body from another is the accidents that inhere in it. If a body were devoid of all accidents, it would not be any specific, determinate body; it would be a mere 'absolute body' (jism mutlaq), which has no independent existence in the external world. The argument thus concludes that a body cannot exist without any accidents, for it would be an unreal abstraction.

Key Terms
تجانس
جواهر أفراد
تماثل
عرض
جسم مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies are inherently distinct and can exist without accidents.
Correction: The Ash'ari argument shows that distinction and existence depend on accidents; without them, a body is an unreal abstraction.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument, why must a body always have accidents?
A) Because accidents are eternal
B) Because bodies are homogeneous and only accidents give them distinction
C) Because the Qur'an says so
D) Because bodies are composed of accidents

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument concludes that no body can exist without accidents because its individuality and existence depend on them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If my identity is not inherent but dependent on accidents (characteristics), then what is my essence? The lesson says a body without accidents is nothing particular. In what ways do I imagine myself as a fixed, independent self, when in reality I am defined by temporary attributes? How would my relationship with change and loss be transformed if I truly believed that I am a 'bundle of accidents'? Commit to one specific way you will stop clinging to a particular label or characteristic and instead acknowledge that your existence is sustained moment by moment.
?
The Ash'ari argue that an absolute body has no independent existence. Think about your own life: in what areas do you assume you have an independent, unchanging core that exists apart from Allah's creation? Where do you behave as if you are a 'jism mutlaq'? What would it mean to live as a creature that exists only through specific attributes given by Allah? Set a daily reminder for one week to recite 'Ya Hayyu Ya Qayyum' and reflect on your dependence.
?
The passage shows that distinction comes only from accidents. Consider your self-worth: does it come from inherent value or from accidental attributes like wealth, status, or appearance? If those accidents were removed, would you still be 'somebody'? This lesson challenges you to root your worth in your relationship with Allah, not in changing attributes. Identify one accidental attribute you overly rely on for identity, and make a plan to detach from it.

Objection to the Ash'ari Argument and al-Nizam's Agreement
Al-Jurjani gives a potential objection to the Ash'ari argument and notes that al-Nizam agrees with the conclusion despite different premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to the Ash'ari argument for the impossibility of a body being free from accidents, and how al-Nizam reaches the same conclusion from a different doctrinal basis.

Core Idea
A body may be free from all accidents if distinction is by only some, and al-Nizam agrees but via his own premise.

Al-Jurjani now presents a critical objection to the Ash'ari argument just given. The objection suggests that bodies might be distinguished by only some accidents, not all, so the conclusion that a body cannot be free of accidents and their opposites does not necessarily follow. He then notes that al-Nizam, a prominent early theologian, actually agrees with the Ash'aris on the impossibility of emptiness, but for a different reason: because he holds that bodies are composed of accidents. This shows how different premises can lead to the same theological conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the two main parts: the objection and the note on al-Nizam.
The objection begins with 'ويرد على هذا الاستدلال'.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objector argues that if bodies are distinguished by only some accidents, then it does not follow that a body cannot be free of all accidents and their opposites simultaneously.
This challenges the Ash'ari assumption that distinction requires all accidents.
3
Examine al-Nizam's agreement
Al-Nizam agrees on the impossibility of emptiness, but his reasoning is different: he believes the body itself is composed of accidents, so it cannot be free of them.
The phrase 'بناء على ما مر من مذهبه' points to his earlier doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد على هذا الاستدلال أنه ربما كان الامتياز ببعض الأعراض فلا يلزم أن الجسم لا يخلو عن شيء من الأعراض وضده معاً. وموافقة النظام في ذلك أي في امتناع الخلو لهم أي للمتكلمين أمر ظاهر يعني أنه وإن خالفهم في تماثل الأجسام لكنه يوافقهم في امتناع خلوها عن الأعراض بناء على ما مر من مذهبه في تركب الجسم من العرض وذلك ظاهر لاسترة به.
And there is an objection to this argument: it is possible that distinction [between bodies] is by some accidents, so it does not entail that the body is not devoid of any accident and its opposite altogether. And al-Nizam's agreement in that—i.e., in the impossibility of emptiness—for them, i.e., for the theologians, is an apparent matter, meaning that although he disagreed with them regarding the similarity of bodies, he agrees with them regarding the impossibility of their being devoid of accidents, based on his previously mentioned doctrine that the body is composed of accident, and that is apparent for [؟].
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the Ash'ari argument just given. The objector says: the argument assumes that bodies are distinguished only by all accidents, but perhaps only some accidents are sufficient for distinction. Therefore, it does not prove that a body cannot be free of any accident and its opposite simultaneously. The author then notes that al-Nizam (a well-known early mutakallim) actually agrees with the Ash'aris that a body cannot be free from accidents, but for a different reason: because he holds that the body itself is composed of accidents. Thus, despite differing on the issue of homogeneity, al-Nizam reaches the same conclusion, which the author finds apparent.

Key Terms
امتياز
أعراض
النظام
تماثل
تركيب الجسم من العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All theologians who deny the possibility of emptiness use the same argument.
Correction: Al-Nizam uses a different premise: the body is composed of accidents, leading to the same conclusion.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nizam agree that a body cannot be free from accidents, according to the passage?
A) Because he believes bodies are homogeneous
B) Because he believes bodies are composed of accidents
C) Because he follows the Ash'ari school
D) Because he denies the existence of accidents

Key Takeaway
Al-Nizam's agreement with the Ash'aris on the impossibility of emptiness shows that different premises can lead to the same theological conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading that al-Nizam agreed with a group whose premises he rejected, ask yourself: In your own faith, are there conclusions you hold simply because they are popular, without really knowing why you believe them? What would it take to make your belief truly your own—rooted in your own understanding rather than borrowed arguments?
?
The objection in this lesson shows that an argument can fail if its premise is not certain. Consider an important belief you hold. If its main premise were shown to be false, would you still have a reason to hold that belief? Are you willing to examine your own foundations, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Weak Arguments for the Impossibility of Emptiness
Al-Jurjani criticizes weak arguments for the impossibility of emptiness, including motion/rest and analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why arguments from motion/rest and analogies are considered weak proofs for the impossibility of emptiness.

Core Idea
Arguments from motion/rest and analogy fail to prove emptiness is impossible.

Al-Jurjani critiques several attempts to prove that a body cannot be devoid of all accidents. The most prominent argument, based on motion and rest, is shown to be insufficient because it only covers one accident pair. Even weaker are analogical extensions of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the flow: first the motion/rest argument, then its criticism, then two analogical arguments.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three arguments being criticized
Find the motion/rest argument, the first analogy (from akwan to all accidents), and the second analogy (from after qualification to before qualification).
These are presented in order of increasing weakness.
3
Understand why motion/rest argument is weak
Al-Jurjani says it lacks generality (`la tam`im fihi`). The claim is about all accidents, but this argument only addresses motion and rest. He gives the example of air being devoid of colors and tastes.
Still, it can refute the Baghdadi and Salihiyya positions.
4
See why the analogies are even weaker
Both analogies lack a common ground (`jami`). Al-Jurjani states they are 'weaker than that weak one' (the motion/rest argument) because the latter at least establishes a partial claim.
This is a key comparative point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من احتج عليه أي على امتناع الخلو بامتناع خلوه عن الحركة والسكون كما مر وهو ضعيف لأن الدعوى عامة في كل عرض مع ضده وهذا الاحتجاج لا تعميم فيه ورب عرض سوى الحركة والسكون يخلو الجسم عنه وعن ضده فإن الهواء خال عن الألوان والطعوم وأضدادهما، نعم يصلح رداً على البغدادية حيث جوزوا الخلو عن الأكوان وعلى الصالحية حيث جوزوا الخلو عن الجميع فيما لا يزال. وأما قياس البعض على البعض وقياس ما قيل الاتصاف بما بعد وبالعكس فأضعف من ذلك الضعيف يعني أن بعضهم حاول التعميم في الاحتجاج المذكور. فقال: لما ثبت امتناع الخلو عن الأكوان ثبت امتناعه الأعراض بالقياس عليها، وهو فاسد جداً فساداً ظاهراً إذ لا جامع فيه أصلاً. وبعضهم أراد إثبات المدعى، فقال: اتفقت الأشاعرة والمعتزلة على امتناع الخلو بعد الاتصاف وذلك لإجراء العادة الله من تعالى بخلق المثل أو الضد بعده عند الأشعري وامتناع زوال العرض إلا بطريان ضده عند المعتزلي فكذا يمتنع الخلو قبله قياساً عليه. وهو أيضاً خال عن الجامع مع ظهور الفارق وإنما كانا أضعف من التمسك بالحركة والسكون لأنه يثبت بعضا من المطلوب بخلافها.
And among them is one who argued for it—i.e., for the impossibility of emptiness—by the impossibility of the body being devoid of motion and rest, as has passed, and it is weak because the claim is general for every accident and its opposite, and this argument has no generalization in it. There may be an accident other than motion and rest of which the body is devoid along with its opposite; indeed, air is devoid of colors, tastes, and their opposites. Yes, it is suitable as a refutation against the Baghdadi, since they permitted emptiness of the akwan, and against the Salihiyya, since they permitted emptiness of all in the future. As for the analogy of some upon some and the analogy of what was said about being qualified with the later and the opposite, they are weaker than that weak one. That is, some attempted to generalize in the mentioned argument, saying: Since the impossibility of emptiness of the akwan is established, the impossibility of emptiness of accidents is established by analogy upon them—and this is extremely invalid, obviously corrupt, since there is no commonality in it at all. And some intended to prove the claim, saying: The Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila agreed upon the impossibility of emptiness after being qualified, due to God's habit of creating the like or the opposite after it according to al-Ash'ari, and the impossibility of an accident ceasing except by the arrival of its opposite according to the Mu'tazili; so likewise, emptiness before it is impossible by analogy upon it. And this too is devoid of commonality, with the difference apparent. And these two are weaker than the argument by motion and rest because it establishes some of the desired, unlike them.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage discusses and dismisses weak arguments for the impossibility of a body being free from all accidents. First, it mentions an argument from the impossibility of being devoid of motion and rest—this is weak because the claim is general for all accident-opposite pairs, but this argument only covers motion/rest. In fact, some bodies (like air) can be devoid of colors and tastes. However, this argument can still refute the positions of the Baghdadi Mu'tazila (who allow emptiness except for akwan) and the Salihiyya (who allow emptiness of all). Then the author presents two analogical arguments that are even weaker. One attempts to generalize from akwan to all accidents by analogy, but there is no common ground. The second argues that since after a body is qualified by an accident, it cannot become devoid without its opposite arriving, similarly before qualification it cannot be devoid; this is also invalid due to lack of commonality. These analogies are deemed weaker than the motion/rest argument because that argument at least establishes part of the claim.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
قياس
أكوان
عادة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from motion and rest proves emptiness is impossible universally.
Correction: It only covers motion and rest, not all accidents; other bodies can be devoid of other accident-opposite pairs.

Checkpoint
Why is the argument from motion/rest considered weak?
A) Because motion and rest are not accidents
B) Because it does not cover all accidents
C) Because bodies always have motion
D) Because the Ash'aris reject it

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani shows that arguments for the impossibility of emptiness based on motion/rest and analogies are insufficient, as they lack generality or common ground.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you relied on a weak analogy to defend a belief you held dear, ignoring its flaws because it seemed to support your position? Will you now seek stronger evidence, even if it challenges you?
?
Are you willing to admit that some of the reasons you give for your faith might be as incomplete as the motion/rest argument—partially true but not universally valid? How will you test them honestly?
?
This lesson shows that even reputable scholars can produce flawed arguments in theology. In your own learning, do you accept arguments from authority uncritically, or do you examine their reasoning as al-Jurjani does? What will you change in your study habits?

The Permissivist's First Proof and Partial Answer
First proof of permissivist and start of answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof of those who permit a body to be free from accidents and the beginning of the Ash'ari response.

Core Idea
If substance entails accident, God would be compelled, undermining free choice.

Al-Jurjani begins presenting arguments from those who allow emptiness—that a body can exist without accidents. Their first proof challenges the necessary connection between substance and accident by arguing it would compel God, contradicting His free will. This lesson introduces that proof and the initial counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage presents a proof and then a brief answer that is cut off.
The answer is incomplete in this window, but the structure is clear.
2
Identify the permissivist's argument
The passage states that if a substance cannot exist without an accident, then God would be compelled to create the accident simultaneously, negating His choice. Understand the conditional logic.
The permissivist uses this to argue against the necessity of accidents.
3
Examine the Ash'ari response
The answer begins: 'That is required of you regarding the rest...' This is a tu quoque argument turning the same problem back on the permissivist. Think about what 'the rest' might refer to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المجوز للخلو بوجوه ثلاثة: الأول: لو لزم من وجود الجوهر وجود العرض لكان الرب تعالى مضطراً إلى إحداث العرض عند إحداث الجوهر وأنه ينفي الاختيار والجواب أن هذا لازم عليكم عن سائر
The one who permits emptiness argued with three proofs: The first: If the existence of a substance necessitated the existence of an accident, then the Lord, exalted, would be compelled to create the accident when creating the substance, and that negates choice. And the answer: That this is required of you [أيها المجوز] regarding the rest [of the accidents?]...
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage begins the presentation of arguments from those who allow a body to be free from accidents. The author states that the permissivist (al-mujawwiz) gives three proofs; only the first is presented in this window. The proof is: if a substance cannot exist without an accident, then God would be compelled to create the accident simultaneously, which would negate His free choice. The answer begins but is cut off: it appears to turn the argument back on the permissivist himself, saying that the same problem applies to his own view (perhaps regarding the 'rest' of the accidents or something similar). Because the answer is incomplete, the full dialectic is not given here.

Key Terms
المجوز

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The permissivist's argument proves that accidents are not necessary for substances.
Correction: The argument only shows that if substance requires accident, then God would be compelled, but the Ash'ari response notes that the same problem applies to the permissivist's own view, so the argument does not disprove the necessity of accidents.

Checkpoint

A) That bodies are eternal

C) That accidents are illusions
D) That the Qur'an does not mention accidents

Key Takeaway
The permissivist's first proof asserts that if substance requires accident then God would be compelled; the Ash'ari answer turns the argument back on the permissivist.

?

?

?
The permissivist argued from compulsion being bad. But have you ever resented God's absolute freedom over your life because it makes you feel powerless? What would it mean for you to trust that His free choice is wiser and more merciful than any compulsion you imagine?

Objection and Answer on Infinite Regress of Knowledge
Objection and answer from al-Baqillani on infinite regress of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani resolves the problem of infinite regress of knowledge by positing a universal opposite that negates all sciences at once.

Core Idea
A single universal opposite can negate infinite sciences.

In this passage, an objection is raised regarding the infinite regress of knowledge and its impossibility. Al-Baqillani responds by introducing the concept of a universal opposite (al-ḍidd al-ʿāmm), a single attribute that can negate an infinite series of sciences simultaneously. This response preserves the coherence of infinite substitution while avoiding logical absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to get the overall structure: an objection followed by al-Baqillani's answer.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that infinite regress by substitution is not impossible because at each moment, the sum of actual and past knowledge is finite.
3
Identify al-Baqillani's answer
Al-Baqillani answers by saying the absence of infinite sciences can be due to a single universal opposite, like death or sleep, which negates all sciences at once.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how a single opposite can cancel an infinite series, showing that infinite absence does not require infinite causes.
This resolves the apparent impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير متناهية على سبيل البدل وليس بمستحيل لأن الحاصل للعبد في كل وقت مع ما قبله من الأوقات متناه قطعاً. ( وأجاب القاضي الباقلاني (بأنه قد يكون انتفاء ما انتفى) عنه ( من العلوم ) التي لا تتناهى ( بضد عام ) هو صفة واحدة مضادة لجميع تلك العلوم المنتفية ولا استحالة في مثل ذلك ) كالموت والنوم) فإنهما ضدان لجميع العلوم على الإطلاق.
Infinite by way of substitution and not impossible, because what is actual for the servant in every moment combined with what preceded it from moments is finite necessarily. And al-Qadi al-Baqillani answered that the negation of what is negated from him of the sciences which are infinite may be by a universal opposite, which is a single attribute opposed to all those negated sciences, and there is no impossibility in such a thing, like death and sleep, for they are two opposites to all sciences absolutely.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and an answer regarding the coherence of infinite regress in knowledge. The objection states that an infinite series by substitution is not impossible because in each moment the servant's actual knowledge is finite when combined with the past. Al-Baqillani replies that the absence of an infinite number of sciences from a person can be due to a single universal opposite, such as death or sleep, which negates all sciences at once. This shows that infinite absence can be caused by a single attribute, removing the apparent impossibility.

The Third Proof: Air and Water's Color
Proof and response on the colorlessness of air and water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that air and water have no perceptible color, and the theological rejoinder that they either have an imperceptible color or transparency is an existent opposite.

Core Idea
Air and water's apparent colorlessness is argued either as imperceptible color or as transparency being a real opposite.

This passage presents the third proof concerning the absence of color in air and water, and the kalam response that accounts for their perceived colorlessness without denying the attribute entirely. The debate hinges on whether transparency is a mere lack or a positive quality. Let's examine the proof and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of proof and answer become clear.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic; they indicate where the text is quoting or glossing.
2
Identify the proof's claim
The proof claims that air and water are free of both a specific color (like black) and its opposite. Why does this lead to the conclusion that they have no color at all?
The proof also mentions freedom from opposing tastes as a parallel.
3
Examine the two answers
The answer denies the absolute absence and gives two options: either air and water have a color too weak to perceive, or transparency itself is an existent opposite of absolute color.
The first option appeals to imperceptibility; the second treats transparency as a positive quality.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself: why can't air simply have no color? The answer suggests that every substance must have either a color or its opposite – a key atomist assumption.
This connects to broader kalam debates about accident and substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث : الهواء (و) كذا (الماء خال عن اللون ) المخصوص كالسواد مثلاً (و) عن (ضده) أيضاً إذ لا لون له أصلاً وكذا هو خال عن الطعوم المتضادة كما مرت الإشارة إليه ) والجواب منع عدم اللون ) فيه (بل) له لون ما لكنه ( لا يدرك لضعفه أو التزم أن الشفيف ) الثابت للهواء والماء أمر وجودي هو (ضد اللون ( المطلق ( لا عدمه.
The third proof: Air, and similarly water, is free of the particular color like blackness, and free of its opposite as well, since it has no color at all. Similarly, it is free of opposing tastes as previously indicated. The answer: The negation of color in it is denied; rather, it has some color but it is not perceived due to its weakness. Or one may adhere that transparency, which is established for air and water, is an existential thing that is the opposite of absolute color, not its non-existence.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is the third proof and its response. The proof claims that air and water have no color because they are free of both a specific color and its opposite. The answer rejects the claim of absolute absence, offering two explanations: either air and water have a color too weak to perceive, or transparency itself is an existent opposite of color, not a mere lack. This preserves the principle that every substance must have some color or its opposite.

Correction: According to kalam, they either have an imperceptible color or transparency is an existent opposite, so they do possess a color-related attribute.

First Proof: Dimensions are Finite (Burhan al-Muwazat)
Proof of finitude of dimensions using parallelism argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric argument known as the proof of parallelism (burhan al-muwazat) and how it demonstrates that all dimensions must be finite.

Core Idea
An infinite line contradicts the impossibility of a first point of intersection.

This lesson presents the first and most celebrated proof for the finitude of dimensions, called the proof of parallelism. It is a geometrical argument that shows why assuming an infinite line leads to a contradiction. We will follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details. Then read the translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice that the author begins by stating the thesis and then presents the first proof.
2
Understand the initial setup
Visualize the setup: an infinite line exists, and from a point we draw a finite line parallel to it. Then we tilt the finite line by rotating it around the fixed point.
The key is that the finite line is initially parallel, so they never meet; after tilting, they must intersect.
3
Identify the contradiction
The tilting causes an intersection at some point on the infinite line. This intersection is a new event and must have a first point. But on an infinite line, any assumed first point has another point above it that would be encountered first. This leads to a contradiction.
The argument hinges on the infinite divisibility of the angle of tilting.
4
Consider the author's summary
The author summarizes the proof as a dilemma: either no intersection occurs (impossible because motion removes parallelism), or there is a first point of intersection (impossible for the reason given).
Both alternatives are false, so the assumption of an infinite dimension is false.
5
Note the objection
The passage ends with an objection denying the possibility of the initial construction. The full answer is not included here; we will cover it in later lessons.
This objection is a standard critique of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(خطأ غير متناه وخطاً آخر ) متناهياً) بحيث (يوازيه) في وضعه الأول أي يكون بحيث لا يلاقية أصلاً، وإن أخرج إلى غير النهاية ( ثم يميل ) الخط المتناهي بحركته مع ثبات أحد طرفيه الذي في جانب المبدأ (من الذي فرض غير متناه (إلا) والمسامتة مع ما قبلها أي فوقها من جانب لا تناهي الخط (قبل المسامتة معها ) . وذلك ( لأن المسامتة) مع أية نقطة تفرض (إنما تحصل بزاوية مستقيمة الخطين) عند الطرف الثابت من الخط المتناهي فأحد الخطين هو هذا المتناهي مفروضاً على وضع الموازاة، والآخر هو بعينه أيضاً لكن حال كونه على المسامتة فكأن هناك خطاً آخر كان منطبقاً عليه فزال بحركته انطباقه مع بقاء أحد طرفيه على حاله، ويزداد اتضاحه بأن نفرض الخط المتناهي خارجاً من مركز كرة موازياً لغير المتناهي ثم نفرض حركتها حتى يصير مسامتاً فيحدث عند مركز الكرة زاوية مستقيمة الخطين وأنها تقبل القسمة إلى غير النهاية إذ قد بين إقليدس في ) الشكل التاسع من المقالة الأولى من كتابه أن كل زاوية مستقيمة الخطين يمكن تنصيفها بخط مستقيم ولا شك أن كل واحد من النصفين زاوية مستقيمة الخطين فيقبل التنصيف أيضاً، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له على أن الزاوية المسطحة إما كم أو كيفية حالة فيه سارية في جهة واحدة منه فتكون قابلة للانقسام أبداً كالمقادير. وكلما كانت الزاوية أصغر كانت المسامتة مع النقطة الفوقانية ) يعني إذا فرض أن نقطة ما هي أول نقط المسامتة لم تكن تلك النقطة كذلك لأن المسامتة معها إنما تكون بحدوث زاوية منقسمة إلى نصفين ولا شك أن حدوث نصفها قبل حدوث كلها وفي حال حدوث النصف توجد المسامتة لزوال الموازاة حينئذ قطعا وتلك المسامتة مع نقطة فوقانية بلا شبهة فلا تكون النقطة الأولى في أول نقطة المسامتة وهكذا فلا يمكن أن يوجد هناك ما هو أول تلك النقط وقد تبين ذلك بأن المسامتة إنما تكون بالحركة وكل حركة منقسمة إلى جزء سابق وجزء لاحق فحال ما يوجد الجزء السابق تكون المسامتة مع نقطة أخرى وهكذا قال المصنف : ( تلخيصه) أي تلخيص هذا الوجه أنه لو وجد بعد غير متناه لأمكن الفرض أي المفروض ( المذكور واللازم باطل لأنه مستلزم إما لامتناع المسامتة أو لوجود نقطة هي أول نقط المسامتة) إذ مع ذلك الفرض إما أن تمتنع المسامتة وهو أحد الأمرين أولا تمتنع فيجب أن يوجد أول نقط المسامتة وهو الأمر الآخر ( والقسمان باطلان ) أما وجود تلك النقطة فلما استحالته واستلزام وجودها تناهي ما لا يتناهى أيضاً مر من وأما امتناع المسامتة فلان زوال الموازاة بالحركة يستلزم وجودها فلا يتصور امتناعهاء على ذلك الفرض كما لا يخفى، ومنهم من فرض الخط المتناهي أولاً مسامتاً ثم تحرك إلى أن صار موازياً ، قال : فلا بد من نقطة هي آخر نقط المسامتة لأنها كانت ثم زالت فيكون لها نهاية لكنه باطل لمثل ما مر وسماه برهان الموازاة . ( واعترض عليه بمنع إمكان الفرض أي لا نسلم أنه لو وجد بعد غير متناه لأمكن وجود خط غير
The seventh section: Dimensions. Existing dimensions are finite from all sides, whether those dimensions are in plenum, like the dimensions concomitant with bodily matter, or in void, like dimensions abstracted from it, if void is allowed. The intention is that the finitude of dimensions does not depend on the impossibility of void, contrary to the Indians, for they held that they are infinite. We say they are finite due to proofs: The first: If an infinite dimension existed, even from one side, we could posit from a given starting point an infinite line and another finite line such that it is parallel to it in its initial position, i.e., it does not meet it at all, even if extended to infinity. Then the finite line is tilted by its movement while one of its ends at the side of the starting point remains fixed, from parallelism inclining toward the direction of the infinite line, and it meets it, i.e., it becomes such that it intersects it by extension. This occurrence of meeting by that movement is known necessarily. The mentioned meeting is originated, being non-existent in the state of prior parallelism, so it has a first moment, as every originated thing does. And that meeting is at a point, because intersection of two lines can only be imagined at a point. Thus there would be a first point of meeting on the infinite line, and that is impossible, because there is no point that can be assumed on the infinite line except that the meeting with what is before it, i.e., above it from the side of the infinity of the line, occurs before the meeting with it. That is because the meeting with any assumed point occurs by a straight-angle line at the fixed end of the finite line. One of the two lines is this finite line assumed in the parallel position, and the other is the same but in the state of meeting, as if another line was coincident with it and then its coincidence was removed by its movement while one of its ends remains. This becomes clearer if we assume the finite line extending from the center of a sphere parallel to the infinite one, then we assume its movement until it becomes meeting; then at the center of the sphere there arises a straight-line angle, and it is divisible to infinity, since Euclid demonstrated in the ninth proposition of the first book of his work that every straight-line angle can be halved by a straight line, and no doubt each half is a straight-line angle, so it can be halved again, and so on to infinity, because the plane angle is either a quantity or a quality inhering in it, extending in one direction, so it is always divisible like magnitudes. And the smaller the angle, the more the meeting is with a higher point. That is, if a point is assumed as the first point of meeting, it is not so, because the meeting with it occurs only by the generation of an angle divisible into halves, and no doubt the occurrence of its half is before the occurrence of the whole, and at the moment of the occurrence of the half the meeting exists due to the removal of parallelism, and that meeting is with a higher point beyond doubt, so the first is not the first point of meeting, and so on, so there cannot be a first of those points. This is made clear also by the fact that meeting occurs by movement, and every movement is divisible into a prior part and a later part, so when the prior part occurs, meeting is with another point, and so on. The author said: Summary of this proof: If an infinite dimension existed, the mentioned assumption would be possible, but the consequent is false because it entails either the impossibility of meeting or the existence of a point that is the first point of meeting. For with that assumption, either meeting is impossible (one of the two), or it is not impossible, so a first point of meeting must exist (the other). Both alternatives are false. As for the existence of that point, it is impossible and entails the finitude of what is infinite, as mentioned. As for the impossibility of meeting, the removal of parallelism by movement necessitates its existence, so its impossibility cannot be conceived on that assumption, as is clear. Some assumed the finite line first meeting and then moving to become parallel, saying: there must be a point that is the last point of meeting because it existed and then ceased, so it has an end, but that is false for similar reasons, and he called it the proof of parallelism. And it was objected by denying the possibility of the assumption: we do not grant that if an infinite dimension existed, it would be possible for a line to exist...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is the main proof that all dimensions are finite, known as burhan al-muwazat (proof of parallelism). It begins by stating the thesis: dimensions are finite from all sides, whether in matter or void. Then it presents the first proof: assume an infinite line. From a point, we draw a finite line parallel to it. Tilt the finite line while fixing one end; it will eventually intersect the infinite line. The intersection is an event that must have a first point. But on an infinite line, any assumed point has another point above it that would be encountered first. This leads to a contradiction: there is no first point. Therefore the original assumption (infinite dimension) is false. The summary clarifies that two options—impossibility of intersection or existence of a first point—are both impossible. An objection is raised denying the possibility of the initial construction, but the text cuts off before the answer.

متناه

زاوية مستقيمة الخطين
برهان الموازاة

Key Takeaway
The proof of parallelism demonstrates that the assumption of an infinite dimension leads to a logical contradiction regarding the first point of intersection, thus proving that dimensions must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You have just followed a subtle logical proof. But how often do you reflect deeply on the signs of Allah's creation? When you look at the night sky, do you see the limits of the cosmos and feel the greatness of the One who bound them, or do you see only a backdrop for your ambitions? Commit to spending five minutes tonight contemplating the finitude of creation and your own dependence on the Infinite Creator.
?
The proof shows that an infinite regress leads to contradiction—there must be a first point. Yet in your repentance and spiritual growth, do you keep delaying the 'first step'? Is there a change you have been postponing, thinking there will always be time to start? The finitude of dimensions reminds you that opportunities also have ends. What will you begin today that you have been putting off?

Logic and Technical Terms
