8 min
The Objection of Denying the Assumption and Its Response
Objection that the geometric configuration may be impossible, and response that it is intuitively necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the Proof of Parallelism (denying the possibility of the geometric assumption) and the response that the assumption is intuitively necessary.

Core Idea
The assumption in the proof is self-evidently possible like basic geometric operations.

The first objection to the Proof of Parallelism challenges the very possibility of the geometric configuration used. The objector argues that even if infinite dimensions exist, the specific combination of an infinite line and a moving finite line might be impossible. The response appeals to intuitive certainty: reason directly knows that such configurations are possible, just as we know that we can apply one line to another or rotate a circle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: objection, then response, then examples.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two ways the assumption could be impossible
The objector gives two scenarios: either one element is impossible by itself, or each is possible but their conjunction is impossible (like standing and not standing). Understand this before moving on.
3
Focus on the response: claim of necessity
The response asserts that we know by self-evident reason that each element and their combination is possible if infinite dimensions are assumed. The impossibility must then come from the infinitude itself.
This is the key move: turning the burden of proof back.
4
Examine the examples of self-evident assumptions
The passage gives geometric constructions (applying, separating, rotating) as clear cases that reason judges possible. Denying them is obstinacy.
Consider why these are considered self-evident.
5
Reflect on the objection's failure
The objection fails because the assumption is not impossible; it is of the same kind as these geometric operations. Thus the Proof of Parallelism stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه بمنع إمكان الفرض أي لا نسلم أنه لو وجد بعد غير متناه لأمكن وجود خط غير متناه مع وجود خط آخر متناه فيكون موازياً للأول، أولاً مسامتاً له بسبب حركته، ثانياً إذ يجوز أن يكون بعض هذه الأمور محالاً نفسه أو يكون كل واحد منها ممكناً واجتماعها محالاً كاجتماع قيام زيد مع عدمه وحينئذ جاز أن يكون البعد الغير المتناهي ممكناً والفرض ممتنعاً على أحد الوجهين ويكون المحال ناشئاً منه لا من البعد الذي لا يتناهى أو يكون كلاهما ممكناً ويلزم المحال من اجتماعهما. وجوابه دعوى الضرورة أي نحن نعلم ببديهة العقل أن كل واحد من الأمور المفروضة ومجموعها أيضاً ممكن على تقدير لاتناهي الأبعاد فلو كان لا تناهيها ممكناً في نفس الأمر لم يكن هناك ممتنع لا بسيط ولا مركب فلا يتصور لزوم محال ولما لزم علم أن المحال هو اللاتناهي وحده واعلم أن من المفروض ما يحكم العقل بجوازه بديهة كالفروض الهندسية مثل تطبيق خط على خط وفصل خط من خط وإدارة دائرة بتحريك خط مستقيم مع ثبات أحد طرفيه إلى أن يعود إلى وضعه الأول وليس لأحد أن يمتنعه إلا مكابرة وما نحن فيه من قبيل هذه الفروض كما نبهنا عليه فلا يتجه عليه منع إمكانه على ذلك التقدير
And an objection was made against it by denying the possibility of the assumption, i.e., we do not concede that if there were an infinite dimension, then it would be possible for an infinite line to exist together with another finite line such that it is parallel to the first, then concurrent with it due to its motion. This is because it is possible that one of these things itself is impossible, or that each one alone is possible but their conjunction is impossible, like the conjunction of Zayd's standing with his non-existence. In that case, it is possible that the infinite dimension is possible while the assumption is impossible in one of the two ways, so the impossibility arises from the assumption, not from the dimension that is infinite; or both are possible but the impossible results from their conjunction. And its answer is the claim of necessity, i.e., we know by the evidence of reason that each one of the assumed things and also their totality is possible on the assumption of the infinitude of dimensions. For if their infinitude were possible in itself, there would be no impossibility, neither simple nor compound, so no impossibility would be conceivable. And when impossibility occurred, it would be known that the impossibility is solely due to the infinitude. And know that among the assumptions are those that reason judges to be possible self-evidently, like geometric assumptions such as applying a line to a line, separating a line from a line, and rotating a circle by moving a straight line while fixing one of its endpoints until it returns to its initial position. And no one can deny it except by obstinacy. And our case is of this kind of assumption, as we have pointed out, so the denial of its possibility on that assumption is not valid.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the Proof of Parallelism and its response. The objector argues that the geometric configuration assumed in the proof (an infinite line alongside a finite one that moves from parallel to concurrent) may not be feasible. Even if an infinite dimension exists, the specific combination of assumptions might be impossible—either because one element is impossible or because their conjunction is impossible, like Zayd standing while not existing. The response asserts that these assumptions are intuitively certain (دعوى الضرورة): reason directly knows that each element and their combination is possible given infinite dimensions. The commentator lists self-evident geometric constructions (applying a line, rotating a circle, etc.) and states that denying them is mere stubbornness. Therefore, the objection fails because the assumptions are clearly possible on the hypothesis of infinite dimensions.

اجتماع
بديهة
فروض هندسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objector is denying the possibility of infinite dimensions itself.
Correction: Rather, they are denying the possibility of the specific geometric setup used in the proof.

Checkpoint
How does the response to the first objection defend the possibility of the geometric assumption in the Proof of Parallelism?
A) By proving that infinite dimensions necessarily imply the assumption.
B) By appealing to self-evident geometric constructions that reason judges possible.
C) By showing that the assumption does not lead to a contradiction.
D) By redefining the terms of the objection.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because the geometric assumptions of the Proof of Parallelism are self-evidently possible to reason.

?

?
‘We know by the evidence of reason’—the passage says. But you live as if the most important matters are uncertain. If you truly believed that reason itself points to God's existence and your dependence on Him, what would you have to change about how you live this week?

The Objection and First Response: Necessity of a First Meeting Point
Objection and first response about the necessity of a first meeting point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the proof of a first meeting point and the initial response that the meeting, being an event, must have a first point.

Core Idea
An event must have a first moment; thus a first meeting point is necessary.

The objector denies that a first meeting point is required, arguing that since the angle and motion are infinitely divisible, there can be no first point. The response counters that the meeting itself is an occurrence (haditha), and every occurrence has a first moment, which necessarily corresponds to a point. Let's examine this exchange closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the objection and its translation. Notice how the objector uses the same reasoning from the earlier proof—divisibility—to deny a first point.
The objector says 'we do not concede' and then gives the reason.
2
Identify the core argument of the objection
The objector argues: because meeting occurs by an angle and motion that are divisible, for any assumed first point, there is a prior meeting with a point above it.
This exploits infinite divisibility to reject a limit.
3
Study the first response
The response states that the meeting is an event, which must have a first moment. That first moment corresponds to a point, making it the first meeting point.
The response shifts focus from the divisibility of space to the nature of events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال عليه أيضا : لا نسلم لزوم نقطة هي أول نقط المسامتة لعين ما ذكرتم في بطلان التالي أي نستدل به على بطلان الملازمة، فنقول : إذا تحرك نصف قطر الكرة كما ذكرتم وجب أن لا يوجد في الخط الذي لا يتناهى نقط هي أول نقط المسامتة لأن المسامتة إنما تكون بزاوية وحركة مقسمين فلا يوجد هناك ما هي أول نقطها لأن كل نقطة تفرض كذلك كانت المسامتة مع ما فوقها قبلها والجواب عن هذا أنا بينا لزوم ذلك بأن المسامتة لها أول لكونها حادثة وهو يكون بنقطة ضرورة فالنقطة التي حدثت المسامتة معها في ذلك الأول هي أول نقطها ودليل امتناع اللازم في نفسه لا يدل على عدم ملازمته لجواز أن يكون الملزوم أيضا ممتنعاً كيف ولو دل على ذلك تم الأقيسة الاستثنائية التي استثني فيها نقيض التالي واستدل عليه وإليه أشار بقوله : وإلا جاء في كل قياس استثنائي يستثنى فيه نقيض التالي وقد يجاب أيضاً: بأنا نستدل هكذا لو كانت الأبعاد غير متناهية وتحرك الخط المتناهي من الموازاة إلى المسامتة فإما أن يوجد أول نقط المسامتة أو لا يوجد، وكلاهما محال بدليلكم ودليلنا وعلى هذا بطل اعتراضكم بالكلية لكن بقي هاهنا بحث، وهو أنا لا نسلم أن المسامتة ببعض الزاوية أو الحركة قبل المسامتة الحاصلة بكلها وإنما يلزم ذلك إذا كان بعضهما موجوداً بالفعل حتى يمكن أن يوجد به مسامتة لكنهما ينقسمان بالقوة لا بالفعل، ولو صح ما ذكرتموه لامتنع حركة نصف قطر الدائرة على قوس منها لأن الحركة إلى نصف القوس قبل الحركة إلى كلها والحركة إلى نصف الزاوية قبل
And it may also be said against it: We do not concede the necessity of a point that is the first point of meeting, for the very reason you mentioned in the falsity of the consequent, i.e., we use it to prove the falsity of the implication. So we say: If the radius of the sphere moves as you mentioned, then it must be that in the infinite line there is no point that is the first point of meeting, because meeting occurs only by an angle and motion that are divisible, so there is no first point among them, because every point assumed as such, the meeting with what is above it occurs before it. And the answer to this is that we have shown the necessity of that because the meeting has a first since it is an occurrence, and it is with a point necessarily. So the point with which the meeting occurred at that first is its first point. And the proof of the impossibility of the consequent in itself does not indicate the non-necessity of its implication, because it is possible that the antecedent is also impossible. Indeed, if it indicated that, then conditional syllogisms in which the negation of the consequent is used as a premise would be valid [universally], and he pointed to this by his saying: 'Otherwise, it would occur in every conditional syllogism in which the negation of the consequent is used as a premise.' And it may also be answered: We argue as follows: If dimensions were infinite and the finite line moved from parallelism to meeting, then either there is a first point of meeting or there is not. Both are impossible by your proof and our proof. On this, your objection is completely invalid. But there remains a discussion here: we do not concede that meeting occurs by a part of the angle or motion before the meeting obtained by the whole. Rather, that would only follow if the parts existed actually such that a meeting could occur by them, but they are divided potentially, not actually. And if what you mentioned were correct, then the motion of the radius of a circle along its arc would be impossible, because motion to half the arc is before motion to the whole, and motion to half the angle is before...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and multiple responses. The objector denies that a first meeting point is necessary, using the same reasoning as the proof (that the angle and motion are divisible) to argue that there cannot be a first point. The first response counters that the meeting is an event and thus must have a first point; the point at which it first occurs is the first meeting point. The second response addresses the logical structure: the objector argues that the impossibility of the consequent (no first point) disproves the implication, but the commentator points out that this would only work if the antecedent (infinite dimensions) were possible; otherwise, the conditional argument still holds. A final response uses a dilemma: either there is a first point or not, both impossible, so the assumption of infinite dimensions is false. The passage ends with a discussion about actual vs. potential division, noting that the divisibility is potential, not actual, so the objection about motion being prior is invalid; otherwise, it would undermine basic geometry like motion along an arc.

Key Terms
أول نقط المسامتة
حادثة
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infinite divisibility of the angle and motion proves there is no first meeting point.
Correction: Infinite divisibility does not negate the first point of meeting because the meeting is an event; every event has a first moment, which determines the first point.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first response to the objection?
A) The angle and motion are actually finite.
B) The meeting is an event, so it has a first point.
C) The objector's argument commits a logical fallacy.
D) The infinite line has a first point.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because the meeting is an event, and every event must have a first moment, which gives a first point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector argued that because something is divisible, it cannot have a first point. Are there areas in your life where you avoid taking the first step because you think the path is too complex or endless? How does this lesson's response—that an event must have a first moment—compel you to act now?
?
Every occurrence has a first moment. Your own faith and repentance—are they events that need a first moment you have been putting off? What 'first point' are you denying while waiting for a perfect condition that never comes?
?
The responder appeals to the very nature of events. How does this change the way you view your daily actions—are they just processes, or do they have definitive beginnings that you must own?

Further Responses: Logical Refinement and the Dilemma
Logical rebuttal, dilemma, and discussion of potential vs actual division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the defender refines the logical structure of conditional syllogisms and uses a dilemma to show the impossibility of infinite dimensions.

Core Idea
Impossibility of the consequent does not prove implication false if the antecedent may also be impossible.

After the initial response, the defender addresses the logical underpinnings of the objection. He explains that the objector's move—using the impossibility of the consequent to deny the implication—is invalid because the antecedent (infinite dimensions) might also be impossible. He then presents a dilemma: assuming infinite dimensions, both options (first point exists or not) are impossible, so the assumption is false. The passage ends with a crucial discussion about potential vs actual division.

Guided Path
1
Read the logical clarification
Read the part about conditional syllogisms. The defender argues that if the impossibility of the consequent always disproved the implication, then no conditional syllogism would be valid—but that's not the case.
The defender points out that the antecedent itself could be impossible.
2
Understand the dilemma
The defender sets up a dilemma: if dimensions are infinite, either there is a first meeting point or not. Both lead to contradiction, so the assumption of infinite dimensions must be false.
This strengthens the original proof.
3
Examine the potential vs actual division discussion
The final part argues that the objector's objection relies on actual division, but the division is only potential. If actual division were required, even basic motions like a radius along an arc would be impossible.
This undermines the objection's basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Shield Argument for Finitude
Iji enumerates the fifth proof for finitude using the shield analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the shield proof for the finitude of dimensions and how it relies on geometric reasoning and the impossibility of an infinite bounded section.

Core Idea
An infinite section cannot be enclosed by finite boundaries.

The shield proof is a creative geometric argument that demonstrates the finitude of all dimensions by dividing a circular shield into sections and considering whether an individual section could be infinite. This lesson walks through the construction, the dilemma it poses, and the geometric clarification that makes the argument rigorous.

Guided Path
1
Construct the shield and divide it
Read the description of dividing a circular body (like a shield) into six equal sections by drawing lines through the center.
The circle is used for simplicity; it could be any shape.
2
Extend the sides to infinity
Understand that we imagine extending the bounding lines of each section infinitely, dividing all space.
This creates a grid-like division of the world.
3
Analyze one section: finite or infinite?
Consider one section in width: if infinite, it is enclosed by two finite lines (impossible); if finite, the whole is finite.
This is the core dilemma.
4
Geometric clarification
See how the author shows that each section is an equilateral triangle, proving that the divergence between lines is proportional to extension.
This verifies the structural consistency of the argument.
5
Connect to previous proofs
Note that the third, fourth, and fifth proofs are essentially the same underlying argument.
This shows unity in the proofs of finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس: أنا نقسم جسماً على هيئة الدائرة وليكن ترساً بستة أقسام متساوية بأن نقسم أولاً محيط دائرته إلى ست قطع متساوية ثم نصل بين النقط المتقابلة بخطوط متقاطعة على مركزه فينقسم حينئذ إلى أقسام ستة متساوية يحيط بكل قسم منها ضلعان، ثم نخرج الأضلاع بأسرها إلى غير النهاية حتى تنقسم الأبعاد كلها في طولها وعرضها أعني سعة العالم بهذه الأقسام ثم نردد في كل قسم فنقول: هو في عرضه إما غير متناه فينحصر ما لا يتناهى بين حاصرين هما الضلعان المحيطان به وإما متناه فكذا الكل متناه أيضاً لأنه ضعف المتناهي الذي هو أحد الأقسام بمرات متناهية هي الستة. وهذا البرهان المسمى بالترسي كالتتمة والتوضيح للبرهان الذي هو تلخيص السلمي لأن كل قسم من الستة كمثلث متساوي الأضلاع لأنك إذا فرضت على ضلعي كل قسم نقطتين متساويتي البعد عن المركز ووصلت بينهما بخط كان ذلك الخط مساوياً لكل واحد من الضلعين، وذلك لأن الزاوية التي عند المركز ثلثا قائمة إذ المحيط بكل نقطة أربع قوائم وقد قسمت هاهنا بست زوايا متساوية، وكذا كل واحدة من الزاويتين الباقيتين ثلثا قائمة لأنهما متساويتان لتساوي وتريهما وإذا كانت زوايا المثلث متساوية كانت الأضلاع كذلك فظهر أن الانفراج بين كل ضلعين بقدر امتدادهما كما في ذلك البرهان، إلا أن هاهنا تصويراً ومزيد توضيح لإمكان خروج خطين من نقطة بحيث ينفرجان على قدر امتدادهما وكان يكفيه هاهنا أن يخرج من نقطة واحدة خطوطاً ستة على أن تكون جميع الزوايا متساوية إلا أن في إمكان ذلك نوع خفاء ففرض دائرة لا شبهة في إمكان تقسيم محيطها إلى أقسام ستة متساوية وحينئذ يلزم تساوي الزوايا المركزية وكون كل واحدة ثلثي قائمة فينكشف مساواة البعد فيما بين الخطين لامتدادهما انكشافاً تاماً وهذه الوجوه أعني الثالث والرابع والخامس كما لا يخفى راجعة إلى برهان واحد
The fifth aspect: We divide a body in the shape of a circle, let it be a shield, into six equal sections by first dividing the circumference of its circle into six equal parts, then connecting the opposite points with lines intersecting at its center; thus it is divided into six equal sections, each section being bounded by two sides. Then we extend all the sides to infinity so that all dimensions in length and width—i.e., the extent of the world—are divided by these sections. Then we consider each section and say: it is in its width either infinite, in which case what is infinite is enclosed between two enclosers, namely the two sides surrounding it; or finite, and thus the whole is also finite because it is the finite (which is one of the sections) multiplied by a finite number of times, namely six. This proof, called the shield proof, is like a completion and clarification of the proof that is the summary of al-Salami, because each of the six sections is like an equilateral triangle. For if you assume on the two sides of each section two points equidistant from the center and connect them with a line, that line is equal to each of the two sides, because the angle at the center is two-thirds of a right angle, since the circumference at every point is four right angles and it has been divided here into six equal angles; likewise each of the remaining two angles is two-thirds of a right angle because they are equal due to the equality of their chords. And when the angles of a triangle are equal, the sides are equal as well. Thus it becomes apparent that the divergence between every two sides is proportional to their extension, as in that proof, except that here there is a depiction and additional clarification of the possibility of two lines emerging from a point such that they diverge proportionally to their extension. It would have sufficed here to draw from a single point six lines such that all angles are equal, but there is some obscurity in the possibility of that, so he assumed a circle, of which it is indubitably possible to divide its circumference into six equal parts; then the central angles are necessarily equal, each being two-thirds of a right angle, and this fully reveals the equality of the distance between the two lines to their extension. And these aspects—namely the third, fourth, and fifth—as is not hidden, reduce to a single proof.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-wajh al-khamis) for the finitude of dimensions, known as the shield proof (burhan al-tursi). The author describes a procedure: take a circular body (like a shield) and divide its circumference into six equal parts, then draw lines connecting opposite points through the center, resulting in six equal sections, each bounded by two sides. Then extend all sides infinitely. Now consider any one section: either its width is infinite or finite. If infinite, then something infinite is enclosed between two finite sides (the two bounding sides), which is impossible. If finite, then the whole world (composed of six such sections) is finite because it is the finite width of one section multiplied by a finite number (six). The author then explains that this proof is a clarification of a proof by al-Salami (likely a reference to a earlier scholar). He shows that each section is an equilateral triangle: because the central angle is 60° (two-thirds of a right angle), and the other two angles are also 60° by symmetry, thus all sides equal. This demonstrates that the divergence (al-infi raj) between any two sides is proportional to their extension, mirroring the earlier argument. The author notes that one could simply draw six lines from a point with equal angles, but the circle construction makes the possibility clearer. Finally, he remarks that the third, fourth, and fifth proofs all reduce to the same underlying argument.

Key Terms
البرهان الترسي
انفراج
امتداد
متناهي
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shield proof requires the world to actually be shaped like a shield.
Correction: The shield is only a geometric illustration; the argument works for any shape as long as the division and extension are possible.

Checkpoint
What is the key contradiction in the shield proof?
A) An infinite section would have to be bounded by finite sides.
B) A circle cannot be divided into six equal parts.
C) The sides cannot be extended infinitely.
D) The sections are not actually triangles.

Key Takeaway
The shield proof shows that the world must be finite because an infinite section would be impossibly bounded by finite lines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The shield proof demonstrates that something infinite cannot be enclosed by finite boundaries. Your life is bounded by finite time and capacity. Are you living as if your days are infinite? What is one thing you will stop doing today because it wastes the limited time you have?
?
The author uses a geometric construction to prove finitude. Are you willing to accept logical conclusions about your own existence even when they are uncomfortable? Where do you resist acknowledging your own limitations?
?
If the entire world is finite, then your actions have finite consequences. Does this motivate you to prioritize deeds that have eternal weight? Name one deed you will start today that has significance beyond this finite world.

The Application Proof: Finitude of Dimensions
Iji explains the application proof for the finitude of dimensions from all directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the application (al-tatbiq) proof that dimensions are finite in all directions and how it refutes the possibility of actual infinities.

Core Idea
If two infinite lines are superimposed, the shorter must equal the longer—a contradiction.

Iji presents a powerful geometric argument known as al-tatbiq, or the application proof. It demonstrates that dimensions cannot be infinite in all directions by considering two conceptual lines that start at different points. This proof is a classic tool in kalam for rejecting actual infinities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note that Iji describes a thought experiment with two lines.
2
Visualize the setup
Imagine an infinite line from point A to infinity. Then imagine a second infinite line from point B, where B is a finite distance before A. When we superimpose (align) the two lines from B onward, the first line extends beyond the second by that finite distance.
This is a conceptual, not physical, construction.
3
Understand the contradiction
Iji argues: either the deficient (shorter) line is equal to the excessive (longer) line, which is impossible because they differ in length, or the shorter line stops, implying both stop (since they are aligned), so both are finite. This contradicts the assumption of infinity.
The proof shows that actual infinites cannot coexist with finite differences.
4
Connect to previous uses
Iji notes that this argument was used earlier against infinite regress and for the finitude of physical forces. Recognize the recurring method.
This proof is a standard kalam argument against actual infinities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس: التطبيق) الدال على تناهي الأبعاد من جميع الجهات ( وطريقه ) هاهنا ( أن نفرض من نقطة ما إلى غير النهاية خطاً و نفرض ( من نقطة قبلها بمتناه خطاً آخر) إلى غير النهاية أيضاً ( ثم نطبق الخطين فالناقصة إما مثل الزائدة ) واستحالته ظاهرة ( أو تنقطع فينقطعان ) فلا يكونان غير متناهيين ) كما تقدم (مرتين مرة في بطلان التسلسل ومرة في تناهي القوى الجسمانية
The sixth aspect: the application (al-tatbiq), which indicates the finitude of dimensions from all directions. Its method here is that we assume from a certain point a line to infinity, and we assume from a point before it by a finite distance another line also to infinity. Then we superimpose the two lines. The deficient [line] is either like the excessive—and its impossibility is apparent—or it stops short, so both stop—thus they are not infinite, as has been presented twice: once in the refutation of infinite regress, and once in the finitude of physical forces.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, called al-tatbiq (application or superimposition). The method: take a point, draw an infinite line from it. Then take a point a finite distance before that point, and draw another infinite line from it. Now superimpose the two lines (imagine placing them so they coincide from the second point onward). The first line extends beyond the second by the finite distance between the starting points. The author argues: either the deficient line (the one starting later) is equal to the excessive line (the longer one), which is impossible because they are of different lengths; or the deficient line stops short, meaning it ends, so then the excessive line also ends (since they are aligned), so both are finite, contradicting the assumption of infinity. This argument is a standard one in kalam, used previously for refuting infinite regress and proving the finitude of physical forces.

Key Terms
التطبيق
متناهي
غير متناهي
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the lines are physical lines in space; they are conceptual lines used in a geometric argument.
Correction: The lines are mental constructs to demonstrate a logical point about actual infinities, not physical entities.

Checkpoint
According to the application proof, what happens when the two infinite lines are superimposed?
A) The longer line is found to be finite.
B) The shorter line must be equal to the longer, which is impossible.
C) Both lines become parallel.
D) The lines merge into one line.

Key Takeaway
The application proof shows that assuming an infinite dimension leads to a logical impossibility, thus all dimensions must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your time on earth is finite, but do you live as if it is? The proof shows even a conceptual infinite cannot stand. What finite thing are you treating as infinite, and how will you reprioritize your day to honor its limit?
?
The argument forces you to reject a contradictory assumption. Is there a comfortable belief you hold that, under logical scrutiny, would collapse? Will you have the courage to abandon it today?
?
Everything created is finite—only the Creator is infinite. How does this logical certainty reshape your reliance on created things? What specific step will you take to anchor your hope in the Infinite alone?

The Seventh Proof: Another Application Variant
Iji gives another version of the application proof using an infinite line with two marked points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh variant of the application proof, which uses an infinite line with two marked points to demonstrate the finitude of dimensions.

Core Idea
Even if a line is infinite, a midpoint forces a contradiction through comparison.

This lesson presents another clever application of the superimposition argument. The author, al-Iji, constructs a scenario with an infinite line and two finite points, then uses the concept of a midpoint to derive a contradiction—proving that even such a line cannot be truly infinite. This variant reinforces the core argument and shows the versatility of the application method.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
The author assumes a line infinite on both sides and marks two points with a finite distance between them. This is the starting point for the proof.
The line is infinite in both directions, but the two points are a finite distance apart.
2
Analyze the two cases
The argument considers whether a certain point (one of the two) is the midpoint or not. In both cases, applying the segments leads to a contradiction if the line is infinite.
If it is the midpoint, then the segments on either side are equal, but then the other point yields an unequal segment. If it is not the midpoint, one segment is shorter.
3
See the conclusion
The author notes that this is just another formulation of the application proof, and that the seven aspects reduce to three basic proofs: two proving impossibility of infinitude absolutely, and one proving impossibility in two directions.
This shows the unity behind the multiple arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع: أنا نفرض خطا غير متناه من الجانبين ثم نعين عليه نقطتين بينهما بعد متناه ونشير إلى نقطة ما من هاتين النقطتين فنقول: هي إما المنتصف أولا فإن كانت المنتصف كان منها في الجانب الآخر مثله فيكون من النقطة الأخرى في ذلك الجانب أقل منه فنطبق أحدهما بالآخر ونتم الدليل، وإن لم تكن المنتصف كان أحدهما أقل من الآخر ونمضي في إتمام الدليل ولا يذهب عليك أن هذا تقرير آخر للتطبيق فقد عادت الوجوه السبعة إلى أدلة ثلاثة اثنان منها يدلان على امتناع اللاتناهي مطلقاً وواحدة على امتناعه في جهتين أو أكثر
The seventh aspect: We assume a line infinite on both sides, then we mark on it two points between which there is a finite distance, and we refer to a certain point from these two points. We say: it is either the midpoint or not. If it is the midpoint, then from it on the other side there is something like it, so from the other point on that side there is less than it; then we apply one to the other and complete the proof. If it is not the midpoint, then one of them is less than the other, and we proceed to complete the proof. And it is not hidden from you that this is another formulation of the application. Thus the seven aspects reduce to three proofs: two of them indicate the impossibility of infinitude absolutely, and one indicates its impossibility in two or more directions.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh and final proof, another version of the application argument. The author assumes a line infinite in both directions, marks two points with a finite distance between them, and then considers a point (maybe one of the two) to compare segments. If that point is the exact midpoint, then the segment from it in one direction is equal to the segment from the other point in that direction? Actually the reasoning is: from the midpoint, the line extends equally in both directions; from the other point (which is not the midpoint), the segment on one side is shorter. By applying (superimposing) the segments, we derive a contradiction if the line is truly infinite. If not the midpoint, then one segment is less, and the same application argument holds. The author notes this is just another formulation of the application proof. He concludes by summarizing that the seven aspects reduce to three basic proofs: two that prove the impossibility of an infinite magnitude absolutely (showing that dimensions are finite from all sides), and one that proves impossibility in two or more directions (likely referring to the shield proof which shows finitude in both length and width).

Key Terms
تطبيق
نقطة منتصف
متناهي
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the existence of a definite midpoint on an infinite line.
Correction: The argument is conditional: it works whether the chosen point is the midpoint or not, so it does not assume the midpoint exists; it considers both possibilities.

Checkpoint
To how many basic proofs do the seven aspects reduce?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Seven
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof is another application variant showing that any infinite line must be finite, and all seven aspects reduce to three fundamental proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof demands that you accept a logical conclusion even when it contradicts the intuitive notion of an infinite line. Do you allow your reason to correct your assumptions about the physical world, or do you cling to first impressions?
?
The proof reduces seven aspects to three fundamental arguments. Have you ever examined your own beliefs—especially about God and the universe—to see if they rest on solid foundations, or do you hold them without rigorous scrutiny?
?
The author calls this 'another formulation of the application.' Are you satisfied with understanding the essence of an argument, or do you feel the need to master every variant? How does that reflect on your pursuit of knowledge in matters of faith?

Opponent's First Objection: Distinction Beyond the World
Iji records objection and answer about distinction beyond the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that distinction beyond the world proves its existence, and the decisive reply that this distinction is mere illusion.

Core Idea
What the mind perceives as distinct beyond the world is not real.

Before we can affirm that the universe is finite, opponents argue that what lies beyond it must exist because we can distinguish directions there. This passage presents that argument and the author's crisp reply: our mind's ability to imagine distinctions does not make them real. We must learn to separate conceptual distinctions from actual existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Notice the opponent's claim and the reply.
The argument uses right/left and poles as examples.
2
Understand the opponent's reasoning
The opponent says: beyond the world, right is distinct from left, etc. Since what is distinct cannot be nothing, there must be something (a dimension) there.
This would imply an infinite extension if the world is finite.
3
Grasp the author's reply
The author flatly denies that such distinction exists in reality. The distinction is purely a product of imagination (wahm) with no basis outside the mind.
The key point: mental distinction ≠ real existence.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
This shows how kalām uses philosophical distinctions to defend the finitude of the world. The imagination can create false realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم على عدم التناهي بوجوه: الأول: أن ما وراء العالم متميز فإن ما يلي يمينه أي يمين العالم غير ما يلي يساره ضرورة. ألا ترى أن بديهة العقل شاهدة بأن ما يلي القطب الشمالي غير ما يلي القطب الجنوبي، وما يلي المشرق غير ما يلي المغرب إلى غير ذلك والمتميز لا يكون عدماً محضاً، فهو إذن موجود وبعد لقبوله التقدير سواء كان مادياً أو مجرداً. والجواب منع ثبوت التميز فيما وراء العالم بحسب نفس الأمر وإنما ذلك التميز الذي ذكرتموه وهم محض لا عبرة به أصلا
The opponent argued against non-finitude with aspects: The first: That what is beyond the world is distinct, for what is adjacent to its right—i.e., the right of the world—is other than what is adjacent to its left, necessarily. Do you not see that the intuition of the intellect testifies that what is adjacent to the North Pole is other than what is adjacent to the South Pole, and what is adjacent to the East is other than what is adjacent to the West, and so on. And what is distinct cannot be pure non-existence, so it therefore exists and is a dimension because it admits of estimation, whether material or immaterial. The answer: The denial of the existence of distinction in what is beyond the world in actual fact; rather, that distinction which you mentioned is pure illusion to which no attention is paid at all.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage gives the opponent's first objection and the answer (jawab). The opponent argues that beyond the world, there is distinction: e.g., what is to the right of the world is different from what is to the left, just as north and south are distinct. Since what is distinct cannot be pure nothingness, these directions must be something, and thus there exists an extended dimension (bu'd) beyond the world, either material or immaterial. This would imply an infinite dimension (since beyond the world is unbounded). The answer (from the author) is to deny that such distinction exists in external reality (fi nafs al-amr). The seeming distinction is merely an illusion (wahm) with no real basis. In other words, the human mind imagines directions beyond the world, but there is no actual distinct things there.

Key Terms
تميز
وهم
ما وراء العالم
بعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the answer is a simple denial without justification; it is actually based on the idea that mental distinction does not imply extra-mental reality.
Correction: The author provides a clear reason: the distinction is pure illusion (wahm) with no basis in actual fact.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the opponent's claim that directions beyond the world are distinct?
A) He accepts the distinction but argues it is a mental concept.
B) He denies that there is any distinction in reality, calling it pure illusion.
C) He argues that the world has no right or left.
D) He says the directions are material bodies.

Key Takeaway
Distinction in the mind does not entail existence outside the mind; the opponent's argument is refuted by showing the distinction is mere illusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the vastness of space beyond the known universe, do you catch yourself assigning it imaginary directions and distances as if they were real? In what other areas of your life do you mistake mental constructs for objective truth? What would it take to surrender those illusions to Allah's reality?
?
The opponent relied on 'intuition of the intellect' to claim existence. Which of your own deeply held beliefs about Allah or the unseen might actually be products of your imagination? Will you examine them with the same critical eye this lesson applies, or will you cling to them because they feel necessary?
?
If the distinction beyond the world is mere illusion, how much more might your assumptions about your own worth, your future, or your relationships be illusions? What would change if you treated your thoughts about these things as mere wahm until confirmed by revelation? Are you brave enough to question them today?

Opponent's Second Objection: Estimation of Size Beyond the World
Iji reports the opponent's argument and the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that what is beyond the world is quantifiable, and the reply that this quantification is an imaginary illusion.

Core Idea
The mind can imagine sizes beyond the world, but imagination does not prove existence.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the second objection from those who assert a body beyond the world. They argue that we can conceive of measurable quantities beyond the world, and since whatever is measurable must exist, there must be something there. The reply is sharp: this imagined quantification is a false illusion that deserves no consideration. Let's see how the author dismantles this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the opponent draws an analogy with relative sizes: a region parallel to a quarter of the world is smaller than a region parallel to a half.
2
Identify the logical structure
The opponent claims that since we can conceive of a quantity (more and less), that quantity must exist. This relies on the premise that every quantity is a property of an existent.
3
Examine the reply
The author denies the reality of that quantification, calling it a false illusion (wahm batil). He argues that the mind's estimation does not imply external existence.
4
Reflect on the method
This shows a key kalam principle: mental estimations (wahmiyyat) are not reliable evidence for the existence of things outside the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه أي ما وراء العالم متقدر فإن ما يوازي ربع العالم أقل مما يوازي نصفه وكل متقدر فهو موجود وكم والجواب أن التقدر الذي صورتموه وهم باطل لا يلتفت إليه قطعاً
The second: That what is beyond the world is quantifiable, for what is parallel to a quarter of the world is less than what is parallel to a half of it, and everything quantifiable is existent and a quantity. The answer: That quantification which you have imagined is false illusion, to which no attention is paid at all.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This is the opponent's second objection: beyond the world, we can conceive of quantities—for example, a region the size of a quarter of the world is less than a region the size of a half. Since such quantities are conceivable, they must exist as real quantities (kamm). The reply is similar to the first: this quantification is merely a false illusion (wahm batil) and has no reality. The mind can imagine different sizes, but that does not prove actual existence.

Key Terms
تقدر
كم
وهم
ما وراء العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can conceive of a quantity beyond the world, then such a quantity must exist.
Correction: The author argues that this conceived quantity is merely a false illusion with no external reality.

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's basis for claiming that beyond the world is quantifiable?
A) He uses geometry to measure distances.
B) He compares sizes relative to the world's parts.
C) He argues from the existence of the world itself.
D) He relies on revelation.

Key Takeaway
The mere fact that the mind can imagine sizes beyond the world does not prove their existence; such mental estimations are illusory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you confuse vivid imaginations or strong desires with reality? When in your life have you assumed that because you could picture something in your mind, it must be true or possible? What does this passage teach you about the reliability of your own mental images?
?
The opponent's error was to treat mental quantification as proof of external existence. Where in your own beliefs might you be doing the same? Are there spiritual matters where you rely on your own imaginative assumptions instead of revealed truth or sound reason?
?
The reply dismisses the entire imagined quantification as 'false illusion' to which no attention is paid. Is there a false belief or worry that you keep giving attention to, even though it has no real basis? How can you apply this lesson to stop feeding that illusion?

The Third Objection: Hand at the Edge of the World
Iji presents the opponent's third objection and begins the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that either space or a body must exist beyond the world, and the beginning of the reply that the inability to extend a hand could be due to absence of space rather than a physical barrier.

Core Idea
The absence of space prevents extension just as surely as a physical barrier.

The opponent presents a clever argument using the image of a person standing at the world's edge. If they can extend their hand, there must be space; if not, there must be a barrier. Either way, something exists beyond. The reply (incomplete here) challenges the second horn: maybe the hand cannot extend not because something blocks it, but because there is nothing to extend into.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: two conditional statements followed by a conclusion, then the beginning of a reply.
The reply is cut off; focus on understanding the opponent's argument clearly.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The opponent presents two possibilities: either the hand can extend (so space exists), or it cannot extend (so a barrier exists). Mark where each horn is presented.
The transition is at 'وَإِن لَّمْ يُمْكِنْهُ'.
3
Understand the opponent's reasoning
In the first horn, extension proves space because pure nothing cannot accommodate a hand. In the second, inability proves a body blocking it. The opponent concludes a dimension (immaterial or material) must exist.
The argument relies on the principle that every motion or lack thereof has a cause.
4
Analyze the reply's opening move
The reply denies the inference in the second horn. It says inability could be due to 'absence of the condition'—the space needed for extension—not the presence of a preventer.
This challenges the opponent's dichotomy: the hand cannot extend because there is no space to extend into, not because something blocks it.
5
Note the incomplete nature of the passage
The answer is cut off at 'مد'. The complete reply continues in the next lesson. Reflect on how the author has already planted a critical distinction.
This lesson covers only the initial objection and the beginning of the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنا لو فرضنا واقفاً على طرف العالم فإن أمكنه مد يده فيما وراءه فثمة فضاء موجود لاستحالة مد اليد في العدم الصرف متقدر إذ ما يسع منه إصبعاً أقل مما يسع اليد كلها، وإن لم يمكنه مد يده فيه فثمة جسم مانع لليد من النفوذ وعلى التقديرين فثمة بعد إما مجرد أو مادي. والجواب: لا نسلم أنه لو لم يمكنه مد يده فيه فثمة جسم مانع لجواز أن يكون ذلك لا لوجود المانع بل لعدم الشرط وهو الفضاء الذي يمكن مد
The third: If we assume a person standing at the edge of the world, then if he can extend his hand beyond it, then there is an existing space, because extending a hand into pure nothingness is impossible, and that space is quantifiable since what accommodates a finger from it is less than what accommodates the whole hand. And if he cannot extend his hand into it, then there is a body preventing the hand from penetrating. On both suppositions, there is a dimension, either immaterial or material. The answer: We do not grant that if he cannot extend his hand into it, there is a preventing body, because it is possible that this is not due to the existence of a preventer but due to the absence of a condition, namely the space in which he could extend...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's third objection and the beginning of the answer. The opponent considers a person at the world's edge. If the person can extend his hand outward, then there must be space (void) beyond, which is quantifiable (a finger's capacity vs. hand's capacity). If he cannot extend his hand, then there must be a physical barrier (jism mani') preventing penetration. Either way, there is a dimension (bu'd) beyond the world, either void or material. The answer (incomplete in this window) begins by denying the inference in the second case: it does not necessarily imply a preventing body, because the inability to extend could be due to the absence of a necessary condition, namely the very space that would allow extension. In other words, the hand cannot extend because there is no space to extend into, not because something blocks it. Thus the opponent's dichotomy is flawed. The text cuts off, but the argument would continue on the next page.

Key Terms
فضاء
عدم
جسم مانع
بعد مجرد
بعد مادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inability to extend a hand beyond the world must be caused by a physical barrier.
Correction: It could be caused by the absence of space itself, not a barrier.

Checkpoint
What alternative explanation does the author give for why a hand cannot be extended beyond the world?
A) There is a divine decree preventing it.
B) The hand is tied.
C) The absence of space prevents extension, not a physical barrier.
D) The world is surrounded by fire.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's dichotomy forces either space or a barrier, but the reply shows the second case does not necessitate a barrier—absence of space suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you tend to assume that every limitation is caused by an external obstacle, when in fact it might be the absence of a necessary condition within yourself? What is one 'space' you lack that prevents you from extending yourself spiritually or morally?
?
The opponent assumed that if the hand cannot go outward, there must be a physical barrier. Are there beliefs or habits you hold that you have never questioned, assuming they are necessary truths? What would it take to consider the possibility of an 'absence of condition' rather than an active blocker?
?
This passage challenges the assumption that every inability is due to a positive preventer. When you find yourself unable to draw closer to Allah, do you blame external circumstances (shaytan, society) rather than the absence of the necessary inner condition (sincere desire, tawakkul, ihsan)? How would you act differently if you took full responsibility for that absence?

Body as a Universal: Objection and Refutation
Objection and answer about body as universal and infinite individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that body as a universal implies infinite individuals is refuted by showing that universality does not necessitate existence, multiplicity, or infinity.

Core Idea
Universality does not automatically mean infinite individuals actually exist.

This passage presents a central objection against the finitude of bodies: if body is a universal notion, it could potentially have infinite individuals, leading to infinite dimensions. The author answers by clarifying that universality alone does not force existence, multiplicity, or infinity—these depend on external factors. The actual impossibility of infinite bodily individuals is already established by earlier proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening statement carefully: 'The body is a universal notion, so potentially it can have infinite individuals mentally. If those individuals existed, then dimensions would be infinite.' Understand the challenge.
The objection relies on the idea that universality allows for unlimited particulars.
2
Understand the answer's core
The answer states that universality does not necessitate existence, multiplicity, or infinity. Focus on the word 'necessitate'—universality only indicates possibility, not actuality.
The author lists three things universality does not force: existence, multiplicity, and infinity.
3
Explore the three possibilities
The answer explains three scenarios: the universal could be impossible to exist (so no individuals), impossible to multiply (so only one), or impossible to be infinite in individuals (so finite). Consider each as a separate way the objection fails.
These are external reasons, not from the concept of universality itself.
4
Connect to earlier proofs
The passage concludes by referencing 'previous proofs' that establish the impossibility of infinite individuals of body. Understand that the objection is already undermined by independent arguments.
This shows the answer is not just theoretical but grounded in established theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: الجسم ماهية كلية فيمكن لها أفراد غير متناهية عقلاً) فإذا وجدت تلك الأفراد كانت الأبعاد غير متناهية (والجواب أن الكلية ) وإن لم تمنع من وقوع جزئيات لا تتناهى إلا أنها ) لا تقتضي الوجود أي وجود شيء من الجزئيات ( ولا التعدد في الجزئيات ) ولا عدم التناهي فيها بل يجوز أن يكون الكلي ممتنع الوجود فلا يوجد شيء من أفراده أو ممتنع التعدد فلا تتعدد أفراده أو ممتنع اللاتناهي في أفراده فلا يوجد له أفراد غير متناهية كل ذلك لأمور خارجة عن مفهوم الكلية وعدم تناهي أفراد الجسم ممتنع للأدلة السابقة .
Fourth: The body is a universal notion, so potentially it can have infinite individuals mentally. If those individuals existed, then dimensions would be infinite. The answer: Universality, although it does not prevent the occurrence of particulars that are infinite, yet it does not necessitate existence-that is, the existence of any particular-nor multiplicity in particulars, nor infinity in them. Rather, it is possible that the universal is impossible to exist, so none of its individuals exist; or impossible to multiply, so its individuals do not multiply; or impossible to be infinite in its individuals, so it has no infinite individuals. All that is due to matters external to the concept of universality. The infinitude of individuals of body is impossible due to previous proofs.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection claims that since body is a universal notion, it can have potentially infinite individuals, and that if those individuals existed, the dimensions would be infinite. The answer rejects this by explaining that universality does not entail actual existence, multiplicity, or infinity. It is possible that the universal itself is impossible to exist, or that its individuals are finite for external reasons. The answer concludes that the infinitude of bodily individuals is already impossible due to earlier proofs, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الجسم
الماهية الكلية
التناهي
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is a universal, it must have infinitely many actual individuals.
Correction: Universality only indicates possibility; actual existence, multiplicity, and infinity depend on external factors (e.g., whether the universal is possible to exist, multiply, or be infinite).

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the relationship between universality and the existence of infinite individuals?
A) Universality necessarily requires existence of infinite individuals.
B) Universality prevents the occurrence of infinite individuals.
C) Universality alone does not necessitate existence, multiplicity, or infinity of individuals.
D) Universality guarantees that individuals are finite.

Key Takeaway
The objection that body's universality leads to infinite individuals fails because universality alone does not necessitate existence, multiplicity, or infinity; the impossibility of infinite bodies is independently proven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any area of your belief where you assume that because something is conceivable in the mind (like a universal), it must be possible in reality? How has this lesson exposed that assumption as faulty? What concrete step will you take to guard against this error in your thinking about Allah's attributes?
?
The objection tried to force a conclusion based on a mere mental possibility. When have you, in your own life, let a mere possibility lead you to a false certainty or despair? Will you now commit to distinguishing between what is logically possible and what is actually real, grounded in revelation and proof?
?
The answer points out that external proofs (like the impossibility of infinite bodies) override the mere potential of a universal. Are you willing to let the definitive proofs of the Qur'an and Sunnah override your own rational inclinations or cultural assumptions? If not, what does that reveal about your submission to the truth?

First Objection: Celestial Motions as Natural
First objection: natural motion, with al-Jurjani's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that celestial motions might be natural and how al-Jurjani refutes it by redefining motion as desired for itself.

Core Idea
Even natural motion can be desired for itself if motion is existence in two places.

In this lesson, we encounter the first objection to the proof that celestial motions are voluntary. The objector suggests that these motions could be natural after all, if the desired end is the motion itself rather than a position. Al-Jurjani counters by clarifying the definition of motion and showing that it can be desired for itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objector's claim and al-Jurjani's response.
The objector uses the idea of 'natural desire' (al-maṭlūb bi-l-ṭabʿ).
2
Identify key definitions
Understand what 'natural motion' and 'desired for itself' mean in this context. Al-Jurjani defines motion as existence in two places at two moments.
3
Analyze the refutation
See how al-Jurjani turns the objection: even if motion were natural, it could be desired for itself under his definition.
This blocks the objector's conclusion that celestial motions are not voluntary.
4
Note the concession
The passage ends with 'سلمناه' (we grant that), showing the objector agrees celestial motions are not natural, moving to the next objection.
This sets up the second objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض) على هذا الدليل أن يقال : ( لا نسلم أنها ليست طبيعية وأنه يلزم ) من ذلك (كون المطلوب بالطبع مهروباً عنه بالطبع لجواز أن يكون المطلوب) في الحركة الطبيعة ( نفس الحركة ) لا حصول وضع معين فإن قيل : حقيقة الحركة هي التأدي إلي شيء آخر فلا تطلب لذاتها بل لغيرها قلنا الحركة عندنا عبارة عن كون الجوهر في آنين في مكانين فجاز كونها مطلوبة لذاتها ( سلمناه ) أي سلمنا أن الحركات الفلكية ليست طبيعية ( لكن لا نسلم أنها ليست قسرية قولكم القسر على خلاف الطبع) أي ما ليس فيه ميل طبيعي لا يقبل حركة قسرية ( ممنوع وقد مر ما في دليله من الخلل على أنه ليس يلزم من عدم كون حركاتها المستديرة طبيعية أن لا يكون لها ميل طبيعي مخالف لهذه الحركة ولا نسلم أيضاً أن القاسر هناك منحصر في الأفلاك حتى يلزم التشابه بل نقول : الحركة الحاصلة من بعضها في بعض تكون حركة عرضية لا قسرية ( سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أن التخيل لا ينتظم على حالة واحدة ولا يدوم سرمداً ( ولم لا يجوز أن يكون تخيله) أي تخيل الفلك (خلاف تخيلنا؟) فلا يختلف ولا ينقطع بل يستمر أزلاً وأبداً بتعاقب أفراد غير متناهية متعلقة بحركات متوافقة متماثلة. فإن قيل : القوى الجسمانية كما مر متناهية مدة وعدة وشدة فلا تستند إليها الحركات التي لا تتناهى . قلنا : قد مر أيضاً ما فيه ولو صح ذلك تعذر عليكم إثبات النفوس المنطبعة في الأجسام الفلكية ( سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أن محل العقل مجرد و ما سيأتي من برهانه ( سنتكلم عليه )
And an objection to this proof is to say: We do not grant that they are not natural, and that it follows from that that the naturally desired is naturally fled from, because it is possible that what is desired in natural motion is the motion itself, not attaining a particular position. If it is said: The reality of motion is passing to something else, so it is not desired for itself but for another, we say: Motion, in our view, is the existence of a substance in two moments in two places, so it is possible that it is desired for itself. We grant that—i.e., we grant that celestial motions are not natural. But we do not grant that they are not forced. Your statement that force is contrary to nature—i.e., that which has no natural inclination cannot receive forced motion—is denied, and what is in its proof of defect has already passed; moreover, the lack of natural circular motion does not imply they have no natural inclination contrary to this motion. And we also do not grant that the coercer there is restricted to the celestial spheres such that similarity would be necessary; rather, we say: The motion produced from some of them upon others is an accidental motion, not forced. We grant that, but we do not grant that imagination cannot remain in one state and continue eternally. Why may it not be that its imagination—i.e., the imagination of the sphere—is different from our imagination, so it does not differ or cease, but continues forever and ever through the succession of infinite individuals pertaining to similar, uniform motions? If it is said: Corporeal powers, as has passed, are finite in duration, number, and intensity, so motions that are infinite cannot be based upon them. We say: What is in that [proof] has also passed, and if that were correct, it would be impossible for you to prove the impressed souls in celestial bodies. We grant that, but we do not grant that the locus of intellect is immaterial, and what will come of its proof—we will discuss it there.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage records three objections to the proof that celestial motions are voluntary, with answers. First objection: Celestial motions might be natural if the object of natural desire is the motion itself rather than a position. Answer: Motion is defined as existence in two moments at two places, so it can be desired for itself. Second objection: Even if not natural, they could be forced; the lack of natural inclination does not preclude force, and the coercer need not be among the spheres. Answer: The premise about force and nature is denied, and accidental motion does not count as force. Third objection: Imagination (takhayyul) could be eternal and uniform if the celestial imagination differs from human imagination. The response: Corporeal powers are finite, so they cannot produce infinite motions. If this fails, the opponent would also fail to prove impressed souls. Finally, the interlocutor grants all but still doubts that the locus of intellect is immaterial, which will be addressed later.

Key Terms
اعتراض (objection)
حركة طبيعية (natural motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that natural motion cannot be desired for itself; it must be for an end.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that motion defined as existence in two places can be desired for itself, even if it is natural.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what is the definition of motion?
A) The passing from one place to another toward a goal.
B) The existence of a substance in two moments in two places.
C) The change of position over time.
D) The actualization of a potential.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani refutes the first objection by redefining motion so that it can be desired for itself, thereby preserving the voluntary nature of celestial motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed something about your own nature was 'fixed' or 'natural' when it was actually a choice you were avoiding? What would it mean for you to redefine that aspect as something you can desire for itself?
?
Al-Jurjani changed the definition of motion to preserve a deeper truth. Is there a belief you hold that forces you to reconsider familiar concepts? What would it cost you to hold onto that truth?
?
If even the motion of the heavens is driven by a self-desired intention, what does that say about your own small movements today? Are you moving through your day as a string of accidents, or as a series of intended actions you desire for themselves?

Second Objection: Celestial Motions as Forced
Second objection: forced motion, with al-Jurjani's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that celestial motions might be forced and how al-Jurjani refutes it by denying that force requires a natural inclination and by offering accidental motion as an alternative.

Core Idea
Force does not require a natural inclination; accidental motion between spheres is not forced.

The second objection argues that if celestial motions are not natural, they must be forced. Al-Jurjani rejects this, explaining that force is not simply the absence of natural inclination, and that the motion of one sphere upon another is accidental, not forced. He also notes that the coercer need not be among the spheres, avoiding the problem of similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the objector's claim that motions must be forced if not natural.
The objector's premise: 'force is contrary to nature' (القسر على خلاف الطبع).
2
Analyze al-Jurjani's denial
He denies the premise that what has no natural inclination cannot receive forced motion. Then he provides an alternative: accidental motion.
Accidental motion (حركة عرضية) is not forced because it arises from the interaction of spheres.
3
Understand the consequence
If the coercer were limited to the spheres, similarity would be necessary, but al-Jurjani says it is not.
4
Note the concession
The objector grants that celestial motions are not forced, moving to the third objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض) على هذا الدليل أن يقال : ( لا نسلم أنها ليست طبيعية وأنه يلزم ) من ذلك (كون المطلوب بالطبع مهروباً عنه بالطبع لجواز أن يكون المطلوب) في الحركة الطبيعة ( نفس الحركة ) لا حصول وضع معين فإن قيل : حقيقة الحركة هي التأدي إلي شيء آخر فلا تطلب لذاتها بل لغيرها قلنا الحركة عندنا عبارة عن كون الجوهر في آنين في مكانين فجاز كونها مطلوبة لذاتها ( سلمناه ) أي سلمنا أن الحركات الفلكية ليست طبيعية ( لكن لا نسلم أنها ليست قسرية قولكم القسر على خلاف الطبع) أي ما ليس فيه ميل طبيعي لا يقبل حركة قسرية ( ممنوع وقد مر ما في دليله من الخلل على أنه ليس يلزم من عدم كون حركاتها المستديرة طبيعية أن لا يكون لها ميل طبيعي مخالف لهذه الحركة ولا نسلم أيضاً أن القاسر هناك منحصر في الأفلاك حتى يلزم التشابه بل نقول : الحركة الحاصلة من بعضها في بعض تكون حركة عرضية لا قسرية ( سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أن التخيل لا ينتظم على حالة واحدة ولا يدوم سرمداً ( ولم لا يجوز أن يكون تخيله) أي تخيل الفلك (خلاف تخيلنا؟) فلا يختلف ولا ينقطع بل يستمر أزلاً وأبداً بتعاقب أفراد غير متناهية متعلقة بحركات متوافقة متماثلة. فإن قيل : القوى الجسمانية كما مر متناهية مدة وعدة وشدة فلا تستند إليها الحركات التي لا تتناهى . قلنا : قد مر أيضاً ما فيه ولو صح ذلك تعذر عليكم إثبات النفوس المنطبعة في الأجسام الفلكية ( سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أن محل العقل مجرد و ما سيأتي من برهانه ( سنتكلم عليه )
And an objection to this proof is to say: We do not grant that they are not natural, and that it follows from that that the naturally desired is naturally fled from, because it is possible that what is desired in natural motion is the motion itself, not attaining a particular position. If it is said: The reality of motion is passing to something else, so it is not desired for itself but for another, we say: Motion, in our view, is the existence of a substance in two moments in two places, so it is possible that it is desired for itself. We grant that—i.e., we grant that celestial motions are not natural. But we do not grant that they are not forced. Your statement that force is contrary to nature—i.e., that which has no natural inclination cannot receive forced motion—is denied, and what is in its proof of defect has already passed; moreover, the lack of natural circular motion does not imply they have no natural inclination contrary to this motion. And we also do not grant that the coercer there is restricted to the celestial spheres such that similarity would be necessary; rather, we say: The motion produced from some of them upon others is an accidental motion, not forced. We grant that, but we do not grant that imagination cannot remain in one state and continue eternally. Why may it not be that its imagination—i.e., the imagination of the sphere—is different from our imagination, so it does not differ or cease, but continues forever and ever through the succession of infinite individuals pertaining to similar, uniform motions? If it is said: Corporeal powers, as has passed, are finite in duration, number, and intensity, so motions that are infinite cannot be based upon them. We say: What is in that [proof] has also passed, and if that were correct, it would be impossible for you to prove the impressed souls in celestial bodies. We grant that, but we do not grant that the locus of intellect is immaterial, and what will come of its proof—we will discuss it there.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage records three objections to the proof that celestial motions are voluntary, with answers. First objection: Celestial motions might be natural if the object of natural desire is the motion itself rather than a position. Answer: Motion is defined as existence in two moments at two places, so it can be desired for itself. Second objection: Even if not natural, they could be forced; the lack of natural inclination does not preclude force, and the coercer need not be among the spheres. Answer: The premise about force and nature is denied, and accidental motion does not count as force. Third objection: Imagination (takhayyul) could be eternal and uniform if the celestial imagination differs from human imagination. The response: Corporeal powers are finite, so they cannot produce infinite motions. If this fails, the opponent would also fail to prove impressed souls. Finally, the interlocutor grants all but still doubts that the locus of intellect is immaterial, which will be addressed later.

Key Terms
قسر (force)
حركة عرضية (accidental motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any motion not natural must be forced.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that accidental motion is a third category that is neither natural nor forced.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jurjani call the motion produced from one celestial sphere upon another?
A) Natural motion
B) Forced motion
C) Accidental motion
D) Voluntary motion

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani refutes the second objection by denying that force requires a natural inclination and by introducing accidental motion as an alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face events that seem random or unexplainable, do you automatically assume they are caused by external forces (coercion) or that they are meaningless? How can the concept of accidental motion remind you that not everything that lacks a clear natural cause is forced, but that God's wisdom may still order it without your understanding? Will you pause before attributing things to mere coincidence or fate, and instead seek to see the divine order behind what appears accidental?
?
Al-Jurjani rejects the assumption that if celestial motions are not natural, they must be forced. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes assume that if your faith does not come easily (natural), it is insincere or forced? What would it mean to view your faith as 'accidental' in the sense of being a gift that is not naturally yours nor coerced, but granted by God's mercy? Are you willing to accept that your acts of worship, even when they feel unnatural, can still be accepted as sincere accidental movements toward Him?
?
This lesson teaches that a coercer need not be assumed simply because motion is not natural. How often do you feel pressured to conform to religious practices because of external expectations (people, culture) rather than internal conviction? In what areas of your life have you mistaken 'accidental motion' (actions done without natural inclination but also not forced) as being forced? Will you examine your intentions and surrender even the motions that seem accidental to God, asking Him to make them sincere?
